HEADLINE: Playing by Our Rules

BYLINE: By Linda Robinson; Linda Robinson is the Latin America correspondent for U.S. News and World Report.

 BODY:

IN THE NAME OF DEMOCRACY
U.S. Policy Toward Latin America
in the Reagan Years.
By Thomas Carothers.
309 pp. Berkeley:
University of California Press.
$29.95. 
 DID the Reagan Administration help promote democracy in Central America, or did it cynically use democracy as a rationale to wangle aid from Congress to fight wars and then claim credit for the democratic change that swept through the region in the 1980's? Most Americans who followed that decade's policy battles reached their own conclusions years ago, and their answers, forged in the heat of controversy, were usually reflexive and categorical.
Finally, with "In the Name of Democracy," someone has painstakingly reviewed the record, not only of United States policy on Central America but also on South America, which rarely appeared on newspapers' front pages. Thomas Carothers, recently an international affairs fellow with the Council on Foreign Relations and now an attorney at the Washington firm of Arnold & Porter, was in those bitter days a State Department lawyer who helped implement some of Ronald Reagan's programs aimed at promoting democracy -- specifically, the effort to produce functioning justice systems in Central America. A typical toiler in the bureaucratic trenches, he never embraced the Reagan ideology, but tasted enough action to know the flavor of the times. For two years after he retreated to the relative safety and civility of think tanks, Mr. Carothers interviewed officials of all ranks, and then sat back to muse over whether the Reagan policies actually helped the cause of democracy in Latin America.
Mr. Carothers's answer is "a qualified no," and he argues this most convincingly in the cases of Nicaragua and El Salvador. The Reagan Administration, he says, was far more interested in fending off Communist threats than in building what most Americans would recognize as genuine democracy. But he also shows how, in El Salvador as elsewhere (Chile is the most notable South American example), Washington insisted that elected civilian government was the sine qua non for its assistance. In the case of Chile and Bolivia, strong advocacy of democratization was largely the work of activist United States ambassadors left alone by Reaganauts inside the Beltway.
While Mr. Carothers sees most of the Reagan Administration's efforts to promote democracy as mere rhetoric, he gives some credit to the United States for assisting transitions that occurred in the above countries and in Honduras, Paraguay and Haiti. Nicaragua's democratic elections of 1990 took place despite unremitting hostility from the Reagan Administration, but Mr. Carothers does not acknowledge that the Marxist Sandinista Government there accepted fair, closely observed elections as a way to get rid of the United States-supported contra resistance -- and that the Sandinistas were defeated because Nicaraguans were fed up with them.
Mr. Carothers is absolutely right that, even where the United States Government sincerely pursued democratization, it employed a narrow, formalistic definition, declaring any government that held free elections to be a democracy, even if civil liberties and functioning institutions were lacking. But taking the author's own definition of building democracy as an evolutionary project, the markers set out by the Reagan Administration were necessary first steps, if only partial ones, to achieving real democracy.
What is most valuable about this comprehensive study is its recognition that promoting democracy has now become perhaps the most important organizing principle for United States foreign policy in the post-cold war era. Mr. Carothers notes how rhetoric that was cynically employed in the early 1980's took on a life of its own; and he even credits the second Reagan term with realizing that democratization could solve many problems previously seen as purely security or geopolitical threats. "Although the Reagan Administration embraced the promoting democracy theme for its rhetorical value," he writes, "there is no question that over the course of the Reagan years the theme gathered some real substance and that an administration initially unsympathetic to what it saw as moralistic crusades abroad became sincerely interested in being a force for democracy in Latin America."
The ways in which the Reagan Administration supported democracy were flawed, but the flaws mostly occurred when sincere support for democracy was lacking. That does not discredit the goal itself, and now that the United States is faced with a growing global community of nascent democracies, it has every incentive to make the maintenance and furtherance of democracy its guiding principle. The Bush Administration has taken some steps to capitalize on the utility of democratic allies. In Nicaragua, for example, elections proved the route to ending war, and in most of Central America antagonists have now agreed to play out their differences according to democratic ground rules. Enormous economic and social problems still plague Latin America and other regions, but so long as troubled countries adhere to those democratic rules, they have some chance to pursue change within a framework of stability.
The merit of "In the Name of Democracy" is not so much that it provides a scorecard of the Reagan era, but rather that it looks at just how the United States can concretely support nations struggling to learn democratic ways. Unfortunately, Mr. Carothers undermines his own critique of the Reagan era, and its potential application to current times, by concluding that in fact the United States can do very little to support what he views as necessarily indigenous processes of change and reform.
The lessons he draws are pessimistic: that Latin America must make itself democratic and that the United States has a limited understanding of the region's problems. This is puzzling, since the author's own examples actually suggest that internationally monitored fair elections have set countries on the path to peace, and that with a greater commitment to reform the United States can push countries farther down that path. Of course we cannot create democrats out of whole cloth, but where they exist, United States backing has been important. Ask any Latin American democrat struggling against the weight of authoritarian traditions, and he will tell you he far prefers to have Washington on his side.

HEADLINE: The Executive Life;
Boffo? No. But Jobless 'Comics' Get a Laugh

BYLINE: By Nancy Marx Better

 BODY:
AS the recession drags on, entrepreneurs across America are trying their best to make a buck off it. One of them is 28-year-old Carylin Berger, manager of Stand-Up New York, a comedy club on Manhattan's Upper West Side.
In November, Ms. Berger created a competition for the title of "Funniest Unemployed Person in New York." The first qualifying round attracted a modest audience and earned a couple of reviews in local newspapers. The second qualifying round -- held the week before Christmas -- drew camera crews from three national television shows and a standing-room-only crowd. 
 "The best comic material relates closely to your own experience," said Ms. Berger. "Clearly, lots of people can relate to the issue of unemployment. That's why these evenings are a hit."
It's hardly due to the quality of the comedy. Of the 10 contestants who appeared in December, only two or three could legitimately be called funny. Several were passably humorous, while a few were downright depressing. Most, however, received thunderous applause; their friends and former colleagues filled the place to capacity.
After an emcee warmed up the house, the first would-be Jay Leno took the microphone at 9 P.M. Richard Eastman, an out-of-work book editor, smoothed his straight blond hair anxiously as he strode onstage. His face seemed ashen against the white-painted brick wall. "Unemployment is becoming a lot more popular these days," he began. "I read in the paper every morning that more and more people are getting into it."
He paused, then continued. "I finally hit the last resort of the jobless. Last week I went up to New Hampshire and registered as a candidate for the Democratic primary."
Mr. Eastman took a bow and sauntered offstage, grinning broadly. "When I worked in the book industry, I always tried to get my hands on humorous material," he said afterward. "I thought this would be a great chance to try what I've learned."
"Funniest Unemployed" grew out of a series of occupational contests staged by Ms. Berger, including "Funniest Accountant," "Funniest Stockbroker" and "Funniest Advertising Person." Even the professional comedians who perform at Stand-Up New York often take business as their theme. One program -- "What's So Funny About Donald Trump?" -- was devoted entirely to jokes about the real-estate developer.
The third contestant, a former reporter for out-of-state newspapers, adopted the stage name Norman K. for the occasion and donned blue jeans and a tweed sportcoat. "I may be unemployed, but I still have a schedule," he announced. "Mornings I reserve for game shows. Afternoons I reserve for talk shows."
Referring to a recent college reunion, Norman K. explained how he fielded queries about his professional life. "I told everyone I'm compiling a daily anthropological archive of American culture," he said. "That means I'm taping 'Geraldo.' "
Following Norman K., the talent went downhill. A former real estate broker's jokes about Robert Maxwell merited a couple of polite chuckles. An out-of-work television writer compared the recession to the Persian Gulf War. An unemployed art director lampooned the term "self-starter." ("If I get up before noon and make coffee and feed the cat, am I a self-starter?")
But the pain was short-lived: Ms. Berger gives each contestant exactly three minutes to run through his or her routine, and requires that material be recession-related. The five-member panel of judges grades performers on three criteria: delivery, originality and humor.
At 10 P.M., a young woman with short brown hair and a black leather motorcycle jacket took the microphone. "I'm Lisa Balch, I'm from Arkansas, and I've been unemployed for seven months now," she said, in a deep Southern accent.
Ms. Balch, a former sales manager for a Manhattan retail company, scored the evening's biggest laughs with a month-by-month account of her unemployment.
"The first month I slept late, watched the soaps and gloated when my friends complained about their jobs," she said. "The second month I did personal projects, like cleaning my closets. The third month I started getting bitter when my roomates came home and didn't notice that I'd showered."
By the fifth month, Ms. Balch said, she was taking phone messages for her roommates in calligraphy, or, time permitting, in needlepoint. By the seventh month, her imagination was running wild. "I pretended to an American Express representative that I was a bereaved relative of mine and that I'd been killed by the Mafia," she said.
When the votes were tallied, Ms. Balch was declared the winner. Norman K. was the first runner-up. Together, they posed for the television crews, waving and winking at their friends.
Yet the prizes aren't exactly the stuff of "Wheel of Fortune." The winner of each qualifying round (a third will be held in January, and then the finalists will face off in February) gets a Stand-Up New York T-shirt. The grand-prize-winner receives a free dinner at a Manhattan restaurant and a consultation with an outplacement expert on finding a job. In addition, he or she gets a set at Stand-Up New York -- a chance to perform alongside headliner comedians, for pay.
But unemployed professionals might not want to spend too much time practicing one-liners. Telling jokes is a "good release of tension," said Dee Soder, president of Endymion Company, a New York executive counseling firm. However, she points out, "Focusing on comedy may distract people from pursuing their career options."

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 2; National Desk

LENGTH: 3165 words

HEADLINE: Balancing Act; A special report.;
One City's 30-Year Crusade for Integration

BYLINE: By ISABEL WILKERSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SHAKER HEIGHTS, Ohio

 BODY:
For 30 years now Shaker Heights, a suburban forest of Old World estates on the eastern border of Cleveland, has made racial integration something of a crusade. It has tried to hold the races together even as they appear alienated outside its borders.
The city has found that integration is not only difficult to begin but also difficult to maintain. If it is to occur at all, residents here say, it requires prodding and pampering and a kind of eternal vigilance and preoccupation with race. The city goes so far as to provide financial incentives to those who move into neighborhoods dominated by the other race. 
 In all the rituals and routines of daily life, it seems, Shaker Heights puts race front and center. The idea is that if left to their own devices, people will resegregate. So the city does not leave them to their own devices. Instead it treats integration like a patient on life support: No one dares pull the plug for fear the patient won't survive.
And so while most Americans bristle when the topic turns to racial tension, here people look you in the eye and relish the chance to talk about it. What has become a delicate and divisive subject most every place else is as ordinary as mowing the grass here.
It is in the public schools, which are considered among the most rigorous in the country, that Shaker Heights's relentless race consciousness is perhaps most on display, as educators try to work on the minds of the young.
Sixth graders are required to take racial sensitivity training, where they learn about such things as racial polarization and the dynamics of prejudice. An elective course on slavery and the Holocaust, called Oppression, is one of the best attended classes at the high school.
Race and questions of fairness and balance figure into the smallest decisions, from recruitment to the field hockey team to how many blacks are pictured in a yearbook layout.
There is open fretting about why there are not more blacks on the soccer team or more whites running track. There is great pride in the racial symmetry of the chess club and the a capella choir. And among city officials, there is careful attention to the racial turnout at public events like block parties.
 
'We Count. We're Aware.'
 "When someone comes back from a block party, we want to know how many people were there, how many blacks and how many whites?" said Donald L. DeMarco, the city's director of community services. "We count. We're aware. We don't pretend not to notice."
Still, for all this effort, there are still black sections and white sections of the city. Only three of the city's nine neighborhoods are truly racially mixed, with a black population close to the 30 percent level that characterizes Shaker Heights in general. Three are at least 90 percent white and are so expensive that they are likely to remain so for some time. Three are 50 percent or more black, and the city is willing to pay whites to move there.
And when forced by geography or circumstance, students tend to segregate themselves at lunchtime, adults tend to go their separate ways in their social lives, and the occasional racial epithet is shouted across a playground.
But with racial tensions erupting in places as disparate as Brooklyn and Dubuque, Iowa, and the growing use of appeals to racial fears in mainstream politics, determinedly integrated cities like Shaker Heights, Oak Park, Ill., and a half-dozen others stand as pockets that, while far from racial utopias, seem well ahead of the rest of the country.
"They are islands in a sea of racism," said Thomas F. Pettigrew, a professor of social psychology at both the University of California, Santa Cruz, and the University of Amsterdam, who is an expert on integration. "That they work at all is what is amazing about these cities. It is much harder to pull it off when the rest of the country is going in the opposite direction."
 
Balancing Housing
City Staves Off The Usual Flight
 Years ago a white Chicago alderman, fearing the arrival of blacks in his neighborhood, defined integration as the time between when the first black person moves in and the last white person moves out. Under that definition, many American communities have suffered a brief period of "integration," bitter for the new arrivals and the old-timers alike.
Shaker Heights has followed a different course. The city, once a thriving settlement of the religious sect it is named for, is an affluent bedroom community that, with its lakes, its own country club, is Cleveland's version of Scarsdale, N.Y. Its collection of homes ranges from artful two-flats disguised as single-family Tudors to pampered estates of lawyers, bankers and corporate executives. The community of 30,831 had a median family income of $55,747 in 1987, according to the city's Department of Community Services.
The first blacks, mostly doctors and dentists and their families, began moving into town in the late 1950's after restrictive housing covenants were declared illegal by the Supreme Court. Shaker Heights was a natural landing place for black Cleveland residents looking for their dream homes. It borders the Cleveland neighborhoods where most of the city's blacks are concentrated, is only a train stop or two from the old neighborhood, has snob appeal and a school system with a great reputation.
Out of a sense of enlightened self-interest, whites in Shaker Heights decided to make the best of it. They tried to figure out a way to prevent a white exodus, maintain property values and a create a stable, multiracial community. They tried to anticipate white racial fears and defuse them before a stampede could begin. One of the first steps was prohibiting, in 1964, the display of for-sale signs on front lawns to stave off panic selling.
 
No Doomsday
 The doomsday predictions of declining schools and property values have not materialized. Increases in property values have outpaced those in the rest of Cuyahoga County and school test scores exceed national averages, just as they did before the first black family moved in.
Shaker Heights manages its racial housing patterns the way companies manage inventory. It tries to stimulate demand where demand is weakest and discourage demand for what is scarce. Because integration is historically a harder sell to whites than to blacks and typically leads to white flight, integration management essentially means keeping white families in Shaker Heights.
"Unfortunately, you can't leave it to market forces," said Stephen J. Alfred, the Mayor of Shaker Heights and an early leader in the integration plan. "Whites can move anywhere they can afford. The number of whites who want to move into a mostly black neighborhood is small. You have to go out and find them. Meanwhile you've got co-workers and grandparents saying, 'Don't do it.' "
 
A System of Rewards
 To get people to swim upstream, the city rewards blacks and whites who move where the city wants them. It is a much-debated form of social engineering that for five years has given low-cost loans to members of either group if they move into neighborhoods where they are underrepresented.
Whites get help with their mortgages if they move into a neighborhood that is 50 percent or more black. Blacks get help if they move into a neighborhood that is 90 percent or more white. The loans are typically $3,000 to $6,000, based on how segregated the neighborhood is, and are paid in a lump sum toward the downpayment or in monthly installments toward the house note. The city grants about 25 loans a year from a $390,000 fund financed by private donations.
Economics limits the program's reach. The white neighborhoods are the leafy mansion districts surrounding the country club, where few blacks can afford to move anyway. The neighborhoods where whites are encouraged to move have the city's most affordable housing, the smaller Tudors and Georgians. So 90 percent of the loans go to whites.
But because many blacks want to move to Shaker Heights, the city tries to direct them elsewhere when it can. So, along with several adjacent suburbs that have significant black populations, it funnels similar loans to blacks if they agree, and are financially able, to move to a virtually all-white suburb. The designated white suburbs, known collectively as the Hillcrest area, neither contribute to the loan program nor advocate it.
"In terms of creativity and ingenuity, it represents a sharp break from past thinking," said Marc Zegans, research director for Innovations in State and Local Government, a joint program of the Kennedy School of Government and the Ford Foundation, which cited Shaker Heights in 1988 for its integration initiative.
"The fact that you put people of different races and ethnic groups together doesn't mean that everyone is going to be comfortable," Mr. Zegans said. "Integration needs to be managed until we have a society that accepts it as the norm."
 
Criticism for Payments
 The idea of rewarding whites to move in and blacks to move somewhere else is the cost of maintaining integration, say city officials and residents, black and white.
But some black residents see it as degrading approach that only reinforces white fear of integration. "When a city will pay to get blacks to move to another community, it sends a powerful message," said Sheila Kelly, a black real estate agent and a longtime Shaker Heights resident. "They will do anything to get white people here. The city is bribing them to live next to blacks."
But the program can actually raise suspicions among potential white homebuyers. Last February, John and Sally Ziemak found the perfect three-bedroom colonial in the Lomond district, a middle-class section that is evenly split between blacks and whites.
Then they heard that the city would give them a loan just for moving there. "We thought, 'Did things really get that bad?' " Mr. Ziemak, a management consultant, said. "Do they need to save the neighborhood?' Growing up in an all-white neighborhood, you have some second thoughts about it. You only know what you see on T.V."
They bought the house anyway -- the neighborhood was charming and safe, they loved the house and the deal was a good one, $100 a month toward their house note for three years. They moved in last May. And even though the Ziemaks get along fine with their black neighbors, the couple said integration was secondary to getting a good education for their two pre-school-age boys.
"The decision was more with our brain than with our gut," Mr. Ziemak said.
 
Apart by Geography
 There are geographic divisions here that feed social separation. Most blacks live south of the primary dividing line, Van Aken Boulevard. Used-car dealerships and empty storefronts dominate the business district in the almost all-black section of Moreland, while boutiques and antique shops are clustered on the mostly white north side.
The city hierarchy is itself predominantly white. There has never been a black mayor, and currently the superintendant of schools, the vast majority of teachers and all but one of the seven City Council members are white. And only one black leads a city agency.
"It's working, but compared to what?" asked Dr. Winston Richie, a retired dentist, who was one of the first blacks to move to Shaker Heights and who was the first black elected to the City Council. "We're three-fourths of the way there. None of us know how to do this stuff. We haven't done it enough. Only if some of us put ourselves in awkward situations until we feel comfortable will any of this change."
 
Emphasizing Schools
By the Numbers, Classes Are Mixed
 It is in the middle of this moral hand-wringing that Shaker Heights is watching a second generation of children raised on a diet of racial diversity.
The first generation, the children of people like Dr. Richie and Mayor Alfred, grew up in the days of proselytizing about the beauty of integration. Blacks were a novelty and it was fashionable for whites to get to know them. Black children were on the top of the A-list for birthday parties and got first pick for the softball team in what some sociologists call the pet phenomenon.
"Everybody wanted the black experience, and we were the ones who could provide it," Dr. Richie said. "My daughters had more invitations to sleepovers than they could fit on their calendars."
Now the novelty has worn off, and the city has had to get down to the business of insuring peaceful coexistence. Because many of the city's residents live in neighborhoods that are dominated by one race or the other, Shaker Heights is at its most integrated when the children go to school. So it is the public schools that become perhaps the best testing ground for integration.
 
Division Remains
 Slightly more than half the students are black, and some schools had become predominantly black or predominantly white until the city consolidated the system and redrew school boundaries in 1987. Less than three percent of the students are Asian, Hispanic or Indian.
Now each elementary school goes only to the fourth grade and takes in enough children from the nearest black neighborhoods and enough from the nearest white neighborhoods to get an enrollment that is 50-50. The racial balance issue is moot for the other grades because there is one school for all the city's fifth and sixth graders, a middle school for all seventh and eigth graders and the single high school.
But even though the students are in the same building, the high school in particular is more like two schools in one. Advanced placement and honors classes are overwhelmingly white. Regular college preparatory classes are overwhelmingly black.
White students in Yale and Dartmouth sweatshirts walk the hallways with whites to their honors classes and black students in Howard and Spelman sweatshirts walk with blacks to their classes.
 
Separation at Lunch
 In the cafeteria, with only a few exceptions, blacks sit with blacks and whites with whites. They are generally segregated by sex as well. There is no pushing, no shoving, rarely a cross word or rolling of eyes, just peaceful, mutual, voluntary segregation.
"You don't have a choice in the classroom," said Kirk Nims, a black sophomore, sitting over a plate of french fries with his friends. "You eat lunch with who your buddies are."
The students say they have nothing against any other group and have friends of other races who they have known since elementary school. They say that they know they should not segregate themselves, but that once you get to high school, the rules seem to change.
"There's a lot of pressure," said Bill Kuendig, a freshman, who follows the script, too, and sits with other whites at lunch. "If you sit with blacks, your friends would say, "Why are you hanging out with those other people?' I know it's not good. But people tend to go where they're comfortable."
 
Opening Minds
Fight Against Bias Is Continuing
 In Terrence Pollack's Oppression class at the high school the other day, the subject was ethnic stereotypes. Hands shot up as the students fired off examples of one group known for large noses, another for being nerdy, another for having rhythm.
"What is the danger of what you are saying?" Mr. Pollack asked the students, as he began to dissect the stereotypes by tracing their history. "Think about the origins. People take generalities and use them to create divisions and to maintain and perpetuate hatred."
Neither city officials nor Mr. Pollack claim to be able to change minds and hearts, only to open them. "We're not attempting to end prejudice, but to make them more sensitive before they discriminate, to make them more open and tolerant."
There are tests of this kind of awareness inside the classroom and out, like the minor crisis in the yearbook office the other day. The student editors of the predominantly white staff were arranging a final page in the junior section of the yearbook when one of them, Tali Lando, noticed that there were no blacks in the layout.
"We've got too many white people on this page," she said. "You can't have a page with no black people."
"But we don't have any good pictures of them," said Heidi Jacobson, another editor. "We have so many good pictures of white people that we already aren't using. I think we should use the best picture we have."
In that moment, the yearbook staff was doing the balancing act that Shaker Heights itself has come to represent.
"We think about it all the time," Tali said. "That's all we do. Every picture we get we have to think, 'Who do we need? Which group are we not representing?' We try to make sure there is a picture of every kind of person."
 
Not All Success
 Sometimes the lessons do not take. Racial slurs crept into a conversation the other day when Dan Babbitt, a white senior at the high school, was hanging out with his white friends. He said he never expected that from them.
"They seem like nice people," he said. "But you take them out of school and see another side to them. I don't know how to react. If this is happening in Shaker Heights, what's happening in the rest of the country?"
Blacks say that Shaker Heights is not yet a racial promised land and that they face prejudice from counselors who they say steer black students into less rigorous classes, from teachers with low expectations and from children trying out a new racial epithet.
Christopher Washington, the 8-year-old son of a two black doctors, said he thought he was "back in the 1970's" when a teammate on his virtually all-white ice hockey team singled him out and called him "black boy."
"I felt bad," Christopher said. "I told them that's not nice. They're not my friends."
The coach, Carlton Strang, later called a huddle and told the boys: "We all have differences. But as a team there are no differences."
 
Messages From Elders
 The mixed messages, many residents say, often come from the parents, most of whom grew up in segregated worlds and still lapse into segregated bridge clubs and dinner parties. "They want integration for their kids, but they don't think of it for themselves," Dr. Richie said.
Indeed, many children are ahead of the parents because integration is all they know. Heather Taylor, a black seventh grader, is planning her 13th birthday party, a sleepover, next month. She already knows she will invite some of her black friends from school, a few white friends from ballet class, a few from figure skating, and the girl whose bas mitzvah she is going to. "It will be half and half," Heather said.
While this may be easy for her, for others counting may be the only way to insure racial balance until the day when such things come naturally, sociologists say. "Color blindness would be great in the 22d century," Dr. Pettigrew said. "Right now we're still evolving from the sins of the American past."

HEADLINE: New U.N. Leader Is Taking Over At a Time of Great Expectations

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Successful mediation in trouble spots around the world and the fading of the cold war have raised expectations that the United Nations may finally fulfill its lofty goals, helping to defuse threats to peace and security and offering a forum for addressing problems that no country can solve alone.
As the sixth Secretary General of the United Nations, Boutros Ghali, a 69-year-old Egyptian diplomat, will play a pivotal role in determining whether the organization lives up to these rejuvenated hopes over the next five years. Mr. Ghali, who is also a lawyer and a former Deputy Prime Minister, will assume the leadership of the United Nations on Jan. 1. 
 Although Mr. Ghali is not yet giving interviews, he tells associates that his immediate goal is to push through a radical restructuring of the United Nations bureaucracy in 60 days, along the lines of proposals advanced by a group of 20 nations and strongly supported by the United States.
 
Seeking a Role as Peacemaker
 He also wants to establish his credentials quickly as a peacemaker. Mr. Ghali has said he will ask the Security Council to help end the civil war in Somalia, which is sinking deeper each week into anarchy. He also expects the United Nations to take a wider role in encouraging democracy by helping to organize and monitor elections in developing nations.
Most diplomats agree that Mr. Ghali is taking over at a critical moment, and that the United Nations faces an extraordinary opportunity to shape a new international system to replace the configuration wrought by the cold war.
Yet the main question being asked about Mr. Ghali is whether he has the dynamism and forcefulness to seize all that may lie within his grasp as the world order changes.
 
'The Opportunity Is There'
 "The opportunity is there to push the organization's authority forward," said Sir Brian Urquhart, a former Under Secretary General in charge of peacekeeping who is now a scholar in residence at the Ford Foundation. "But does he have the nerve, the habit of authority, to exploit his chances?"
"There is an embarrassment of possibilities before him at the moment. He must clearly define his priorities and go after them," said Edward C. Luck, head of the United Nations Association.
A member of one of Egypt's most affluent and politically active Christian Coptic families, Mr. Ghali counts a prime minister as grandfather and a finance minister as his father. Fluent in English, Arabic and French and known for his ironic wit, he represents an intellectual, cosmopolitan class that was prominent in Egypt between the world wars and was immortalized in the four novels that make up Lawrence Durrell's "Alexandria Quartet."
Indeed, some say he was the model for Nessim Hosnani, the powerful Coptic financier who plays a central role in the story.
To be sure, doubts have been raised about previous secretaries general, most of whom took up the job with less experience of high office and intricate diplomacy than Mr. Ghali. The second-guessing is not surprising, because secretaries general are invariably compromise candidates who win election by gaining the support of different interest groups.
The departing Secretary General, Javier Perez de Cuellar, leaves office this week with a highly successful record, though he was a relatively obscure Peruvian diplomat at the United Nations when he was chosen in 1981. His nomination stirred the same kinds of early doubts now expressed about Mr. Ghali.
 
Risky Exercises In Security
 Formidable challenges await Mr. Ghali, who is taking over just as the United Nations plans new and risky peacekeeping exercises in the Western Sahara, Yugoslavia and Cambodia. The organization has begun nine such operations since 1988, compared with 13 in the previous 43 years.
Many hope Mr. Ghali can capitalize on this expansion in peacekeeping and on the Security Council's rapid reprisals for Iraq's invasion of Kuwait to develop the kind of collective security arrangements that the United Nations' founding fathers envisaged.
The United Nations is also getting more involved in the politically sensitive business of setting standards for its members in fields like human rights, democracy and environmental protection.
Mr. Ghali will appoint a new disaster-relief coordinator this year with unparalleled powers to intervene in emergencies around the world. And he must help organize the most concerted effort to date to legislate against threats to global temperatures, an environmental meeting scheduled this summer in Rio de Janeiro.
 
The Job Is Changing As He Takes Over
 In the cold war days, the big powers often left it to the Secretary General to act as mediator so they could avoid being drawn into a confrontation. But Mr. Ghali has a reinvigorated, less confrontational Security Council that views its responsibilities differently.
"As the cold war was responsible for much of the enlargement of the role of Secretary General, so its termination might produce some shrinkage in that role, even if it also makes the United Nations more useful," said Innis L. Claude, an expert on the United Nations at the University of Virginia, in a new study.
But the new Secretary General must also face the fact that with a top-heavy bureaucracy and members that refuse to pay their dues, the United Nations is ill-equipped to make the most of its opportunities.
Mr. Ghali starts out with the advantage of being a leading choice of both the Security Council and the General Assembly, in marked contrast to the last election, in 1981, when a bitter fight over Kurt Waldheim's decision to seek a third term ended with the surprise selection of Mr. Perez de Cuellar.
As one of the candidates nominated by the Organization for African Unity, Mr. Ghali can also expect cooperation from the 51-nation African bloc in the General Assembly.
But appearances are deceptive. Three permanent Council members -- the United States, Britain and the Soviet Union -- were initially unenthusiastic about Mr. Ghali. They argued that that he did not have enough political stature, and that at 69 he was too old for the job. They also disputed the assertion by African diplomats that Africa had the right to provide the next Secretary General.
There is still an undercurrent of resentment among some diplomats from sub-Saharan African countries, who note that the Security Council chose the only light-skinned "Arab-African" on their list of candidates. Arab diplomats appear to be delighted; Mr. Ghali will be the first Arab secretary general.
 
Pressure to Trim The Secretariat
 The new Secretary General's enthusiasm for administrative reform reflects pressure from a group of 20 industrialized and developing countries -- including India, Nigeria, Indonesia and Mexico as well as the United States, France and Britain -- to adopt a plan for overhauling the Secretariat.
Under the current system, more than 30 top officials report directly to the Secretary General from jealously guarded bureaucratic fiefs responsible for areas ranging from arms control to deep-sea mining. The arrangement developed largely in response to pressure from member governments for plum positions for their citizens.
The plan is to slim down this structure, giving responsibility for day-to-day management to just four or five Under Secretaries General working directly under the Secretary General. This would free Mr. Ghali to concentrate on diplomacy and peacemaking.
Such a reorganization of top echelons would complement the 14 percent reduction in staff that was undertaken by the United Nations at Washington's insistence in 1987. The United Nations has already introduced a budget process that gives the United States a veto over any spending, a move that has since allowed Washington to freeze the organization's budget in real terms.
The restructuring does not address other demands for change, like reducing the General Assembly's 150-item agenda, pruning bodies that no longer have a function, like the Trusteeship Council, and ending fruitless debates on certain economic and social issues.
Mr. Ghali played a critical role on Egypt's team during the negotiations that produced the Camp David peace accords, and diplomats have said that the Israelis expressed admiration for his skills. His experience leads many to believe that he is well-qualified to expand the Secretary General's role as a mediator, though in closer cooperation with the Security Council than in the past.
What remains to be decided is whether he should be given additional peacemaking tools, as various nations and experts have proposed.
Thomas M. Franck, a professor of international law at New York University, has suggested that the United Nations sharply increase the Secretary General's ability to intervene in crises by freeing him from many ceremonial duties and giving him a $40 million mediation fund that would enable him to send investigative teams and observers to areas of tension.
Sir Brian, the former Under Secretary General, favors asking countries to designate troops that would be on call for enforcement activities under Article 43 of the United Nations Charter. Under his proposal, the Security Council would create a "rapid intervention force" that could be sent into countries where law and order have completely broken down, like Somalia, or to tense regions before fighting breaks out.
 
With Dues Unpaid, Plans Are Academic
 But all plans to make the United Nations more effective will come to nothing unless the new Secretary General can persuade governments to pay their dues promptly and in full.
Of the United Nations' 166 member states, 102 have failed to pay their dues in full, leaving the organization with a cumulative shortfall in funding of almost $1 billion.
The United States is still the biggest single debtor, owing $344 million toward the regular budget and another $140 million toward the cost of peacekeeping operations. Although the Reagan Administration encouraged Congress to withhold funding from the United Nations in the 1980's to force some reforms, President Bush now favors full funding and paying off back dues. But with the current strains on its federal budget, Washington has fallen behind again in finding the money.
 
Security Council Faces a Realignment
 The new Secretary General will be working with a Security Council that is likely to be more cooperative toward the United States and other Western members. On Jan. 1, Cuba and Yemen end their two-year terms in rotating seats and will be replaced by Venezuela and Japan.
As a result, the number of Security Council members from the movement of countries espousing "nonalignment" will fall from 7 to 6. And with 9 votes needed to approve a decision by the 15-member Council, the nonaligned will no longer be able to block actions by voting together.
In the longer term, the Council will face pressure to increase its size by naming more permanent members. The five permanent seats are held by the United States, Britain, France, China and now, Russia, which is inheriting the Soviet Union's seat.
Japan and Germany, citing their role as economic superpowers, both want a permanent voice in Council deliberations. A much-discussed proposal would involve changing the United Nations Charter to merge the British and French seats into a single "European" seat representing the 12 European Community countries, which are committed to a joint foreign policy, and thereby answer Germany's concerns.
Japan and several populous developing countries like India, Nigeria and Brazil might then be offered new permanent seats, but without the veto rights of other permanent members.
But the United States, Britain and France, which have a veto over changes in the Charter, oppose any such amendments, saying they see no need to tinker with machinery that is finally working.
So far, Mr. Ghali seems as relaxed about the challenges he faces as the next Secretary General as he once did about his chances of getting the job.
At a lunch in Paris before his election, he told associates he was already "two-thirds" confident of victory, predicting that the Soviet Union, China and France would support him and saying he doubted that the United States or Britain would veto his candidacy.
He also predicted that he would be able to leave his mark on the United Nations because of what he called "my greatest strength -- the fact that I am not looking for re-election."

HEADLINE: Mexico Is Trying Hard to Lift Its Political Profile in the U.S.

BYLINE: By TIM GOLDEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY

 BODY:
When Carlos Salinas de Gortari went to the United States recently, he did the sort of things that Mexican presidents have usually done on such visits. He had breakfast with Henry A. Kissinger. He had lunch with business leaders in New York. And he met with President Bush.
But Mr. Salinas also did things in connection with this trip that no Mexican President had done before -- like visiting with the president of the Mexican Soccer League of New Rochelle, N.Y., sending lobbyists all over Capitol Hill, and giving an award to a Mexican-American human rights advocate who has criticized the pace of Mr. Salinas's political reforms. 
 Abandoning a history of diplomatic and public relations restraint, Mr. Salinas's Government has carried out a systematic effort to transform Mexico's political presence in the United States.
 
Ambitious New Embassy
 In Washington, where Mexico kept a low profile for years, in part to discourage American interference in its affairs, an elegant new Mexican Embassy brims with ambitions to join the city's most influential foreign missions. The traditional handful of quiet diplomats has given way to an army of expensive lobbyists, an office full of trade representatives and a more specialized diplomatic staff that has more than doubled since Mr. Salinas took office in 1988.
In places like Tulsa, Okla., and Plattsburgh, N.Y., officers from Mexico's 40 consulates in the United States have become almost familiar visitors. Where a college or Rotary Club will listen, they extol the virtues of the free trade agreement Mexico is negotiating with the United States and Canada, trying to draw a portrait of a vibrant, sophisticated neighbor eager for closer ties.
And from the Southwest to Chicago and New York, Mexican officials have worked intensely to reverse the antagonism and mistrust that have long characterized the Government's relations with Mexican immigrants to the United States and their descendants. While the Salinas administration has had only limited success so far in drawing Hispanic political groups behind its campaign for free trade, officials hereare suggesting that Mexican-Americans could become a better source of political support for Mexico in the United States.
"The model in that sense has been Israel," a senior aide to Mr. Salinas said. "I don't think that kind of lobby is realistic for us, but I think it is very possible to aspire to a cohesive relationship, a relationship that is mutually beneficial."
It has been Mexico's quest for a North American Free Trade Agreement, more than anything else, that has dictated its new, more vigorous approach to American politics. But in trying to win over many of the American special-interest groups that have opposed a trade pact, the two Governments have also involved more new players in the relationship than either of them might have chosen.
 
Rising Influence
 The Bush Administration finally won permission to negotiate a treaty that Congress could not amend after promising last spring to address concerns about the expected effects of the plan to establish a continental free trade zone. Since then, American labor, environmental and human rights groups, which the Mexican Government used to ignore, have watched their influence in Mexico rise sharply. And while Mexican officials insist that their responsiveness to foreign criticism has been only a matter of publicizing measures they intended to take by themselves, they have to various degrees acknowledged the pressure of foreign advocates in a series of recent actions.
After Mexico's opposition parties charged fraud and foreign critics echoed them, Mr. Salinas in effect annulled two important state elections his party had won. With environmentalists complaining that free trade would make Mexico a haven for American companies seeking to escape United States pollution-control standards, he pledged $460 million to protect the border environment.
When two prominent international human rights groups assailed the prevalence of police torture in Mexico, Mr. Salinas's Attorney General all but thanked them for looking into it. And just before flying to New York, Mr. Salinas met the criticism of American environmentalists with a bill to impose prison terms on those who "intentionally" kill dolphins, sea turtles and other protected marine species.
"The effectiveness of pressures from the United States has become immense," said Adolfo Aguilar Zinser, a Mexican political scientist and a critic of the Government who has been active in the nascent alliances of Mexican, United States and Canadian groups focused on the free trade negotiation.
 
Unknown Results
 For all its intensity, the results of Mexico's new approach to American politics remain to be seen.
After hundreds of conversations with members of Congress and their constituents, Mexican officials still found their hopes for achieving a free trade accord next year to be at the mercy of political forces beyond their influence when Mr. Salinas met with Mr. Bush on Dec. 14. With the United States economy still weak, labor opposition to a trade pact is strong, and with the Presidential primaries only a few months away, Mr. Bush agreed only to try to conclude a trade agreement "as soon as possible."
Since the early days of the free trade debate, the Salinas Government has treated the accord almost less as a mechanism to dismantle tariff barriers than as a means to convey new images of Mexico abroad.
To business executives for whom Mexico evokes thoughts of corruption and state control, the agreement offers a guarantee that the sweeping economic liberalization undertaken by Mr. Salinas will continue after he leaves power. More widely, the Government has sought to expunge old stereotypes of men drinking tequila and sleeping under big hats, offering instead visions of an ancient culture, skilled economic managers and a more forthright political leadership.
The message of a new Mexico has been delivered with particular insistence in Washington.
 
Embassy Doubles in Size
 Under Gustavo Petricioli, the Yale-educated former Finance Minister whom Mr. Salinas named Ambassador to the United States, the Embassy in Wahsington has roughly doubled in size. The number of press attaches and liaisons to Congress has grown even more quickly, and the new embassy is a gleaming structure that occupants invariably describe as being "just down Pennsylvania Avenue from the White House."
At the strong recommendation of Canadian officials, Mexico also began spending millions of dollars to hire many of Washington's best-known trade lobbyists, including Charls E. Walker, Robert Herzstein and at least two former top aides to Senator Lloyd Bentsen, the Texas Democrat who is chairman of the powerful Senate Finance Committee.
 
Visiting Several U.S. Cities
 But it has not simply been a matter of Mr. Salinas's going to Washington, although the Mexican leader has now met with Mr. Bush eight times in three years. In April, Mr. Salinas visited six other American cities, sweeping through Texas and speaking Spanish to Mexican-Americans in Chicago. And in New York, San Antonio and Los Angeles, where he has inaugurated each stop of an art exhibit, "Mexico: Splendors of 30 Centuries," the Government has choreographed celebrations as rich in subtle politics as in art.
Ambassador Petricioli, breaking from a tradition of men who rarely ventured farther south or west than the State Department, has ranged as far as Alaska to tell Americans they could play a pivotal role in free trade, and consular officials have traveled around the country giving similar speeches.
Across the Southwest, the governing party's politicians have been sent as consuls. Their mission, officials said, has been to oversee the public-relations offensive and to improve relations with local Mexican-Americans.
 
Mexican-Americans Undecided
 In telephone interviews, officials of several Mexican-American political groups said they had yet to make up their minds about whether or how actively they would back the campaign for free trade. It seemed at least a small measure of Mexican Government success, however, that Mr. Bush has singled out Hispanic-American support for free trade in recent comments defending the idea.
If anything, Mexican-American officials seemed more convinced that the Salinas Administration was looking at the United States -- and its citizens of Mexican descent -- quite differently.
"They've come to understand that we are Americans first, of Mexican descent -- that we care about democracy and that at times we're very critical of the lack of political openness there," said Antonia Hernandez, president of the Mexican-American Legal Defense and Educational Fund in Los Angeles.
"A relationship as complex as this one is going to have to develop over time," she added. "But they are beginning to see that it might be in their self-interest to better understand us."

HEADLINE: Neediest Cases Donors Reflect on '91

BYLINE: By J. PEDER ZANE

 BODY:
In the big picture, 1991 may well be remembered as the year the Soviet Union dissolved and the Persian Gulf war was won. But for donors to The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund the year was highlighted by personal experiences that brought joy, sorrow, anxiety and the compassion to give to those less fortunate.
"As 1991 closes so too do the doors of adversity open and close," wrote Harriet and Stan Hendel of Highland Mills, N.Y., who gave $25. "For the hostages now free the hard times are a memory. For those who serve the neediest of New York City, the difficult hours are ahead." 
 Ellie and Jerry Spielman of Somerset, N.J., said it "hasn't been a great year." In a note attached to their $25 donation they explained that "like most families we feel the results of the economy but in many respects we are so fortunate. Our first grandchild, a little girl, was born this year."
 
'A Duty to Help Others'
 For several contributors, the year was marked by tragedy. "1991 has been a very difficult year for me, culminating in the death just before Thanksgiving of my much beloved father," wrote a Manhattan woman, who gave $25. "Throughout the year I have received a great deal of support from friends and colleagues at work. I hope this contribution will be used to brighten the holiday of someone who has not had the parental love and collegial friendship I have known."
For others, 1991 is closing on a high note. "I have finally landed an interesting job in a tough market," wrote Mary Numata of Manhattan, who gave $25. "I realize that there are many people who will never be as lucky as I've been. I believe those of us whom fortune has favored do have a duty to help others."
 
Moved by Weak Economy
 Since it was founded in 1912 by Adolph S. Ochs, who was then the publisher of The New York Times, the Neediest Cases has enabled millions of people -- through two World Wars, through the Roaring 20's and the Great Depression, and through the booming 80's and the slumping 90's -- to lend a helping hand to New York's sick, poor and disabled. Last year at least 15,580 individuals and organizations contributed $4.4 million to the Neediest Cases.
The Times covers all of the campaign's overhead so that every dollar collected is passed on to the seven charities. The money buys food for the hungry, homes for the homeless, and clothing for those without. It also helps provide holiday gifts for the city's poorest children.
Several contributors, like Peter and Bernice Landis of Montclair, N.J., said the declining economy prompted them to give their first donations to the Neediest Cases. "As 1991 nears its end, our family is giving thanks for a better year than we had expected while many families are suffering through far worse years than they can remember," they wrote in a letter attached to a $100 donation.
"It is heart-wrenching to read the news stories about the people who are hurting but heart-warming to to read about the people who are trying to ease the pain through their gifts," the couple wrote. "And becoming part of the gift-giving involves a certain selfishness. We admit we feel a lot better about ourselves."

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
The Industry's Year of Exceptional Turbulence

BYLINE: By STUART ELLIOTT

 BODY:
In a tumultuous 12 months during which more large accounts and leading executives moved elsewhere than ever before, how well change was managed said a lot about how the year went.
Every year in advertising has peaks and valleys. But 1991 was unusually intense, the swings magnified by the nation's economic slump. Lost accounts were not easily replaced; new business produced a prosperity that contrasted sharply with the industry's pervasive gloom. 
 Here is a look back at some people and agencies involved in big changes:

 Gordon Bowen: The executive creative director at Ogilvy & Mather New York was to advertising what Mario Cuomo was to politics. After weeks of widely watched pondering, and not a few comparisons to Hamlet, Mr. Bowen decided to accept an offer from McCann-Erickson to work on its flagship Coca-Cola account. His switch from Ogilvy, where he was a key executive on the prestigious American Express account, resonated beyond those agencies. He typified all the top creative talents who, after assiduous courtships, changed addresses.
Among the others were Marty Cooke, who left Chiat/Day/Mojo Inc. for Frankfurt Gips Balkind; Cary Lemkowitz and Eric Weber, who left Young & Rubicam New York for, respectively, Bergelt Litchfield Inc. and Foote, Cone & Belding, and Nina Di Sesa, who left McCann for J. Walter Thompson Chicago. Will they meet the high expectations of their new employers, who are paying them high salaries?

 The Leo Burnett Company: A series of account victories -- among them, Miller Lite beer and the Sony Corporation of America -- brought in about $225 million in new billings, by far the most won by any agency.
Despite that, spending cutbacks by current clients kept total domestic billings flat. Burnett will be "tightening its belt" in 1992, Richard B. Fizdale, the incoming chairman and chief executive, has told employees.

 Chiat/Day/Mojo: Like one of the bungee jumpers in a television commercial for Reebok International Ltd., the agency has snapped back: more than $100 million in new billings has reversed a string of account losses. A daunting achievement in any year, it was particularly impressive in 1991.
Chiat also benefited from executive mobility when Joel Babbit, president of Babbit & Reiman Advertising Inc., joined as executive vice president and general manager in New York.

 Della Femina, McNamee Inc.: A string of account losses, as well as the end of Pan American World Airways, cost the agency a quarter of its billings, around $200 million. And its chairman, Jerry Della Femina, kept up a public feud with the agency's French parent, Euro RSCG. But ever-optimistic, the agency is aggressively pursuing new business and spirits seem high. Witness a holiday greeting that Louise McNamee, the agency's president, sent Mr. Della Femina, in a print advertisement for Cutty Sark Scotch. A tiny card visible in the ad read, "To Jerry Love, Louise."

 Robert L. Dilenschneider: The longtime public relations executive broke key rules in advertising by generating more attention for himself than for his clients. He was forced out as president and chief executive of Hill & Knowlton Inc. amid controversy over accounts like the Church of Scientology and reports of clashes with Martin Sorrell, chief executive of the agency's parent, the WPP Group P.L.C. of London.
Now, the man who once advocated "bigger is better" is changing courses, opening a small firm, the Dilenschneider Group, with offices in Manhattan and Chicago -- a far cry from Hill & Knowlton's 66 offices in 25 countries. Still, few executives would deny they continue to watch Mr. Dilenschneider.

 Goodby, Berlin & Silverstein: An upstart agency that more than doubled its billings, catapulting into the ranks of leading midsized agencies by landing accounts like the Carl's Jr. fast-food chain and American Isuzu Motors Inc. Now it faces the challenge of delivering on a promise of outstanding creative work. Industry executives are intensely curious about the agency's first Isuzu campaign, due in early March.

 Levine, Huntley, Schmidt & Bea ver: A midsized agency that built a reputation for sprightly, effective advertising for Subaru of America, Frito-Lay and others, it could not survive the loss of those two accounts.
Other accounts followed, as did the chief executive, Robert H. Schmidt, and the agency had no succession plan. While Edward H. Vick, the president and chief executive, and Rochelle Klein, senior vice president and executive creative director, were praised for their efforts to stem the collapse, the shop collapsed with a dizzying rapidity.

 John L. Mezzina and William G. Brown Jr.: Two months after leaving Young & Rubicam, where they worked on the Camel cigarette account, this pair shocked the industry when the R. J. Reynolds Tobacco Company awarded the creative portion of the $40 million account to Mezzina/Brown Inc.
Industry executives are wondering how they and about a dozen former Y. & R. colleagues who followed them to Mezzina/Brown will fare on their own, particularly as anti-smoking activists intensify attacks on Camel's "Smooth character" cartoon camel campaign.

 Ogilvy & Mather:  The WPP agency suffered one of its worst years ever. It lost several important assignments, like the creative portion of the American Express charge card account and the domestic portion of the Compaq Computer Corporation account. And several senior executives left, including Mr. Bowen; David Warden, who joined Mr. Bowen at McCann, and Parry Merkley, who joined Doremus & Company. And trade publications continue to report that clients like Sears, Roebuck & Company are looking elsewhere for ideas on special projects.
Ogilvy's top management is working strenuously to prevent the stream of losses from becoming a flood. Assisting those efforts was a decision by L.A. Gear Inc. to award the agency its $30 million account. An important test for the agency is its revamped advertising for an important remaining client, Hardee's Food Systems Inc., due in mid-January.

 Michael Ovitz: He is not an adman, nor does he work for an ad agency. Yet the Hollywood power broker turned the ad world topsy-turvy when Coca-Cola appointed Creative Artists Agency, the talent agency of which he is chairman, as its worldwide marketing and communications consultant.
Industry executives are hungry for clues as to what changes in Coke's advertising might be made by Mr. Ovitz and his team of 14 C.A.A. employees.

 James Tennant and Mark Wein stein: The pair, top executives at Foote, Cone & Belding Direct, took 18 colleagues with them -- and two clients -- to found their own breakaway shop. Foote, Cone sued, and six days later, T.W.O. Direct -- for the Tennant Weinstein Organization -- closed, victim of what Advertising Age called "a comedy of errors."
According to Foote, Cone's court papers, Mr. Tennant and Mr. Weinstein sought to be reimbursed for at least one dinner at which they recruited a Foote, Cone executive for T.W.O. Direct. Mr. Tennant, who was president of Foote, Cone Direct, returned, along with his 18 co-workers. But Mr. Weinstein, who had been Foote, Cone Direct's senior vice president and managing director, decided "to pursue other opportunities."

 Wieden & Kennedy: Lauded for its quirky campaigns for Nike Inc., the Portland, Ore., agency gained a second significant national account when it wrested Subaru from Levine, Huntley.
The victory gives Wieden a chance to dispel perceptions that its only skill is a talent to amuse while selling sneakers. Many executives have praised the agency's advertising for Subaru's 1992 models, carrying the theme, "What to drive," though they will be counting just how many cars Subaru can sell in the face of the automotive industry's severe slump.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Notable figures in the advertising business this year included, from the left in a montage, Jerry Della Femina and Louise McNamee, whose agency lost a quarter of its accounts; Edward H. Vick, whose agency collapsed because of lost accounts; Michael Ovitz, whose talent agency was hired to market Coca-Cola, and John L. Mezzina and William G. Brown Jr., whose agency snared the R. J. Reynolds account. (The New York Times (Vick, Mezzina and Brown) and Associated Press (Ovitz))

HEADLINE: U.S. Health Bill Expected to Rise By 11% for '91

BYLINE: By PHILIP J. HILTS

 BODY:
The Commerce Department plans to report today that it expects national spending on health to have increased 11 percent in 1991 over the previous year, the fifth consecutive year with a double-digit increase.
The report said spending on health care for 1991 was $738 billion, based on data collected for the first 11 months of 1991 and a projection for December. 
 The department also predicted that health care spending would be $817 billion in 1992 and that such expenditures would rise at an average annual rate of 12 percent to 13 percent over the next five years.
The figures appear in the Department's United States Industrial Outlook for 1992.
 
Costs Helps Fuel Rise
 Because of slow economic growth, the report projects, the proportion of the nation's total output of goods and services that is represented by health care will rise to a record 14 percent in 1992, slightly more than the 1991 level.
The rise in the costs of health care, which is largely responsible for the increase, is the subject of much concern nationally, and many proposals in Congress have been put forward to halt the increase and to extend health insurance to cover the 34 million people who are now believed to be without it.
The report cited several reasons for the rising spending, including the aging of the population, increases in doctors' fees, the high cost and increased use of high-tech equipment and the growth in the use of psychiatric care.
In addition, it noted, more than 600,000 jobs were created in doctors' offices and hospitals between 1988 and 1990, accounting for some of the increase.
But the report said one factor in the cost of health care had been falling: malpractice claims. The Commerce Department reported that the number of malpractice claims dropped steadily, to 8 claims for every 100 doctors in 1989, the last year for which data are available, from 17.8 claims in 1985.
In an analysis of the figures, a health advocacy group in Washington, Public Citizen Health Research Group, pointed to growth in employment as one of the most significant trends.
 
'Megatons of Paperwork'
 "It is likely that most of this increase is in administrative personnel needed to push the megatons of paperwork generated by having 1,500 different health insurers in this country," it said.
Regarding the causes for the rise in spending, Dr. James S. Todd, executive vice president of the American Medical Association, added to the list the cost of paperwork brought on by new regulations, the increased cost of labor and supplies and treatment of an increasing number of people who have little or no hope of recovery.
He acknowledged that doctors' fees had risen, but he argued that limiting doctors' charges would be "an arbitrary and unreasoning" way of dealing with costs. Other costs would be more sensible to control, he suggested.

THE ECONOMY
Robert Mosbacher said Japan was partly to blame for the lingering U.S. recession, becoming the first Administration official to blame Japan publicly. The Commerce Secretary's comments are especially significant because he will head Mr. Bush's re-election campaign. [Page D1.]
SAIF is a rare bird in the wilderness of workers' compensation insurance: a failing state-owned insurer that suddenly stopped its fall and rose to become a striking business success. [D1.] 
 The sharp decline of interest rates has affected virtually all Americans in one way or another. But economists agree that the elderly, many of whom live on fixed incomes, have been made especially vulnerable. [A10.]
Orders rose for American-made machine tools in November, a trade association reported. [D2.]
 
COMPANIES
Wal-Mart now seems to be angling toward Safeway and Kroger in the grocery business, having overtaken K Mart and Sears in retailing. The company has acquired a small Arkansas grocery chain and is expanding its Super Center stores, which devote half of their space to groceries. Potential rivals fear it will use its clout with suppliers to demand price breaks. [D1.]
G.M.'s latest experimental vehicle has a body of carbon fiber, like many race cars, making it extremely light. [D2.]
 
THE MEDIA BUSINESS
This was a year when the biggest thing on television was the stuff of real life. [D6.]
If it's the end of the year, those must be special year-end issues of magazines on the newsstand. [D6.]
Edward L. Bernays recently turned 100, giving him the most protracted view of public relations, a discipline he is credited with creating. He is not happy with the field now. [D6.]
The newspaper industry may be united in grappling with a steep decline in advertising, but there is no unanimity about what will happen when the economy recovers. Alex S. Jones: Press. [D6.]
The last year was unusually intense in the ad industry, with the usual swings magnified. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D1.]
International
The Bank of Japan reduced its official discount rate by half a percentage point, to 4.5 percent, effective immediately. [D5.]
The Bundesbank's decision to raise interest rates shows that despite lofty rhetoric, European unity is easier said than done. [D1.]
 
TODAY'S COLUMNS
Investment bankers and economists say the quarter-point drop in interest rates since mid-December is likely to bring a torrent of corporate debt issues soon. Kenneth N. Gilpin: Credit Markets. [D5.]
Infinity has generated some excitement in broadcasting circles with its plan to buy WFAN-AM. The deal appears set, assuming investors buy stock in Infinity, which was taken private three years ago. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D4.]
