HEADLINE: Wilder Seeks to Mix Black Support With Middle-Class Votes

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: GORHAM, N.H.

 BODY:
At 7:15, the pale gray light of an early December morning found L. Douglas Wilder, Presidential candidate, standing in the cold outside a paper mill eagerly, and unsuccessfully, looking for warm hands to shake. Arriving at the James River Corporation plant a half hour behind schedule, Mr. Wilder had missed the 7 o'clock shift change when more than 200 workers report to work. So Mr. Wilder, who readily acknowledges that his campaign's main problem is that few people even know his name, missed a chance to meet anyone except a handful of stragglers.
The Wilder campaign seems disorganized, with few volunteers or paid workers in New Hampshire. His fund raising has not met his own expectations. He offers little more than slogans and a few details on important issues like health care and the economy. The Presidential-campaign experience of his manager, Joe Johnson, consists of running Michael S. Dukakis's losing effort in Nevada in 1988. 
 At first blush, Mr. Wilder's effort gives pundits and political professionals little reason to think of the Governor of Virginia as a serious contender or to predict that his candidacy will last much beyond the New Hampshire primary.
Yet this does not seem to dent Mr. Wilder's optimism. In part, he says, that is because he does not view his opponents as all that imposing.
"The amazing thing is that when people talk about my candidacy they talk as if I'm up against giants," Mr. Wilder said. "Who's a giant out there? I don't quarrel with you and I'm not being Socratic. I'm just being logical. If I'm so weak, who's so strong?"
For most other candidates in Mr. Wilder's position, such sang-froid would be unthinkable. But his campaign strategists and professional political prognosticators think he has an ace in the hole: the black vote.
 
His Metamorphosis
Playing Up Racial Pride
 At the start of his quest for the Democratic nomination, Mr. Wilder seemed to downplay the importance of his race in his Presidential campaign, even rejecting an idea made by some supporters that he announce his intention to run at George Washington's home, Mount Vernon, thereby establishing a link between the nation's first President and its first black elected Governor.
But in a contest for the hearts of the liberal voters who are such a dominant force in the Democratic presidential primaries, and with the Rev. Jesse Jackson opting not to run, Mr. Wilder seems to have chosen to emphasize the main characteristic that distinguishes him from the other Democratic contenders.
Mr. Wilder's unabashed appeal to racial pride was on display at the close of the first debate among the six Democratic aspirants in early December. While other candidates offered visions of the basis of their campaigns -- establishing a national health care plan, aiding the middle class, or making the nation competitive -- Mr. Wilder summed up by referring to the Jim-Crow environment of his youth and much of his adult life and by evoking the memory of a black abolitionist.
"My parents named me for Frederick Douglass," Mr. Wilder said, glossing over the fact his first name is Lawrence and his middle name is spelled differently from the 19th-century hero. "He was a slave. And on September the 3d, 1838, he escaped and found his dream for freedom.
"I wonder if on that date he could have imagined that just 153 years later, his namesake, a grandson of slaves, would be speaking to the American people as the Governor of the Commonwealth of Virginia, running for the Presidency of the United States of America."
Such a nakedly racial appeal represents a metamorphosis for Mr. Wilder. Having won his party's nomination for Lieutenant Governor in 1985 and Governor in 1989 without a primary fight, he has not run a campaign against a white Democrat in years. As a result, in his last two campaigns he played down race in favor of themes like fiscal responsibility and support for the death penalty, which appeal more to moderate whites. He was able to pursue such a strategy secure in the knowledge that blacks would support him out of either racial solidarity or fear of his Republican opponent.
With his victories in Virginia demonstrating an ability to win white votes, many Democrats saw Mr. Wilder as a more palatable alternative to Mr. Jackson, whose strong identification with blacks and the poor was, some party strategists felt, alienating white middle-class voters.
Though the Governor, whose relationship with Mr. Jackson is cool at best, declines to view himself as a rival to the civil rights leader, he does draw distinctions between himself, an elected official in charge of a state, and Mr. Jackson, who for the first time last year was elected to an official position and became a nonvoting delegate to the Senate for the District of Columbia.
"Jesse is an activist, perhaps the foremost activist of our time," Mr. Wilder said during a campaign flight in a Commonwealth of Virginia airplane in New Hampshire. "I'm an elected official. I have to work with more coalitions, tolerate more diverse viewpoints. It's not stellar, but it's more productive over the long run. We've just chosen different roads."
Yet, the roads he and Mr. Jackson have chosen to travel to capture the Democratic nomination seem, at least for the present, to be strikingly similar. With Mr. Jackson eschewing a run for the Democratic nomination, Mr. Wilder appears to be positioning himself to inherit Mr. Jackson's constituency.
But one impediment to Mr. Wilder's securing the black vote may be Mr. Jackson himself. In late December, Mr. Wilder accused Mr. Jackson of pressuring delegates who supported the civil rights leader in 1988 to run as uncommitted this time. Indeed, three days before Mr. Wilder appeared at a recent convention in Las Vegas of black elected officials, Mr. Jackson met with a number of those attending and asked them not to commit to any candidate just yet.
"His message, as far as I can discern, was, 'Chill,' " said Regis F. Groff, a Colorado State Senator and the president of the National Black Caucus of State Legislators. Mr. Jackson has said the Governor was using him as a scapegoat for his faltering campaign.
 
His Strategy
Gathering Speed In the South
 Though they insist they are not ignoring the country's first primary, Wilder strategists make clear that as the campaign moves south from frigid and overwhelmingly white New Hampshire to states with significant black populations, they hope his candidacy -- like Mr. Jackson's run in 1988 -- will gather speed.
"It's too early to call New Hampshire," said Mr. Johnson, the campaign manager. "There may be a situation where we might do well there. But the expectation for us is not in Iowa and New Hampshire, but in Maryland and on Super Tuesday."
Maryland may well be the most critical state for Mr. Wilder in the early stages of the primary campaign. A solid showing by the Governor in the state's March 3 primary could garner sufficient news media attention to bring out black voters in the Florida, Georgia, Mississippi, Louisiana and Texas primaries that will be held a week later.
There are indications that Mr. Wilder has a toehold in Maryland. A telephone survey by Mason Dixon Public Opinion of 428 Maryland Democrats who are likely to vote in the primary conducted showed Mr. Wilder leading the Democratic field with 20 percent of the vote. Bill Clinton, Governor of Arkansas, followed with 12 percent, and Senator Bob Kerrey of Nebraska was third with 10 percent.
The survey, conducted from Dec. 11 through Dec. 14, has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus 4.5 percent.
Spurred primarily by the spillover from Washington across the state line east into Prince Georges County, Maryland has one of the fastest-growing black populations in the country. Blacks now make up nearly 25 percent of Maryland's population, according to 1990 census data, and the state is projected to have the third-highest percentage of blacks of any state, after Mississippi and Louisiana, by the turn of the century.
And, especially in Prince Georges County, which contains many Federal employees, these blacks tend to be middle class, well-read and likely to vote. In 1988, Mr. Jackson got 88 percent of the black vote in the state's primary, which was won by Mr. Dukakis.
"We have a rather large and rather sophisticated black population that could go heavily for Wilder," said Ray Jenkins, editorial page editor of the Baltimore Evening Sun. "They vote heavily, at least in Presidential elections."
If his strategy is successful, Mr. Wilder will have positioned himself for a serious shot for the Vice Presidential nomination or for a future run for the Presidency.
"I don't think he believes he can be a Presidential candidate this year," said Larry J. Sabato, a professor of government at the University of Virginia who is a veteran Wilder-watcher. "But if he solidifies his support, he could become a real player at the convention. And in the right circumstance, he could even set his sights on the Vice Presidency."
Mention the possibility that he is running for the Vice Presidency to Mr. Wilder, however, and one gets a testy response.
"I didn't file for that," Mr. Wilder said in an interview in his office in Virginia's capital, Richmond. "I didn't announce for that. I've never said that. I know it's difficult for people like you to understand that people like me could want to be the top. Maybe you never wanted to be the top. But I've always wanted to be at the top.
"When I say people like you, I don't mean it personally. It is difficult -- very, very difficult --for people to assume that an African-American could actually believe that he could be the top. And it always has happened to me in reference to elections that people have said, oh come on, you got to be kidding. Governor? Of the state of Virginia?"
Being Governor may provide a solution to Mr. Wilder's current money problems. Last month the campaign revised its plan to raise $1 million by the end of the year, saying it hoped to have that amount by Feb. 1. But as a sitting Governor -- albeit one who cannot succeed himself -- who is about to submit a budget, Mr. Wilder will probably get an infusion of contributions from lobbyists and others who do business with the state hoping to shape the budget to benefit them, Mr. Sabato said.
 
His Appeal
For Middle Class, Fiscal Caution
 While gaining a solid black base is intended to make Mr. Wilder a serious contender in the South and border states, his strategy after Super Tuesday is to do well in financially strapped states like Michigan and New York by playing up his fiscal conservatism.
Mr. Wilder also hopes to attract middle-class votes with his idea of an immediate $50 billion cut in Federal spending. He says $10 billion in the savings would come out of defense, $15 billion from items in the budget that are not needed and $25 billion by cutting administrative and bureaucratic waste. The savings would be used to cut taxes and to send money to cities and states to handle social problems like drug abuse and homelessness.
Describing his ideas in his announcement speech, Mr. Wilder said, "My plan: Put America first, begin the process of getting the economy moving again, to put us on a path that will attack Federal spending, to begin the process of relieving the regressive tax burden on middle-class families and to aid localities that provide innovative leadership in attacking the pernicious evil of drugs and drug-related crime."
But so far, Mr. Wilder has not presented a detailed plan of exactly what would be cut to provide money for such projects.
"It's amazing what you can do," he said. "You call on the agencies and say give me three percent cuts across the board; five percent cuts across the board in administration. Tell me the things that you would rather not do without and the things you feel you could sacrifice. Allow them the flexibility. I did that in Virginia."
On the stump, he likes to boast of his ability to balance Virginia's budget in the face of revenue shortfalls without raising taxes and laying off less than 400 state employees.
But such a message is not the stuff that traditionally has brought in black support. Mr. Wilder also lacks the fire and passion of Mr. Jackson and does not have a personal history of being on the front lines of the civil rights movement. Speaking at the convention of black officials in Las Vegas, Mr. Wilder recounted a recent visit to Selma, Ala., where in 1965 state troopers on horseback clubbed peaceful civil rights marchers as they crossed the city's Edmund Pettus Bridge.
"As we drove across the Edmund Pettus Bridge I said to myself, 'I wasn't here in 1965, and didn't participate in that battle,' " Mr. Wilder told his audience.
He quickly minimized his absence, however, with a joke. "I have often said that if everybody was there on that Edmund Pettus Bridge that said they were there, the bridge would have fallen down," he quipped.

HEADLINE: U.S. Says Number of Farm Owners Is at Lowest Level in the Century

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
A Government study has found that the number of people who own farmland in this country has dwindled to the lowest level in the century.
The report, by the United States Agriculture Department, said nearly half of all American farmland is in the hands of 124,000 owners. 
 Most of the owners are individuals or couples who own the farms jointly, it said; corporations hold about 15 percent of the land.
 
Farm Size Tripled
 And while total farm acreage was slightly more than in 1900, the average farm size has more than tripled, the study found.
The number of owners of farmland is of great interest to the Government because as it drops, decisions about the use of land and about improvements, conservation and other significant areas will be made by an increasingly smaller proportion of the overall population, the report said.
The report, by the Agriculture Department's Economic Research Service, also found that many current owners have little direct role in operating the nation's 2 million farms and ranches.
"That means that decisions concerning larger operations are being made by fewer farmers and fewer, but proportionately more, non-farmer landowners," the report said. "These changes in the structure of farmland ownership parallel the enlargement of farm size, reduction in farm numbers and reduction in farm employment."
The report said a 1989 survey of farm ownership estimated that by the end of 1988, 2.95 million people owned 833 million acres of private farmland. But roughly 124,000 owners held 47 percent of the land. And owners whose holdings consisted of fewer than 50 acres accounted for 30 percent of the total but held only 2 percent of the acreage.
 
Outlook for Some Trends
 The report also said that of the total number of owners, 44 percent were non-operators who held 41 percent of the land.
"The century began with more operators than owners, and is ending with substantially more owners than operators," the study said. "Compared with owners and operators, the amount of land in farms, which peaked in the 1950's, has changed little."
The study said current trends should leave 1.7 million farms and 2.7 million owners by the year 2000, as against 5.7 million farms and 4.9 million agricultural landowners in 1900.
"The shrinking number of farms and landowners will contribute further to the decline of rural communities and may affect markets for commodities and factors of production," the report said.

HEADLINE: Wal-Mart Is Making Supermarkets Edgy

BYLINE: By EBEN SHAPIRO

 BODY:
Wal-Mart, which overtook K Mart and Sears to become the nation's largest retailer, now seems to be angling toward Safeway and Kroger in the grocery business.
In October, Wal-Mart Stores Inc. acquired a small Arkansas grocery chain, Phillips Companies, and is expanding its Super Center stores, which devote half of their space to groceries. Wal-Mart is also stepping up its efforts to sell ground beef, freshly baked bread, heads of lettuce, and other fresh foods at its Sam's Clubs, the company's fast-growing warehouse-style stores. 
 For now, food is a small part of Wal-Mart's overall sales. But given Wal-Mart's track record of rolling over competitors and its unabashed willingness to throw around its weight with suppliers, many food retailers and some food processors are already nervous.
"You'd have to be asleep not to be concerned," said Craig Schnuck, chief executive of a 59-store Schnuck's Market grocery chain based in St. Louis. "No one has ever been as big as Wal-Mart is."
What supermarket executives seem to fear most is that Wal-Mart will use its clout with suppliers to demand price breaks not available to mere grocers. Some also acknowledge that Wal-Mart's fabled skills in distribution could allow the chain to offer lower prices on many grocery products.
To combat Wal-Mart in areas like St. Louis, traditional supermarket operators are sprucing up their offerings of fresh fruits and meats and emphasizing service. Mr. Schnuck said his company was also exploring ways to make its operations more efficient.
Some food executives are worried that Wal-Mart will become so powerful that it will demand special prices on big wholesale orders. "There are a lot of manufacturers that are concerned about special deals," said Charles McCarthy, president of the sales division of the Campbell Soup Company. "There is always pressure from the power buyers like Wal-Mart once they have a lot of your business."
Wal-Mart is also building a line of its own "Sam's American Choice" food products in crisp, attractive packaging. The company is selling its own version of cola, cookies and juice in some stores. Last year, the company acquired the McLane Company, a grocery distributor.
"I believe that it is their intention to be one of the largest food retailers in the country by the end of the century," said Ryan Mathews, editor of Grocery Marketing, published by Gorman Publishing in Chicago.
Wal-Mart executives declined to discuss their plans to expand food and grocery sales.
But Robert F. Buchanan, a retail analyst with Alex. Brown & Sons in Baltimore, said there was little doubt that Wal-Mart was "moving big into food." Food retailing accounted for $362 billion in sales last year, according to Alex. Brown, compared with $84.6 billion in discount department store sales last year. Mr. Buchanan said that building up grocery sales will play an increasingly large role in the company's stated goal of reaching $100 billion in sales by the end of the decade.
Last summer, Wal-Mart struck a deal with Mexico's biggest retailer, Cifra S.A., to open warehouse-style stores in Mexico that would sell a range of products, including stationery, electronics and food.
 
Uneasy Ripples
 Some in the food industry are concerned that Wal-Mart will gain too much power as it expands into an important new area of food retailing. In Canada and Europe, where there is a far greater degree of consolidation in food retailing, a few large retailers wield tremendous influence over the food industry. "It makes negotiating more difficult," said Mr. McCarthy of Campbell.
Already, some of Wal-Mart's recent moves are sending uneasy ripples through the food industry. In November, Wal-Mart told manufacturers that it would no longer deal with the brokers who act as middlemen between manufacturers and retailers.
Wal-Mart says cutting out the brokers will improve communications and allow the company to react quicker in ordering. The company argues that the sheer volume of its business -- it had sales of $32.6 billion last year -- mandates that it has direct ties to its suppliers.
 
'This Is Not Passive'
 Some food retailing executives have a different interpretation. "It's Wal-Mart flexing its muscles and saying we are a national, dominant retailer and we decide the rules of a game, and not you," said Brian Sharoff, president of the Private Label Manufacturers Association in New York. "At one time retailers were viewed as passive. This is not passive."
Grocers are also worried that Wal-Mart's decision to stop working with brokers will give it a price advantage. Food processors pay brokers small commissions for selling their products. Grocers say that manufacturers dealing directly with Wal-Mart could pass the savings on to the chain in the form of discounts.
Federal law bars food makers from cutting out middlemen simply to avoid paying a commission and then passing along the savings to a supermarket in the form of additional discounts.
"We are concerned that people might find a way to get around that," Mr. Schnuck said. "I just want to be given the same opportunity that the other guy is given."
The supermarket industry has commissioned a study, due out early next year, on whether discounters are getting special prices not available to other retailers.
 
Selling Through 'Super Centers'
 Some retailing experts say they think Wal-Mart's primary food-retailing efforts will be done through its Super Centers, which range from 97,000 square feet to 211,000 square feet.
Half of each center is a supermarket. The other half is a typical Wal-Mart, selling underwear, brooms, diapers, toys and myriad other apparel and household items. Wal-Mart has just six of the giant centers, all in the Midwest, but plans to open another 14 in rural areas next year.
Analysts said the stores are more effective than the huge Hypermart stores that Wal-Mart tested but decided not to expand after a poor response from consumers. The Hypermarts were too unwieldy for consumers, who were also put off by the difficulty of consistently finding the same product at the stores. Regular Wal-Mart stores already do a brisk business in supermarket-type snacks and other small items, like peanuts, candy, potato chips, napkins and paper towels.
 
K Mart's Food Strategy
 Wal-Mart's chief rival, the K Mart Corporation, is also looking at selling more food. The company is testing a complete grocery store as part of a Super K Mart Center in Medina, Ohio, near Cleveland. The store opened in August and devotes nearly one-third of its 147,000 square feet to selling groceries. The combination stores are designed to appeal to time-pressed families that are increasingly accustomed to shopping at discounters.
K Mart also sells groceries through its Pace membership warehouses. These, along with the Costco and Price Club warehouse chains, are taking a bigger share of the food retailing pie each year.
Still, supermarket executives said that the discounters will find supermarket chains to be more formidable competition than the small hardware stores and five-and-dimes that Wal-Mart and K Mart overran to start the discount industry 20 years ago.
"Food stores are much more complex than discount stores," said Michael W. Wright, chairman and chief executive of Super Valu Stores Inc., a supermarket operator and food wholesaler based in Minneapolis.
 
After the Recession
 Also, some supermarket operators assert that the success of Wal-Mart and others that sell groceries in no-frills warehouses has been built on a bad economy. "This recession is made to order for discount operations," said Michael J. Rourke, a spokesman for the Great Atlantic and Pacific Tea Company Inc., which operates supermarkets in 26 states. "As the economy improves, their appeal will not be as great."
But Wal-Mart's expertise in logistics and distribution will be an enormous benefit in the grocery business, where efficient shipping and warehousing are crucial to profits. Mr. Buchanan, the Alex. Brown analyst, recently shopped at Wal-Mart grocery stores in Missouri and said that prices were 11 to 14 percent lower than at competing local grocery stores.
"Traditional supermarkets have left their flanks exposed," said Mr. Mathews, the editor of Grocery Marketing. "They didn't understand that people were willing to trade off some amenities for cost. If you have a family of four and you are making $35,000 a year, you really don't care if there is classical music on the Muzak system."

HEADLINE: Mosbacher Criticizes Japanese

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Commerce Secretary Robert A. Mosbacher today became the first Administration official to say publicly that Japan was partly to blame for the lingering recession in the United States.
Mr. Mosbacher's comments are especially significant because he will head Mr. Bush's re-election campaign next year and serve as the chief fund-raiser. The remarks came as President Bush prepared to leave Monday on a trip to Japan and three other Asian countries that will focus on trade issues. 
 That trip was originally scheduled in November as a demonstration of America's political and military commitment to its Asian allies, but it has now become focused on trade and jobs. The change of emphasis seems to reflect President Bush's slippage in polls and attacks from Democrats saying that he is more concerned with foreign affairs than with the nation's economic problems.
 
Remarks on 'Meet the Press'
 Today, on the NBC News program "Meet the Press," Mr. Mosbacher was asked whether he agreed with a comment by Representative John D. Dingell, Democrat of Michigan, that the current recession is being "made in Japan." Mr. Mosbacher replied, "Not totally, no."
Pressed as to whether he meant that Japan was partly responsible, Mr. Mosbacher replied, "I would say they are exacerbating the problem" by not allowing in American exports that would produce jobs in the United States. Nearly 20,000 jobs are created by each $1 billion increase in United States exports, he added.
Mr. Mosbacher mentioned earlier in the program that the United States had the option of restricting Japanese car exports to the United States. But he cautioned that the nation's deficit with Japan grew over many years and could not be quickly reduced.
 
Vehement Denial by Japan
 Hiroshi Hirabayashi, the deputy chief of the Japanese Embassy in Washington, vehemently denied that Japan bore any responsibility for the United States recession. Some American managers and Government officials "want to place the responsibility on the foreigners, on the Japanese," he said, adding, "In my opinion, it's outrageous."
Japan's Ministry of International Trade and Industry has made many efforts to accommodate American demands, he said, citing voluntary restraints on car exports and programs to increase imports. "MITI has offered many initiatives to Mr. Mosbacher, and then he blames the Japanese," he continued. "I think it is not fair."
Some obstacles to imports do persist in Japan, Mr. Hirabayashi added, but he said the main reasons for the trade imbalance, particularly in automobiles, lay in poor-quality management at American corporations and the failure of American political leaders to reduce the Federal budget deficit and adopt other responsible economic policies.
 
Shifts in Bush Stance
 Mr. Mosbacher's willingness to blame Japan was only the latest in a series of signs that the Administration is concerned about the trade issue. President Bush, for example, now refers to the free-trade agreement under negotiation with Mexico as a "fair trade" agreement. Fair trade has often been a euphemism in Washington for protectionism, although Mr. Bush has repeatedly said, most recently on Thursday, that he will not become a protectionist.
 
No Car Cuts by Japanese
 The Bush Administration is under pressure to take more aggressive action on trade, partly because industries suffering from foreign competition are reluctant to give generously to the President's re-election campaign. Robert K. Morris, the director of international trade at the National Association of Manufacturers, said Mr. Mosbacher might be freer to address these industries' concerns now because he will soon be leaving the Administration.
President Bush announced last month Mr. Mosbacher's departure from the Commerce Department, and Administration officials privately predict that this will take place around Jan. 15.
Many more United States industries have developed close ties with Japanese companies and the Japanese economy and are reluctant to see those ties jeopardized, Mr. Morris added.
Also on "Meet the Press" today, Deputy Foreign Minister Koji Watanabe of Japan said his Government had been studying ways to expand imports of American goods. But he repeatedly denied reports that Japan might voluntarily cut shipments of automobiles to the United States.
Mr. Watanabe appeared prior to Mr. Mosbacher on the television program and did not have an opportunity to respond to the Commerce Secretary's remarks.
The House majority leader, Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, and five Democratic members of Congress from Michigan proposed legislation on Dec. 20 to reduce sales of Japanese cars in the United States unless the bilateral trade deficit is reduced from $42 billion now to less than $5 billion by 1997.
Asked whether significant improvement was possible in five years, Mr. Mosbacher responded, "We're not going to get that."
Mr. Dingell said on Dec. 20, "I don't think that it is good that America's trade policy is made in Japan or that the recession, which our people are now undergoing, is made in Japan."
Senator Donald W. Riegle Jr., Democrat of Michigan, said the same day, "The continuing trade invasion by Japan is the central part of our economic problems and the continuing loss of American jobs."
 
Adopting Gephardt Concern
 Clyde Prestowitz, a former Reagan Administration trade negotiator, said Mr. Mosbacher's statements appeared to represent a shift in the American position on trade.
"In my experience over the last 10 years, the position of successive Administrations has been that recessions have been due to macroeconomics," like the nation's low savings rates and persistent Federal budget deficits, he said.
 Mr. Gephardt's legislation would cap sales not only of Japanese imports but also of many cars produced in the United States by Japanese companies using large quantities of imported parts.
Mr. Mosbacher adopted this concern today, saying that these companies should rely more heavily on American-made parts.
He said, "What we want to do is make sure in free trade we have access, not only in Japan but in the U.S., to U.S. auto-parts makers into the transplants -- the Japanese companies here -- so that we can sell U.S. auto parts in our own country."

HEADLINE: The Bank of Japan Cuts Its Discount Rate to 4.5%

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News

DATELINE: TOKYO, Monday, Dec. 30

 BODY:
The Bank of Japan reduced its official discount rate by half a percentage point today, to 4.5 percent, effective immediately, central bank officials said.
The reduction was expected after the Federal Reserve Board cut the United States discount rate to 3.5 percent on Dec. 20. 
 Finance Minister Tsutomu Hata said the reduction was intended to supplement earlier fiscal stimuli, including a planned increase in spending for public works.
Before the reduction, the Japanese Government had included measures in its 1992 budget aimed at offsetting an accelerating slowdown in the economy, Mr. Hata said in a prepared statement.
 
Seen Circumventing Criticism
 Currency traders said the timing of the reduction was probably intended to circumvent American criticism of relatively tight Japanese monetary policy before President Bush arrives in Japan on Jan. 7. It is hoped that these measures, combined with this third reduction in the discount rate in this year's second half, will benefit every sector of the economy and help build public confidence, Mr. Hata said.
The discount rate peaked at 6 percent in 1989. The first reduction after five consecutive increases came on July 1 of this year, when the rate was cut to 5.5 percent. On Nov. 14, it was reduced to 5 percent.
In a separate statement, the Bank of Japan said the rate reduction was made in light of recent economic fundamentals and financial market movements. "It is the judgment of the bank that this action will provide a sufficient monetary condition for achieving more balanced non-inflationary growth," it said.
 
Effort to Aid Planning
 The central bank said the timing of the reduction took into consideration the fact that once the new year begins many Japanese businesses will be preparing business plans for the 1992 fiscal year, which begins April 1. Many Japanese businesses are on holiday through the end of this week.
The bank said it hoped the cut would help insure a smooth transition to a more balanced economy based on stable prices.
Currency traders said the move could temporarily bolster the dollar in a thin pre-holiday market, although the rate reduction had already been largely discounted by market participants.
Stock and bond traders said the credit easing would also benefit Japanese stocks and bonds, but they added that the move was already mostly factored into securities prices and that the duration of any positive movement might be short-lived.

HEADLINE: G.O.P. Strategy in Budget Battle In Albany Is Calculated Gamble

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has called the Republicans who run the State Senate obdurate, reckless and irresponsibly sluggish. "They're playing chicken with the state of New York in the car," he said.
But if this is a game of chicken, then the Democratic Governor has already balked, and more than once, offering several major concessions from the budget plan he first advanced weeks ago. 
 The Senate remains adamant in its position: no plan but its plan, which would force an unprecedented retrenchment in the state's social-welfare programs. And as the fight over how to close $5 billion in budget gaps goes into its seventh week, it seems increasingly clear that inherent in the Senators' stand is a very deliberate strategy that includes a very calculated gamble.
It is that they will not suffer the brunt of blame if the impasse winds up creating genuine turmoil -- and more specifically, if it leads Wall Street to make another cut in the state's credit rating, already the third-worst in the nation, ranking after Louisiana and Massachusetts.
A credit downgrading, which the state last received last year in the midst of another budget standoff, could add tens or hundreds of millions of dollars to the cost incurred by the state and its agencies in borrowing money on Wall Street. But even more ominously it could affect the borrowing ability of hundreds of cities, towns and school districts whose own credit ratings are tied to the state's.
"No one seems to realize yet that we are heading toward a crisis," said one top official in the Democratic-controlled Assembly, which in many ways is in the middle of the bitter fight between Mr. Cuomo and the Republicans. "For some of these places," said the official, who spoke on the condition of anonymity, "it would mean they'd be turned into Junk Bond City. It's the only way they could borrow money."
 
Putting Its Foot Down
 The Senate majority leader, Ralph J. Marino, insists that after years of compromising with the Democrats, this is the year his party will put its foot down. This is not so much a battle of small versus big spenders: what the Senate is really seeking to do is protect against cuts in local-assistance programs, especially school aid, which all amount to a politically potent form of property-tax relief in the suburban areas from which the Republicans derive their strength.
If Mr. Marino gets what he is holding out for, New York will have taken actions that the Democrats and advocates for the poor describe as horrific and that would probably have seemed unimaginable to both major parties a few years ago: cuts of 20 percent in some grants to the unemployed, a moratorium on any new construction of group homes for the mentally ill, elimination of the state emergency-assistance program for the homeless.
But what if Mr. Marino does not get what he wants? After all this arguing, the Governor and the legislative leaders have taken no joint action toward closing so much as a penny of either the $875 million gap they face in the current budget, or the potential $4 billion deficit in the budget for the fiscal year that begins on April 1. If the impasse continues without resolution, then there is an increasing danger that Wall Street will step in with a downgrading of the state's credit.
The credit rating agencies have come to act as sort of a baleful Greek chorus, commenting on Albany's inability to control the budget, severely out of balance for the third year in a row. A possible downgrading is the wild card in the proceedings now.
 
No Obvious Deadline
 In fact, one of the oddities in the situation is that there is no obvious deadline for a solution, other than a date that heads off action by the rating agencies. But no one knows exactly when this might come, and the two major agencies that assess the state's creditworthiness, Standard & Poor's and Moody's Investors Service, have declined to set any timetable, even though they have both warned of increasing concern about the lack of a solution.
No one here, of course, says they want to see the state's credit rating cut, although some Republican Senators have said they do not believe such action is imminent. But on Friday Mr. Cuomo said it was a threat that could strike "at any time."
But if the action does come, the major political question is who will be held responsible for it. The answer, which could affect next fall's elections and even alter the balance of power here, is not at all clear.
In many ways, the whole concept of a credit rating is arguably an abstraction to most voters, and it may be an even more abstract proposition for them to blame their local state lawmaker for the problem.
One Republican Senator, Nicholas A. Spano, of Yonkers, said earlier this month that even though his constituents said they were angry about the chaos in Albany, they were receptive when he explained to them what, in his view, the Republicans were trying to do. "They say: 'That's right, I don't want our school aid cut. I want you to take the cheats off of welfare.' "
Because Governor Cuomo is the person most in charge of things in Albany, he tends to be the person who gets blamed the most when things fall apart. But this year the Governor, who does not have to worry about re-election next fall, has strongly suggested that he may begin an all-out attack against the Republican state lawmakers, pinning the blame on them as they run to keep their seats, which are up every two years.
 
Parlor Guessing Game
 For all his eloquence, Mr. Cuomo has never turned the weight of his oratorical fury against the Senate Republicans at election time. Last year, as he concentrated on his own re-election, he offered a few pro forma pitches for an all-Democratic Legislature but did not campaign against any Republican incumbents.
The reason for Mr. Cuomo's apparent caution in taking on the Republicans has long been the object of a parlor guessing game in Albany, although he heatedly denies it exists at all. Some political analysts say he always believed the Republicans would keep control of the Senate anyway, making him reluctant to burn too many bridges with them, while others hypothesize that he actually likes having the Republican Senators around as a brake on the more forceful liberal wing of his own party.
 
Genuinely Outraged
 Now it may not matter because the Governor really does seem genuinely outraged by the Republicans, whom he has blamed, among other things, for keeping him out of the Presidential race by failing to cooperate with him on a budget deal.
And so New York may be treated next fall to the spectacle of a vengeful Governor Cuomo traveling to individual Senators' districts to implore people to throw them out of office.
It might work. On the other hand, it might backfire. Even when Mr. Cuomo did not campaign against them in 1990, the Republicans willingly brought him into the race anyway, telling voters they remained the only real bulwark against a Governor who was not exactly at the height of his popularity.
Many Republicans might well welcome a visit to their district from the Governor next fall, even a distinctly hostile one, since it would only help to raise their profile.

HEADLINE: MATTITUCK JOURNAL;
Despite Protests, the Golden Arches Are Coming

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MATTITUCK, L.I., Dec. 27

 BODY:
There are only three towns on Long Island without a fast-food restaurant. Soon there may be only two.
Over the strenuous opposition of residents, the Town of Southold has agreed to permit a McDonald's here. But the decision continues to generate debate, and the battle may not be over. 
 After McDonald's announced plans last year to build a restaurant on the main road, Route 25, the Zoning Board of Appeals held three public hearings. Speaker after speaker demanded that this historic town of 19,836 people, settled in 1640, remain fast-food free -- like nearby East Hampton and Shelter Island -- and they begged the board to deny McDonald's the needed permit.
"Are we brave enough to be different and be the only town in the world without a McDonald's?" said Agnes Dickinson of Southold.
If McDonald's came to town, many said, other fast-food restaurants would follow, and in a garish twinkling the Island's long ribbon of fast food strips, now pressing eastward from neighboring Riverhead, would roll over a narrow landscape of the North Shore that still looks in places like storybook New England.
"Other corporations wait for McDonald's to open the door," said Deborah A. Sidlauskas of Mattituck.
McDonald's countered with traffic studies that show roads could carry the extra traffic and with an architectural design the company says will make its restaurant fit in.
This month, in a 3-to-2 decision that seems to have pleased nobody, the zoning board voted to allow McDonald's to build on a three-acre parcel sandwiched between an insurance office and a gas station, and near an automotive shop, a concrete contractor, a dentist's office, a small medical center, a local newspaper office, and an Italian restaurant.
But the board declined to permit a drive-through window, the source of nearly half the profits for most McDonald's. The board chairman, Gerard P. Goehringer, said the zoning code allowed drive-in restaurants, a style of food service that has nearly vanished, but said nothing about drive-throughs.
McDonald's opponents called the decision less than half a loaf and predicted the company would sue the board or build first and apply for the drive-through window later.
"They will be back in two years asking for the drive-through if they can't get it now," said Linda Fletcher of New Suffolk, the vice president of the North Fork Environmental Council. "And this company stops at nothing. They are involved in lawsuits all over the country."
Paul M. Facella, a regional vice president for McDonald's Corporation in Valley Stream, L.I., said the company had not decided how to respond to the board's decision. But he said he was disappointed by the denial of the drive-through window.
"We showed we could work with the town to take a piece of land that's an eyesore and build a building that would improve the village ambience," he said.
Mr. Facella said the Mattituck restaurant would be a New England cape set deep in the lot, with cedar siding painted driftwood gray, and a gabled roof with four dog-housedormers. The restaurant's trademark sign would be rendered in wood and kept low.
When the fast-food foes said garbage from the Mattituck McDonald's would litter their lawns and stuff the town's garbage landfill, McDonald's offered to cart the garbage out of town.
The company also estimated the restaurant would mean 75 new jobs, a $500,000 annual payroll in a hard economic time, and $9,000 in property taxes for the town.
Opponents remain unswayed. "These are low-paying jobs for a small number of people," said Ms. Fletcher. "The big money does not stay in the community. It goes back to McDonald's corporate headquarters." Others said damage to tourism would outweigh any tax money.
McDonald's has questioned whether the vocal opponents reflect the views of most town residents. But in the zoning board's offices, hundreds of letters and petitions in three thick folders suggest community opinion is running heavily against fast food.
"I feel like I'm Rambo up here taking on a whole division," said Frank Carlin of Laurel, a pro-McDonald's speaker at one of the hearings.
Mr. Carlin said he counted four pizza parlors, two ice cream shops, seven delicatessens and three 7-Elevens in the town. "So what's wrong with having one McDonald's?" he asked.
Another supporter said, "When you go to McDonald's at least you know what you are getting."
"Yeah, a heart attack," a woman in the audience replied.

HEADLINE: Profitably Policing Workers' Comp

BYLINE: By PETER KERR

 BODY:
While skyrocketing costs endanger workers' compensation insurance in many states, the SAIF Corporation of Salem, Ore., is a rare bird in the wilderness: a failing state-owned insurer that suddenly stopped its fall and rose to become a striking business success.
Three years after it was losing $1 million a week, the company reports that it expects to end 1991 more than $45 million in the black. SAIF has reduced premiums to the company's 37,000 business clients by 23 percent in the last two years while doubling its payments to legitimately injured workers. And in addition to rate reductions, it has refunded $37 million to policyholders. 
 But even more striking in the $60 billion workers' compensation field -- which private insurers in many other states are abandoning as unprofitable -- is that SAIF executives largely attribute the turnaround to a crackdown on fraud and abuse.
In the process, SAIF's president and chief executive, Stan Long, has riled lawyers, providers of medical services and leaders of the insurance industry, who say he has unfairly exaggerated the cost of fraud and the failings of the insurance industry.
Although experts have in the past generally attributed the rising cost of workers compensation to medical costs, litigation and expanding benefits, a small but growing group of government officials and insurance executives across the country now say that exaggeration of claims and outright fraud are costing billions of dollars a year.
Executives of SAIF, a mutual insurance company that competes with privately owned carriers for workers' compensation business in Oregon, say their experience shows that in many states where workers' compensation is in crisis, part of the blame lies with insurers who refuse to look insurance cheats straight in the eye.
"Most companies come away from a case involving fraud saying, 'We just dodged another bullet,' " said Brian M. Steffel, senior vice president and claims officer of SAIF, which stands for State Accident Insurance Fund. "They are dodging their obligation to fight fraud."
But the company has riled lawyers, chiropractors and private insurers who say SAIF has portrayed them unfairly. SAIF's critics charge that that the company has denied rightful payments to truly injured workers as it has tripled its rate of rejecting claims.
"You would be able to reduce your premiums, too, if you did not pay out claims to many, many workers who deserve it," said Evohl Malagon, an attorney in Eugene who represents workers.
Workers' compensation is a system of insurance that reimburses employees for the economic impact of injuries and illness caused on the job. In some states, the insurance is sold to employers by private companies, in others by public agencies, and in many states by both.
Workers are compensated for lost wages, medical bills, and in some states job retraining; in cases of death, benefits are paid to families. In most states, the law requires employers to insure all workers and pay the premiums. Patients covered by workers' compensation insurance pay no deductibles and no part of their medical bills.
 
SAIF's New Methods
 Over the last decade, the costs of the insurance programs in all 50 states have grown more than 150 percent -- or 50 percentage points more than the growth in health-care costs over all.
As regulators deny requests for double-digit premium rises, insurance companies in many states have often responded by saying they cannot continue to lose money, and have dropped large numbers of customers. In some places, notably Maine and Rhode Island, large companies have abandoned the entire states, forcing many employers to get coverage from state-run programs intended for high-risk customers.
In Oregon, where employers were struck with double-digit premium increases in the late 1980's, SAIF began to restructure its operations in 1989 to combat fraud. It assigned 55 employees to fraud investigations and established undercover and surveillance units that have since turned up fraud cases involving more than 200 workers and medical professionals. Cases have been handed to district attorneys and professional boards for action.
The company's lawyers, meanwhile, were trained and deputized by the state Attorney General's office. Among other measures, claims adjusters' tasks were simplified and their caseloads reduced, and a new unit was established to accept or reject the majority of claims in less than 14 days. The company says it has found that one of every four claims involve some sort of fraud.
SAIF says it is aiming its antifraud activities at the small number of cases responsible for disproportionate costs. It is also helping client companies to set up safety and back-to-work programs.
 
Fewer Premiums, More Profits
 After the crackdown began, the percentage of disability claims rejected by SAIF rose to more than 37 percent, from 17 percent, and the number of claims filed has dropped 19 percent. Of the claims denied, 90 percent have been upheld on appeal. Although premiums paid to the company dropped by more than $40 million in 1990, SAIF's net income reached $46 million that year, compared with a $10 million loss the year before.
At the same time, the Oregon Legislature also enacted a number of changes, increasing medical cost-control programs, tightening the rules on compensation and reducing the number of hearings permitted with lawyers.
The director of the state Department of Insurance and Finance, Gary Weeks, said the legislative reforms helped drive down costs and increase safety. For example, he said, the safety reforms have cut the number of deaths on the job from accidents in Oregon by half.
The changes and SAIF's aggressive new methods have been praised by some labor leaders and by customers like the Schuler Corporation of Salem, a 300-employee maker of wooden cabinets and vanities with $30 million in sales. Schuler's premiums have dropped to $7,000, from $275,000 in 1987.
 
The Controversial Mr. Long
 Perhaps the most controversial element in SAIF's success story is Mr. Long himself, the company's 60-year-old president and chief executive, who faults the entire insurance industry for not challenging dubious claims. As long as insurers can pass costs to the public through higher premiums, Mr. Long said, insurers have no incentive to seek out fraud or abuse.
"If you decide that you are going to overlook fraud and get paid to overlook fraud, you, too, can be a successful insurance executive," Mr. Long said.
But critics of Mr. Long say SAIF has denied far more than fraudulent and abusive claims and has taken credit for changes instituted by the Legislature that have reduced premiums for all companies in the state. Mr. Long, they charge, has exaggerated the company's past problems and has unfairly dropped smaller and unprofitable customers.
In response to complaints, the Department of Insurance and Finance is reviewing SAIF's increased claims-rejection rate to determine whether the criticism is justified.
 
'He Is Being Unfair'
 Perhaps most disturbed by the statements of SAIF executives are leaders of rival insurance companies in Oregon.
"When Stan Long says one out of four claims involved some kind of fraud, he is being unfair to the people of this state," said Tony Feronator, a senior vice president with the Liberty Northwest Insurance Corporation, the second-largest workers' compensation insurance carrier in the state and a subsidiary of the Liberty Mutual insurance company of Boston.
"There is concern here about whether what they are doing is appropriate," Mr. Feronator said. "The bottom line in workers' compensation is not just bringing down claims costs."

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Radio Deal Would Link Drive-Time Rivals

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
TOGETHER, for the first time, Howard Stern and Don Imus.
Each weekday morning, these two radio stars battle for a share of the morning "drive time" market, along the way sometimes heaping abuse on each other. Mr. Stern ranked second in the market, with a 7.0 rating on WXRK-FM, in last summer's Arbitron ratings for morning drive time, the latest figures available. Mr. Imus ranked seventh, with a 4.0 rating, on WFAN-AM. Overall, WXRK tied for 10th in the New York market, at 3.8; WFAN ranked 15th, with a 3.3 rating. 
 Now the Infinity Broadcasting Corporation, which owns WXRK-FM, is planning to buy WFAN-AM, the home of Mr. Imus, for about $70 million from the Emmis Broadcasting Company of Indianapolis. The deal appears set to go through, assuming the investing public is willing to finance the acquisition by purchasing stock in Infinity, which wants to go public only three years after a leveraged buyout took the company private and left it saddled with debt.
Infinity officials declined to be interviewed last week, but the deal has generated some excitement in the radio industry.
"There is a tremendous synergy on the morning shows," said J. T. Atherton, managing editor of Radio Business Report, an industry publication. "You put them together and that will be one hot item to sell" to advertisers. Besides putting the two morning stars into one advertising package, there is speculation that Infinity intends to export Mr. Imus's program to some of its other stations, as it already does with Mr. Stern's.
Infinity owns 17 radio stations in 12 markets, including stations in Philadelphia and Washington that carry Mr. Stern's show, and it has done better than many broadcasters in the current recession, with revenues rising at a slow rate, and losses diminishing. Investors in Infinity will no doubt be hoping that under the company's ownership, the two New York stations -- WXRK with Mr. Stern and WFAN with Mr. Imus -- will show improved results, for the price being asked in the offering is not a cheap one.
The company plans to go public by selling six million shares at a price of $16 to $18 a share.
In this deal, the company's name seems appropriate, because any price is an infinite multiple of its nonexistent profits. The company reported a loss of $39.6 million in 1990, and $24.1 million in the first nine months of this year, not including an extraordinary gain it recorded from buying back bonds at a discount.
In the world of broadcasting, of course, profits under generally accepted accounting principles are often not deemed to be an achievable goal, and managements urge investors to ignore the depreciation of intangible assets. What counts, they say, is cash flow.
The proposed public offering values the company at 11.7 to 12.3 times its 1990 cash flow, incorporating WFAN's results. That multiple evidently will be somewhat lower for 1991, because Infinity's results improved slightly in the first nine months of this year, while WFAN's moved up significantly.
Infinity obviously thinks there is plenty of room for improvement in operations at WFAN, which carries Mr. Imus weekdays from 5:30 to 10 A.M., and all-sports programming the rest of the time: the games of the New York Mets, Knicks and Rangers, and a host of sports talk shows.
The figures included in the prospectus for the offering indicate that WFAN had cash flow -- before interest expenses and before management overhead -- of $4.6 million on revenues of $21.4 million in 1990. At $70 million, Infinity is paying 15 times that cash flow.
In fact, WFAN's results are improving. For the first nine months of 1991 it reported cash flow of $6.4 million on revenues of $20.7 million. Because the prospectus does not give comparable results, and because the fourth quarter is not an especially strong one for WFAN -- with baseball over and the two local professional football teams on other stations -- it is not possible to estimate the multiple being paid based on 1991 cash flow.
By the standards of a few years ago, when deals relied on rapidly advancing ad revenues or soaring prices of radio stations, this deal is conservative. But its success still seems to be dependent on capital markets remaining friendly.
The pro forma financial results indicate that had this deal been done at the beginning of 1991, and the bonds repurchased then, Infinity would have had operating cash flow of $9.8 million, for the first nine months, even after paying its interest expenses. If the fourth quarter is that good, that would leave $13 million of cash flow available for the year, compared with a margin of only about $3 million in all of 1990. All figures ignore capital spending, which is running at better than $1 million a year.
Unfortunately, $13 million will not be good enough in 1992, because the company is scheduled to repay $16.9 million of long-term debt that year, a figure that rises gradually each year to $21.5 million in 1994, and then zooms to $252.9 million in 1995, when Infinity will have to refinance its operations.
One aid to 1992 cash flow may be the proceeds from the sale of Infinity's current AM station in New York, WZRC. After converting from a Spanish-language format in 1990, that station now plays rock music, but it does not have enough listeners to be included in the Arbitron ratings. Under Federal Communications Commission rules, Infinity will have to sell WZRC after it completes the acquisition of WFAN. Infinity has asked for a one-year waiver in order to avoid an abrupt sale.
It may be encouraging to prospective buyers of Infinity shares that insiders at the company are not taking out cash from the offering. In fact, they put cash into the company at the end of September, enabling the company to repurchase bonds at a discount.
But those insiders, who include management and Lehman Brothers, which is underwriting the new offering, did not pay retail. Their price was about $12 a share, or a discount of 25 to 33 percent from the price being asked of the public investors.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Flood of Corporate Debt in the Offing

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
If the declines hold, investment bankers and economists say, the quarter-point drop in both short- and long-term interest rates since mid-December is likely to bring a torrent of corporate debt issues in the first weeks of the new year.
The holidays have prevented most companies from moving faster. As the end of the year approaches, traditional buyers retreat to the sidelines, and many of the investment bankers who market the debt are on vacation. 
 "There is no one around to buy them or sell them, and investment committees are not going to meet this time of year," said a corporate finance specialist at a large New York investment bank. "But the declines in rates we have seen over the last two weeks are very significant and make billions of bonds callable. The question is if companies will do it quickly."
 
Way to Aid Balance Sheets
 Large companies accustomed to tapping the credit markets are likely to be quickest to respond to the drop in rates. But analysts and executives say corporations of all sizes could help their balance sheets by issuing new securities.
"This last downward move in rates was sufficient to put in place the economies needed for replacement financing for virtually all corporations," said Lindley B. Richert, who heads MCM Inc., an investment advisory firm in New York, "and it opens the door for tenders and outright net redemptions without replacement financing."
The securities have not yet been brought to market, but the list of corporations that have filed so-called shelf registrations with the Securities and Exchange Commission is growing.
Among those who may offer new debt are companies that investment bankers say are rarely in the debt markets, like the Polaroid Corporation and the Phelps Dodge Corporation.
In addition to the filings, a handful of companies placed calls over the last week on outstanding debt securities carrying interest rates that until recently would have seemed reasonable.
This group includes PSI Energy Inc., which on Friday said it was redeeming all of its outstanding 8 7/8 percent bonds due on Oct. 1, 2008. The company said that it would refinance the issue through secured medium-term notes and that it anticipated savings of more than $10.5 million.
 
Record Volume in 1991
 On Dec. 20, the day the Federal Reserve Board surprised and delighted the financial markets by slashing its discount rate by a full percentage point, to 3.5 percent, Southern New England Telephone said it intended to redeem $80 million worth of 9 5/8 percent debentures due on Dec. 1, 2010. The announcement came a week after the company sold $80 million worth of 8.70 percent 40-year notes through an underwriting group led by Salomon Brothers.
A new surge in corporate debt issuance in early 1992 would come on top of record volume in the year just ending, a period when short- to intermediate-term interest rates have fallen sharply and long-term rates have inched lower.
Merrill Lynch's corporate bond index shows that yields on AAA- and AA-rated bonds with maturities of one to 10 years have fallen to 7.16 percent over the last year, from a 52-week high of 8.96 percent. As of Thursday, yields on A-rated bonds of similar maturity stood at 7.77 percent according to Merrill's index, down from a high of 10.01 percent.
Given those declines, it is little wonder that new debt issuance has skyrocketed.
Through Dec. 26, more than $133 billion worth of new investment-grade corporate bonds had been priced in 1991, according to data from Moody's Investors Service, the bond-rating agency, easily eclipsing the $110 billion worth of debt offered in 1986.
 
Helping to Lower Expenses
 The money raised has helped to lower companies' expenses. "This will be the first year since 1983 during which total corporate interest expense will decline," said John Lonski, a senior economist at Moody's. "It may be more than coincidence that 1984 was the last year during which Moody's upgraded more corporate ratings than it downgraded."
Daniel Scotto, a senior vice president and director of high-grade credit research at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, said that declines in interest rates over the last year had allowed many companies to reduce what he called their "imbedded cost of interest," their total interest expenses, by a full percentage point.
 
'Could Be a Tough Sell'
 "That is pretty significant," Mr. Scotto added. "I can't remember a year when companies were able to do as much" to pare interest costs.
With most analysts forecasting that the economy will remain weak through the first half of 1992 and that the Fed is unlikely to show any inclination to tighten monetary policy and push up rates until a recovery is fully under way, the chances seem good that corporations will have additional opportunities to reduce their interest expenses.
But Mr. Richert and others questioned whether sufficient demand would surface for securities carrying much lower coupons than those they replace.
"Potential buyers might ask why they would want a security with a 5 percent or 6 percent coupon, instead of the 8 percent or 9 percent they have now," he said. "Potentially, it could be a tough sell."
At least some money managers disagreed.
"Yields on AAA corporate bonds are still 50 basis points to 75 basis points over Treasuries, which looks pretty attractive," said Marshall B. Front, president of Stein, Roe & Farnham Inc. Investment Counsel, managers of about $15 billion worth of fixed-income securities. "That means that even at these levels, we will be buying some corporates, as well as some Treasuries."
With bond markets around the world effectively on holiday through Wednesday, the chances seem slim that a burst of new corporate debt will be seen this week. But investment bankers said they expected the pace to quicken once work resumed in earnest around Jan. 6.

HEADLINE: Japan May Buy More U.S. Parts

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: TOKYO, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Japan's five leading automobile manufacturers plan further increases in their purchases of American parts, an economic newspaper, Nihon Keizai, reported today.
The newspaper said the companies would announce the specifics of their plan during President Bush's visit Jan. 7-10. 
 Under Secretary of State Robert Zoellick told Prime Minister Kiichi Miyazawa in Tokyo last week that trade in automobiles and parts would be a main topic of discussion during Mr. Bush's visit.
Without citing its sources, Nihon Keizai said Toyota, Nissan, Mazda, Mitsubishi and Honda would announce parts purchases from the United States of 30 to 40 percent more than they agreed to in November.
The companies' offices were closed today. The newspaper said the Ministry of International Trade and Industry is demanding that the auto makers increase purchases of American parts by 50 percent.
The plans announced by the five auto makers last month had called for raising purchases of American-made auto parts to $16.5 billion a year by fiscal 1994, which ends March 31, 1995. Of that, $2.5 billion worth of parts were to be used in Japan and the rest at the Japanese companies' plants in the United States.

HEADLINE: Business Optimism Off

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Business confidence declined in December, according to a monthly poll by the United States Chamber of Commerce.
The chamber said its business confidence index slumped to 39.3 in December, from 45.4 in October and 55.9 in August. 
 In the poll of 8,044 business people, 45.3 percent said they expected the economy to shrink in the next six months, while just 17.6 percent said they expected the economy to expand. Another 37.1 percent saw no change.
The December responses were substantially more pessimistic than those in October, when 32.3 percent said the economy was likely to shrink in the next six months, and 23.6 percent saw it expanding.
In the August poll, just 25.9 percent expected the economy to contract, while another 25.9 percent predicted the nation's output of goods and services would grow.
In this month's poll, the outlook was also bearish for sales and employment.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
News Events Become Biggest Television Hits

BYLINE: By BILL CARTER

 BODY:
In 1991, no situation comedy, ensemble drama, mini-series, movie or documentary dominated American television.
Nor was the biggest thing on television this year a for-sale sign, despite incessant rumors that one or two of the networks -- specifically NBC and CBS -- would be put up for sale. 
 Instead, this was a year when the biggest thing on television was the stuff of real life, world and national events that appeared on television only because television showed up to cover them: the Persian Gulf war, the attempted Soviet coup, the Supreme Court confirmation hearings of Clarence Thomas, the William Kennedy Smith trial, the resignation of Mikhail S. Gorbachev and the dissolution of the Soviet Union.
 
Not From Hollywood
 None of these events was a "television program" at all; they were not anything that anyone at a network or a Hollywood studio planned, wrote or produced.
The dominance of non-entertainment on television, several industry executives said, was at least as indicative of the changes taking place in the industry as a turnover in network ownership would have been.
"When you walked up the hall this year, the things people were talking about, the things people were watching, were the war, the hearings, the trial," said Marshall Cohen, executive vice president of research for MTV Networks.
"Nothing was made in entertainment that got people talking like that," he said. "Nothing was watched in high enough numbers to get people talking."
Part of the reason for that result, Mr. Cohen conceded, is that people now watch television in so many different ways, because of the many options they have, that it is much harder for any piece of entertainment to cross over all the divided tastes watching separate channels.
But, he added, "it's mainly a function of nothing being good enough."
What is happening to television can best be defined by broadening the category of what had impact on television this year, said David F. Poltrack, senior vice president of research for CBS. What people watched in 1991, he said, was information.
He said all forms of television connected to information enjoyed growing success, from "The Oprah Winfrey Show" to "reality shows" like NBC's "Unsolved Mysteries" and CBS's "Rescue 911," to the Cable News Network and C-SPAN.
The trend toward informational programming was predictable, Mr. Poltrack said, and reflected the changing makeup of the television audience. "As the American population ages, informational programming is what they want to see," he continued. "When people get to their late 30's or early 40's, they become more interested in news and information."
What has been happening in recent years to the television audience, he added, is that more members of the baby-boom generation, by far the largest segment of the American population, have reached this chronological turning point.
The networks have found a way to take advantage of this shift by packaging news in entertainment formats. News magazine programs, like "60 Minutes" and "20/20," had higher ratings this year than the year before.
The problem for the networks is that they continue to spend hundreds of millions of dollars on entertainment programs that inevitably suffer a falloff in popularity.
At the same time, the networks must continue to cover the kind of news that the mass audience they seek finds compelling -- even though these events are often prohibitively expensive to cover.
NBC for example, has estimated that its losses on the gulf war alone totaled about $45 million as a result of canceled advertising and the costs of news coverage.
Still, Robert C. Wright, president of NBC, sees a reason for the networks to embrace the 1991 trends toward informational television.
"Network television still has the advantage of having 100 percent coverage of the country," Mr. Wright said. "CNN doesn't have that. That still makes the difference between ourselves and cable really meaningful. We just haven't yet figured out how to arrange the economics."
Mr. Wright added: "It used to be that at the beginning of the year you would budget five, six, ten million dollars for crisis coverage. Everybody in the company would hope the year would go by and nothing would happen so we wouldn't have to spend that money. Now all of a sudden, we look for those moments to happen."
Mr. Wright acknowledged there was some incongruity between this high interest in covering news and the painful cutbacks at the network news divisions. "We've underestimated the excitement that can surround these events," he said, "and we've held back our efforts to enhance that excitement. That's something we have to change."
 
Financing News Coverage
 The other thing that has to change, he and Mr. Poltrack said, is the method of financing news coverage.
"We have to educate the advertisers," Mr. Poltrack said. Almost none of the network coverage of the gulf war included advertising, because of skittishness about potentially graphic war scenes. "I think some of the advertisers now realize they were foolish," he added.
Mr. Wright and Mr. Poltrack agree that the main way such a change is likely to take place is through shifting more and more regular news programs to prime time. Instead of interrupting entertainment programs to cover news, the networks are likely to try to steer their coverage into some established news program.
"I expect we'll see at least one network news program in prime time every night of the week," Mr Poltrack said. "'We're almost there now. As the population continues to age, I'm sure we're going to see more."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Television this year was dominated by news events, from Mikhail S. Gorbachev's downfall to Anita F. Hill's dramatic testimony against Clarence Thomas before his Supreme Court confirmation.
