HEADLINE: Arts Groups, Too, Struggle With Recession

BYLINE: By WILLIAM H. HONAN

 BODY:
As the recession tightens its grip, arts organizations across the country have found themselves jilted by the Government and by corporate and private patrons on which they had come to depend.
Consequently, productions are being canceled, exhibition hours reduced and staffs cut to the bone. A few companies -- among them the six-year-old Los Angeles Theater Center, a $6 million, four-theater operation -- have gone out of business. An even more prominent institution, the Detroit Institute of Arts, has threatened to close if the state of Michigan imposes any more substantial cuts beyond the 41 percent reduction it has already made in the subsidy that provides most of the museum's operating funds. 
 In the scramble for survival, many nonprofit cultural groups are shedding their old ways of doing business and adopting new practices -- some welcome, others not. Interviews with artists, arts managers and government officials across the country indicate that the financial squeeze has forced cultural organizations to become better managed and far more sophisticated in dealing with their audiences. But they have also become more conservative in their choice of programs and artists.
This process has given rise to a new cadre of influential arts consultants, hired by nonprofit groups to help them raise money and manage their organizations in these difficult times. Much like media consultants in politics, these arts consultants, like Arts Market Consulting Inc. of Marion, Mass., and the Kreisberg Group Ltd. of New York City, have become a new center of influence, wielding wide power behind the scenes in shaping the direction arts groups take all across the country.
"The day of the impresario flying around with a cape and buying a dozen camels for 'Aida' is over," said Arnold N. Breman, executive and artistic director of the Kravis Center for the Performing Arts in West Palm Beach, Fla. "Today, that guy says, 'Gee, camels cost $4,000 apiece. Let's do it with lighting.' "
 
More Safety, Less Stretching
 One way to pinch pennies is to play it safe. That approach makes life tougher than ever for newcomers and curtails the presentation of avant-garde or "difficult" work.
"You can still do the Mendelssohn Violin Concerto, but God forbid you do the Alban Berg," said David Levenson, managing director of the Brooklyn Center for the Performing Arts at Brooklyn College. "I have to stretch my audience less than I'd like. Instead of two avant-garde dance groups, I present one."
Mr. Breman said: "Can you see Sol Hurok presenting 'A Great Night for the Irish'? If he were alive today, it could happen."
Patrick Hinson, director of SoHo Booking, a small New York agency that handles eight avant-garde dancers, said: "One presenter told me, 'Personally, I liked watching your dancer crawl across the floor in a rug, but I can't sell it today.' "
Symphonic soloists in the middle price range, who earn $6,000 to $15,000 a performance, are especially vulnerable to cutbacks. "Instead of paying $15,000 for a violin soloist, whose name may not mean that much outside of New York," Mr. Levenson said, "presenters say, 'why not let our own concert master do it?' "
The most dramatic example of Government cutbacks happened in Michigan, where Gov. John Engler, a conservative Republican who says he believes that the state has no business supporting the arts and is also motivated by the need to close a $1 billion gap in the state budget, abolished the state arts council in July. Governor Engler later re-established the council as a subdivision of the Department of Commerce, but with its budget cut by more than half. Several small theater groups and a dance organization were immediate casualties.
The Detroit Institute of Arts has responded to severe budget cuts by reducing its staff from 340 to 190 in the last six months. Samuel Sachs 2d, director of the museum, said last week that he and his staff had developed a contingency plan to lend pieces from the museum's permanent collection to the National Gallery in Washington, the Metropolitan Museum of Art in New York and other museums capable of taking care of it in case Governor Engler carries out a promise to "zero fund" all state contributions to the arts and the museum is forced to close.
Arts councils are also under fire in several other states, including Missouri, where the chairman of the state's House budget committee recently announced that he would soon propose legislation to eliminate the State Arts Council, whose annual budget is $5 million.
Collectively, state arts agencies reduced their contributions to individual artists and cultural organizations from $273 million last year to $214 this year, according to a study by the National Asssembly of State Arts Agencies.
Corporations and private patrons are not making up the loss, said Joanne Hayes, president of the New York City-based American Association of Fund Raising Counsel Inc., the professional association of fund-raising consultants, because many arts organizations well schooled in applying for Government grants have not yet mastered the techniques for approaching the private sector. "It isn't like switching the style of your jacket," Ms. Hayes said. "It takes years to cultivate private patrons."
The impact of these and related losses, coupled with the dampening effect of the weak economy on ticket sales, is felt throughout the arts.
At Lincoln Center, "things are sluggish," said William W. Lockwood Jr., executive producer of programming. He added that he was apprehensive about ticket sales in 1992.
The Theater Development Fund, which maintains the discount TKTS booths in Manhattan and Brooklyn, reports having sold 8 percent fewer tickets to Off Broadway shows from July through November than in the same period last year, said Robert Callely, director of operations.
Ingrid Kidd, president of the National Association of Performing Arts Managers and Agents and president of Mainstage Management International Inc., a medium-sized theatrical agency based in Los Angeles that books family-oriented entertainment throughout the country, said an unusual number of presenters had recently canceled appearances when scheduled touring companies were virtually at their doors.
"Ordinarily," she said, "you get one or two last-minute cancellations every year because somebody forgot to deliver the advertisement to the local newspaper, but this year we've had 18 cancellations by seasoned presenters who know what they're doing. They just weren't able to sell the tickets."
 
Advice From Outside
 Many arts groups have all but put their fate in the hands of outside consultants. A year ago, the Indianapolis Opera was struggling with a $150,000 debt, subscription sales had been declining for four years, there were always plenty of empty seats in the house, and board members were wondering whether the company should expand, contract or even call it quits. It hired Arts Market Consulting, the Massachusetts group, for $30,000.
"As of today," said Durand Pope, general manager of the opera company, "we are totally out of debt, all productions are sellouts and subscriptions are up by 45 percent over last season."
Old favorites like "Brigadoon" were not the answer. The company has remained faithful to the standard repertory, this season producing "Porgy and Bess," "Madama Butterfly," "Cosi Fan Tutte" and, next month, "Lucia di Lammermoor" starring Lisa Saffer, who scored a triumph in "Die Soldaten" this season at the New York City Opera.
The consultants "helped us analyze what we were doing wrong," Mr. Pope said. "We learned, for example, that our audiences had what we joked about as 'fear of opera.' They were afraid someone might ask them in the intermission what they thought, and they wouldn't know what to say. So we began to educate our audiences with our newsletter, among other things. Also, we learned we weren't doing well with young singles, so we started Opera Lite, which is an event for singles at which we serve hors d'oeuvres and have a cash bar. A bunch of men from the chorus put on skits explaining surtitles, how you dress for the opera and opera plots. We're demystifying opera."
Louise Stevens, president of Arts Market Consulting, said her company's business had increased by about 20 percent this year. A former music critic for The Chicago Daily News and The Milwaukee Journal as well as a former arts manager, Mrs. Stevens works with a small staff of 4 to 12 people, depending on the assignment. Her specialty, she said, is strategic planning and market research, tasks that entail setting goals for performing-arts groups and locating potential subscribers and supporters within a community.
Another arts consultant, Louisa Kreisberg, a former director of publicity of the Museum of Modern Art who is now president of the Kreisberg Group Ltd., said that just as her company played a role in most of the major museum expansions of the late 1980's, her guidance was being sought in what she called "the payback decade."
The Kreisberg Group offers services ranging from advice on a per-diem basis to planning two-year campaigns. It becomes involved with membership, development, strategic planning, advertising and public relations. "I think of myself as an institutional strategist," Ms. Kreisberg said.
 
Learning New Strategies
 Some Government arts agencies are trying to help their clients seek new sources of income and to provide services as well as grant money.
"Many cultural organizations don't realize that the African-American community is highly philanthropic, greatly disproportionate to their income, in the fields of religion and education," said Luis R. Cancel, New York City's new Commissioner of Cultural Affairs. "We'd like to help arts organizations tap into that community, as well as to the Latino, Asian and other ethnic communities."
Adolfo V. Nodal, general manager of the Los Angeles Department of Cultural Affairs, said his agency was starting a development division to help arts groups raise money and was beginning to offer technical assistance as well. "We're focusing beyond the money thing, since that's now limited," he said.
Some arts groups, while plainly suffering from the recession, are also finding ways to take advantage of it. "Because of the weak real-estate market in New York, we were able to renegotiate the loan on our East 19th Street space to save about $20,000 a year," said Norton Owen, development director of the Limon Dance Company, "and now we're actually talking with other dance companies about jointly buying a building at a bargain price in this market."
The importance of good management, and the perils of its opposite, have also become manifest in recent months. The Los Angeles Theater Center collapsed in early autumn not only because of the recession, but also because of poor management, Mr. Nodal said. "We put $24 million into them over six years," he said, "but they were always in the newspaper going from one crisis to another, and people lost confidence in them."
In contrast, the Trinity Repertory Company of Providence, R.I., executed a remarkable turnabout this year. "Our subscription sales are now 70 percent ahead of last year, and that's despite the fact that Rhode Island is one of the most economically depressed states in the country," said Lynn Kelly, director of public relations for the company. "Two years ago, subscriptions had fallen to 5,500, and that's dangerously low for us. Now they're up to 9,400."
The explanation, she said, is careful management of limited resources; a new artistic director, Richard Jenkins, who shifted from the heavily avant-garde schedule of his predecessor, Anne Bogart, to a relatively mainstream choice of program, and effective promotion of the idea that subscription tickets offer patrons an inexpensive night out.
"It isn't that we're just doing light comedies," Ms. Kelly said, " 'Glass Menagerie' was a sellout, and we plan to close the season with a new play."
"We still have our problems," she continued. "We've got a half-million-dollar debt to deal with, but we have a new chairman of the board, and he's very much into exploring new sources of income. I guess you could say we're being creative."

HEADLINE: SIDELINES: POLITICAL FOOTBALLS;
Jersey Sports Authority Passing Hat

BYLINE: By William N. Wallace

 BODY:
Can Rutgers use a larger football stadium? Is more better?
Those are among the issues to be presented at public hearings next Monday in Trenton before the appropriations committee of the New Jersey State Senate. 
 The state's Sports and Exposition Authority is seeking the approval of a $416 million bond issue that would refinance $223 million of oustanding bonds at lower interest rates, build a convention center in Atlantic City and add 35,000 seats to Rutgers Stadium in Piscataway, N.J., which now has 23,000.
The projection is that once the Big East Conference gets going with a round-robin football schedule in 1993, the Scarlet Knights will fill those seats and need more. Games at Giants Stadium in East Rutherford, N.J., where Rutgers has never drawn well, would be abandoned.
Speaking of Giants Stadium, the Sports Authority is negotiating to borrow $30 million from United Jersey Bank to purchase the 76 suites on the mezzanine level of the stadium. The authority has an option, at $25 million, to purchase those suites from a Connecticut company, Skybox Partners Limited.
The MARA family provided the financing for the suites when the stadium was built 15 years ago and subsequently sold the leases. Rentals of the suites generate about $1.3 million a year according to JOHN SAMERJAN, the director of public relations for the Sports Authority.
This may not be a propitious time to seek financing for fun and games. But the Sports Authority, said BOB MULCAHY, its president, will count on its record as a generator of revenue to convince the legislators.
According to Mulcahy it provides a billion dollars in economic activity annually plus thousands of jobs and has generated a $60 million surplus for the state.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: PRESS;
Amid Dark Clouds of Gloom, Newspapers See Some Hope

BYLINE: By Alex S. Jones

 BODY:
FOR newspaper executives, this year has been a subject for black humor.
When several executives were asked if they could discern any good news for the newspaper business amid the current gloom, the initial response of virtually all was derisive laughter, ranging from a mordant chuckle to prolonged, acid-tinged guffaws. 
 At the end of a year that the newspaper industry considers to be the worst in half a century, positive thinking takes some effort.
"I think the worst is behind us," said Cathleen Black, president of the American Newspaper Publishers Association. That sentiment was echoed by many newspaper executives with as much hope as conviction.
But though the newspaper industry is united in grappling with a steep decline in advertising during 1991, there is no unanimity about why it happened, and what will happen when the overall economy recovers.
The majority view seems to be that the drop in newspaper advertising is partly cyclical, reflecting the downturn in the overall economy, and partly structural, reflecting a fundamental change in the advertising business in which newspapers have been the losers. These structural changes include growing competition from rivals like cable television and advertising cutbacks by big department stores because of the consolidation in the retailing industry.
Kenneth T. Berents, a media analyst at Alex. Brown & Sons Inc., a Baltimore stock brokerage, said structural problems unrelated to the recession accounted for about half of the drop in advertising.
Other newspaper executives agree that such noncyclical elements are important. But many consider structural changes to be about a third of the overall problem.
The more structural the change, the more difficult it will be for the newspaper industry to recover the lost advertising and the more resistance there will be to higher ad rates.
The good news for those who see a significant structural problem is that newspapers are moving rapidly to broaden their business on the assumption that the core daily newspaper will no longer be able to provide the profits of years past.
"The severity of the situation is making newspapers look very hard at other ways of generating revenues," Mr. Berents said. He pointed to such things as newspapers' setting up delivery systems to compete with the Postal Service and creating elaborate data bases to use in marketing.
A few maverick newspaper executives view the structural problem as a fantasy, and their view of the good news is quite different.
"I do not see evidence of structural change in our business," said W. Dean Singleton, chief executive officer of Media News Group Inc., which owns The Houston Post, The Denver Post and several other papers.
Mr. Singleton says the newspaper business is reeling because, for the first time in half a century, all three categories of newspaper advertising -- retail, national and classified -- are in decline. In previous recessions, newspapers fared reasonably well because, for instance, if help-wanted advertising was down because of an economic downturn, retailers would advertise more in order to attract wary shoppers.
Mr. Singleton argues that this recession is different, but not because of structural change.
Retailers -- especially big department stores -- have been forced to cut costs because of the huge debts created by the industry's consolidation, and the easiest cost they found to cut was advertising. But Mr. Singleton contends they still believe that newspapers are their best means of moving merchandise.
In the national advertising category, financial ads disappeared because of the 1987 stock market crash and airline ads dwindled after several airlines failed.
Classified advertising, especially in help-wanted and real estate, soared during the 1980's boom and have now fallen just as precipitously.
Mr. Singleton said the good news was that all three ad categories would one day return to newspapers. The retailers are gradually getting their financial houses in order, financial ads will reappear as the economy improves, the airlines industry will eventually stabilize, houses will start to sell again and employers will start to advertise jobs.
"What I can't tell you is when it comes back," Mr. Singleton said.
What passes as good news in the industry these days indicates just how glum the year has been.
James K. Batten, chairman of Knight-Ridder Inc., one of the nation's largest newspaper groups and owner of The Miami Herald and The Philadelphia Inquirer, has been especially concerned about the decline in newspaper readership over the last two decades.
Citing research done by Philip E. Meyer at the School of Journalism and Mass Communication at the University of North Carolina, Mr. Batten noted that about 73 percent of the population read a newspaper every day in 1967. Since then, the percentage dropped about one percentage point a year, to 50.6 percent in 1988.
Mr. Batten said the good news was that since 1988 newspaper readership had been rebounding, reaching 51.5 percent this year.
But searching for hopeful signs does little to change the fundamental realization within the industry that newspapers as a business property have lost a significant portion of their value in the last year or two.
Gerald D. Reilly, a newspaper broker based in Greenwich, Conn., said the typical 25,000-circulation daily in a good market would have sold for about $20 million in 1989. He estimates that same paper would bring $10 million to $13 million today, if a buyer could be found.
Mr. Reilly predicted that newspaper values would eventually rebound, though probably not to previous levels any time soon. He said he based his optimism in part on conversations with more than 50 newspaper executives after he was hired by the Gannett Company to try to find a buyer for The Arkansas Gazette before it was closed in October.
No one was willing to buy the paper, Mr. Riley said.
But he added, "Everyone I polled said, 'We are lean, trim, efficient and, when it turns around, the improvement will come straight down to the bottom line.' "

HEADLINE: THE YEAR IN BUSINESS: 1991;
Russell L. Ray: An Era Ends In Aviation

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS

 BODY:
Shortly after Russell L. Ray Jr. took over as president and chief executive of Pan American World Airways in September he acknowledged that reshaping the carrier into a smaller airline whose flights were mostly to Latin America would be a daunting task.
Yet the executive rolled up his sleeves and began to hold a series of huddles with Delta executives, who offered assistance; labor union leaders, and creditors, who began to offer some support. 
 But there were two key groups of people who did not pledge support: travel agents, who began to book away from Pan Am, and travelers, many of whom turned to other airlines.
And if that were not enough, the Delta executives later decided they had thrown enough money into the bleeding carrier and Pan Am was forced to shut down Dec. 4, putting 10,000 people out of work.
It was a tumultuous year for Pan Am. In January, its parent company, the Pan Am Corporation filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection. In July, Pan Am agreed to sell its European routes, planes, Frankfurt operations and the shuttle to Delta. That was the beginning of the end.
By the time Mr. Ray assumed control of the carrier little could be done to save it. And while he and most of the staff in New York had moved to a hangar at Miami International Airport to plan a new carrier. Instead they ended up organizing an orderly shutdown.
Pan Am, which was founded in 1927, was the third big United States airlines to stop flying in 1991. Eastern Airlines ceased flying in January and Midway Airlines closed in November.

HEADLINE: CHRONICLE

BYLINE: By NADINE BROZAN

 BODY:
Now that Christmas has passed, here is something to look forward to in the New Year: a film festival entitled "It Can't Happen Here: Films of the Great Depression." Thirty movies, some made during the Depression, others made years later, some familiar, some obscure, will be shown by the Film Society of Lincoln Center from Jan. 10 through Feb. 19 at the Walter Reade Theater, 165 West 65th Street. SAYRE MAXFIELD, program associate with the society, had the idea for the festival about a year ago when it was her turn to suggest books for a literary group to which she belongs. "I came up with two books of that era that couldn't have been more different from one another and that led to me think maybe there were different kinds of depression movies, comedies as well as serious ones."
She took the idea for a series to RICHARD PENA, program director, and they began collecting titles. "We came up with lots that I had never heard of," Ms. Maxfield said. "For example, 'Gabriel Over the White House,' which was produced by William Randolph Hearst. Walter Huston plays a president who declares martial law and takes over the Government in ways that Franklin Delano Roosevelt never dreamed of. He decides that the rest of the world must disarm, so he calls in all war debts and rules the world." 
 Ms. Maxfield said that although some of the films on the agenda were unquestionably depressing, she found reality worse. "What depressed me far more was watching newsreels about Herbert Hoover," she said. "In many of the movies there is a solution in the end.
"We wanted to start this series on Oct. 29, the day the stock market crashed 62 years ago, but the theater was not open then. As it turned out, as we get deeper and deeper into recession, I guess this is a good time to open."

HEADLINE: Editorial Notebook;
Europe, Fearful

BYLINE: By DIANE CAMPER

 BODY:
"I can't even count the number of conferences on immigration I've been to in the last few months," said an international consultant on migration issues. "There's a lot of talk about the subject, but nothing much is being done about it."
The speaker was one of about 45 people at a recent conference on "Immigration Policies in the Developed World," convened in England by the Ditchley Foundations, which have sponsored Anglo-American conferences for 30 years. The participants were Western officials, demographic experts, academics and journalists. They were encouraged to speak openly on the understanding that their remarks would not be attributed. 
 What emerged was fear. Not fear of the racism of young neo-Nazis who have attacked immigrants in Germany. Not fear of the hateful anti-immigrant rhetoric spouted by right-wing politicians like Jean-Marie Le Pen in France. Among the European participants, there was fear of being overwhelmed by "strangers at the gate."
Among the other participants, particularly from the U.S., Canada and Australia, which are used to accepting and assimilating different groups of people, there was a strong sense that Europe has to do more than just talk about and try to control "the problem."
No one knows exactly how extensive the flow of migrants will be. But the panic arises from concern that the numbers will be uncontrollable, and that the newcomers will represent deep differences of race, culture and religion, particularly Islam.
Those fears cloud the response to who the "strangers" might be. Economic migrants, who are traditionally subject to immigration controls, are lumped together with refugees fleeing political persecution. It is refugees who have the greatest claim on humanitarian impulses from richer societies.
As one United Nations official noted, most of the future refugees are likely to be fleeing generalized violence, not individual persecution. That may call for broader regional responses to supplement the efforts of individual countries to give sanctuary to individual asylum seekers. But thus far, Europe's response has been stingy: tighter border controls and stricter visa requirements.
Philip Rudge, general secretary of the European Consultation on Refugees and Exiles, who was not a conference participant, wonders whether the new European community is "creating an economic giant, but a human rights dwarf."
Demography frightens Europeans still more. One British scholar predicts that in the next decade the population of Africa will double to 1.2 billion, raising the prospect of a mass of starving people heading north. An American professor suggested that Europe's reaction might be different if the economies of the developed countries were more stable. Why let in immigrant workers when there are some 12 million unemployed in Europe?
After three days, there were no concrete solutions but some worthwhile suggestions. One U.S. Government official urged European countries to stop talking about "the problem and try to start managing the issue." That would include developing coherent and flexible migration policies, particularly for likely new categories of asylum seekers. Also discussed were regional containment of disputes and establishing protection for some persecuted people within their own borders.
Mostly, perhaps, Europe and other developed countries need a greater sense of positive urgency. As the United Nations official noted, it is the rich and powerful nations that, ultimately, set the international agenda. "If you are not moved to do it out of a spirit of decency, then do it for self-preservation."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Essay;
Office Pool, 1992

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE; Anthony Lewis is on vacation.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
"Mikhail Gorbachev will be ousted by Yeltsin-Sobchak-Shevardnadze reformers following public revulsion after a bloody crackdown" was the prediction made in this space last year.
Never mind that I recanted under pressure of television lights, or foolishly dismissed Norman Schwarzkopf as Ethelred the Unready; in the Nostradamus dodge, you remember only the time you were right. 
 Be your own pundit; astound your friends; time for the inimical, though much imitated, 18th Annual Office Pool.
1. The Nobel Peace Prize goes to (a) de Klerk and Mandela of South Africa; (b) Talabani or Barzani of Kurdistan (not to be confused with the new Kyrgyzstan); (c) Wei Jingsheng, the world's foremost dissident, still imprisoned in China; (d) James Baker for showing Arabs and Israelis that good corridors make good offices.
2. The U.S. economy at the end of 1992 will see (a) real growth under 2 percent with the Dow Jones industrials double-dipping below 3,000; (b) real growth over 4 percent with the D.J.I. over 4,000; (c) 3 percent growth as per consensus, Dow middling about 3,300, and prime interest rate headed back up to 3 percent.
3. The first foreign leader to pass from history's stage will be (a) Cuba's Castro; (b) Britain's Major; (c) Iraq's Saddam; (d) Libya's Qaddafi.
4. The most dramatic moment in the U.S. political conventions will come when (a) Jesse Jackson undermines Douglas Wilder with his ringing nomination of Tom Harkin; (b) George McGovern causes unending anguish at the Republican Convention by nominating Pat Buchanan; (c) Dan Quayle loyally refuses to dump his unpopular running mate; (d) Richard Nixon receives a welcome-back ovation.
5. The Russian leader who will be Time's Man of the Year is (a) Newsweek's Yeltsin, who will be laughing at those fainthearts who doubted the wisdom of free markets; (b) Vice President Rutskoi, conservative who will take over after the onset of free-market food riots; (c) Leningrad Mayor Sobchak, who will carry reform's banner if Yeltsin loses heart; (d) Gorbachev again, who will also appear in Nixon's "comeback box" at the Republican Convention.
6. Sleeper best seller of the year will be (a) Michael Crichton's Japan-bashing novel, "Rising Sun"; (b) Gay Talese's family memoir, "Unto the Son"; (c) Martin and Susan Tolchin's controversial "Selling Our Security"; (d) Steven Weinberg's mind-stretching "Dreams of the Final Theory."
7. Freedom will overwhelm authoritarianism next in (a) Iraq; (b) Singapore; (c) China; (d) North Korea.
8. The use of force will be universally deplored when (a) India takes out the Pakistan bomb facility; (b) Israel removes Saddam in delayed repayment for the Scuds; (c) the U.S. finds Qaddafi's tent with a smarter bomb; (d) Iraq attempts the final solution to the Kurdish problem.
9. America will be proudest of herself for (a) establishing a "Capitalists' Corps" to show former Soviet republics how to prosper; (b) repudiating the nativist resentment against immigrants; (c) developing an acceptable middle ground on the abortion issue; (d) leading the world into astonishing breakthroughs in neuroscience and genetics.
10. Scandal of the year will be (a) B.C.C.I.-Lavoro investigators turn up the political connections; (b) the revelations from the K.G.B. and Stasi files involving Western politicians; (c) sex and politics at a high and low level; (d) corruption in the Bush Administration.
11. Gov. Bill Clinton's running-mate will be (a) Bob Kerrey, who will never tell another joke; (b) Sam Nunn, for foreign-policy ballast; (c) Jay Rockefeller, to trumpet the health-care theme; (d) Al Gore, in payment for stopping the last-minute Cuomo movement.
12. Election results: (a) Bush in a 60 percent landslide, Republicans gain majority in Senate; (b) Democratic upset with decisive one-party Government for the 90's; (c) respectable win for Bush, but Democrats narrowly retain Congress; (d) squeaker for Bush, Demos gain in Congress, political paralysis in store.
13. Unpredictable mind-boggler of the year will be (a) U.S. military action in October; (b) GATT collapses,trade war breaks out; (c) Germany flexes muscles, takes over Europe; (d) terrorists get a nuclear bomb; (e) China convulses, goes democratic.
My picks are 1a, 2b, 3b, 4d, 5a, 6c, 7c, 8a, 9 all (my pools, my rules), 10b, 11a, 12c, 13e. By playing now you earn the right to hoot at the pundit next year.

HEADLINE: Theater in Review

BYLINE: By Stephen Holden

 BODY:

Reno Once Removed
The Public
Anspacher Theater
425 Lafayette Street
Manhattan
 
Conceived, written and performed by Reno; directed by Evan Yionoulis; original music by Mike Yionoulis; lighting by Dan Kotlowitz. Presented by I.P.A. Presents and the New York Shakespeare Festival, JoAnne Akalaitis, artistic director; Jason Steven Cohen, producing director; Rosemarie Tichler, associate artistic director.
Among performance artists who have emerged from the East Village in the last decade, Reno may be the most physically uninhibited. Her arms flailing, her jaw thrust out, her eyes darting with mischief, she spins around the stage in her new show like a perpetual motion machine. Squirming around the floor while trying to find shade from an imaginary bonsai tree or demonstrating how she wrings out and saves her paper towels to protect the environment, Reno triumphantly joins a classic slapstick tradition. 
 "Reno Once Removed," her one-woman show, had its premiere in the summer at Alice Tully Hall, where it was one of the hits of Lincoln Center's Serious Fun festival. Spruced up with some new material, its timing sharpened by the director Evan Yionoulis, it is tighter and funnier in its return engagement at the Anspacher.
Early in the 90-minute intermissionless show, Reno, who suggests a more compact, kinetic Bette Midler, accurately describes herself as "an entertainer passing as an artist." Several things, however, set her above and apart from mainstream stand-up comedy. One is her rough-and-tumble physicality. Another is her outspoken antiestablishment political stance. She lets the Republicans have it with both barrels smoking.
Reno is wonderful discussing the abuse of the word "denial," which she calls "the upper-class version of lying." In a funny spiel about the economy, she imagines America as a "pyramid scheme" that she traces back to Columbus's discovery of America. "Of course, there were 850,000 people here," she observes. "This country was born in denial."
And there is much more that is just as blunt in its polemics and scarily funny in its evocation of an overall moral bankruptcy.

CORRECTION-DATE: December 31, 1991, Tuesday

 CORRECTION:
A picture yesterday with a theater review of "Reno Once Removed" was published in error. It showed Mary Shultz in "Mad Forest" at the Perry Street Theater.

HEADLINE: Decline in Interest Rates Gives Retirees the Jitters

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BOCA RATON, Fla., Dec. 26

 BODY:
Any time David Schiller looks back to the early 1980's, he fondly recalls earning as much as 16 percent interest on the money he was then depositing in the bank. Those were the days, he said, when he could afford to dream of a worry-free retirement in some sunny clime.
Mr. Schiller is indeed retired now, and he and his wife, Ruth, own an apartment here. But the 66-year-old New York City native also finds himself working for the minimum wage as a security guard at the entrance to Century Village, a retirement community where he lives, and worrying about the rapid drop in interest rates that has cut drastically into his income. 
 "Even if you have $100,000 in the bank and you were getting $700 a month before, now you are only getting $400 on that same investment," Mr. Schiller, who was a program analyst at Morgan Guaranty Trust before moving to Florida five years ago, said as he waved cars past his checkpoint. "For people looking to their investments to supplement Social Security or a pension, that's a big loss in your income that you can only make up by going back to work."
 
Worried About Rates
 What alarms Mr. Schiller and other retirees here is the prospect that additional drops in their income may lie ahead. Millions of small investors will go to their banks on Jan. 2 to redeem certificates of deposit and bonds, only to find that interest rates have dropped to their lowest level in 27 years, thanks to the Federal Reserve's decision last week to reduce the interest rate it charges on loans to banks by a full percentage point, to 3.5 percent. The surprisingly big cut in the discount rate was seen as part of a broad effort to stimulate borrowing and perk up the economy.
The dramatic decline of interest rates over the last 18 months has affected virtually all Americans in one way or another. But economists agree that the elderly, many of whom live on fixed incomes, have been made especially vulnerable, a sentiment echoed in conversations with more than a score of retirees who have settled in pensioners' havens in South Florida.
"This is paradise here, but I have to tell you that I am worried," said Rella Feigelman, a retired data processor from Commack, L.I., who moved here with her husband, Irving, a former optician, to a retirement community called Whisper Walk two years ago.
"We are working people who worked very hard and saved very hard, and now we find out that we are going to have less than we thought we would have. At the same time, health-care costs, taxes and everything else is still going up. My husband tells me not to worry, but I do."
Few people would favor returning to the sky-high interest rates of 1980 and the 12.5 percent inflation rate that accompanied them, but interest rates are now half of what they were less than two years ago, and it is hard to know where they are headed next.
 
'Just About Get Along'
 Of course, many retirees are likely to feel little or no impact from the decline in interest rates. Around the country, there are large numbers of retirees who lived from paycheck to paycheck during their working years and never accumulated the kind of nest egg that would allow them the luxury of living off their interest in retirement.
"I don't have that many assets anyway, so I don't think it will have much of an effect on me," said Edith Jaffe, an 82-year-old widow who lives in Miami Beach and works at a senior citizen's center. "I don't get a pension and the only C.D.s I have are a pair of small ones of $1,000 each, so between Social Security and what I earn at my job, I just about get along."
For many others, whatever interest they earned on their investments has traditionally provided them with their only margin of security and comfort. Deprived of that cushion, they must now scramble to avoid joining the ranks of the elderly poor.
"It would be nice if rates just leveled off and didn't fluctuate so much," said Milton Mathias, who owned a dress manufacturing company in Manhattan's garment district before retiring. "It's hard to plan in this kind of environment."
Florida, whose population of 13.5 million people includes a higher percentage of people 60 years of age or older than any other state, is full of hundreds of thousands of middle-class retirees who thought they were set for life when they sold their homes in the Northeast and Midwest at the peak of the 1980's real-estate boom and moved here. Now, they are being forced to reassess their finances and their priorities.
"The price of a new car, even with a recession, is not going down," said Bill Nerenberg, a 70-year-old accountant originally from Philadelphia. "If the interest you are earning suddenly drops from $10,000 a year to $4,000, you're going to think twice about making a purchase like that, and will probably put it off for another year if you can."
The odd thing about the current economic situation, Mr. Nerenberg added, is that "the Government would like very much for people like us to spend," but by pushing interest rates down, the Federal Government "has curtailed our ability to spend, which only deepens the problem of the recession."
The downturn in interest rates has forced many retirees who live off their investments to gradually give up the small pleasures they once took for granted and to spend more time at home in an effort to stay within their reduced means.
"I used to bowl, play contract bridge, go to dances and eat out a lot," said an 87-year-old man who refused to be quoted by name as he stood in front of his home at Tallowwood, a trailer park south of here. Saying he retired to Florida in 1972 after a career on Wall Street, the man said he did not "have the income I used to have from my investments, so I have had to tighten my belt."
Having learned the hard way that investing in C.D.'s or buying stock does not necessarily guarantee financial stability, retirees whose knowledge of financial matters never extended much beyond individual retirement accounts now find themselves considering tax deferred annuities and more exotic instruments that promise to cushion them from recent fluctuations of the financial markets.
But the prospect of moving into that world, many retirees say, is a bit daunting.
"You can get a little more if you are willing to take a risk," said Albert Lorenz, a 75-year-old former car salesman who lives at Deerfield Lakes, a mobile home development for the elderly near here. "But at our age, people are cautious and want security. They are afraid to put their money in the stock market, much less the riskier things like junk bonds."
Many retirees say they know they can earn higher interest rates by investing in C.D.'s with long periods of maturity, up to five years. But they say they are reluctant to make commitments of that sort, fearing that they may have to tap their savings for a major operation, expensive drugs or some other emergency, like helping a son or daughter who has lost a job.
"Everybody knows somebody who is dipping into principal," said Lou Marloe, a former advertising salesman originally from Framingham, Mass. "It's something everybody wants to avoid, but there are a lot of people out there who just can't afford to reinvest."
Retirees say they know that politicians and economists are predicting that both the recession and the low interest rates will eventually end. But they also recognize they cannot be sure they will live to see that day.
"A football game has four quarters, and I'm in overtime, so I just play it day by day," said Mr. Lorenz, explaining that his annuity will run out early in 1992. "That's all I can do. I hope that things don't get any worse, but I am afraid they will."

CORRECTION-DATE: January 3, 1992, Friday

SECTION: Section D; Page 6; Column 2; Financial Desk

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
At 100, Public Relations' Pioneer Criticizes Some of His Heirs

BYLINE: By GLENN RIFKIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass.

 BODY:
Edward L. Bernays recently turned 100 years old, giving him the most protracted view of the discipline that he is credited with creating in the 1920's. But he is not happy with it now.
"Public relations today is horrible," he said in an interview at his home here. "Any dope, any nitwit, any idiot can call him or herself a public relations practitioner." 
 "A couple of weeks ago, a young woman called me to ask for advice about her career," he said. "She said she was in public relations. I asked her what she did. She said she gave out circulars in Harvard Square."
He bristled at her misuse of the term "public relations."
Mr. Bernays has spent a lifetime pushing public relations as a "social science" rather than a playing field for press agents. He says he persuaded H. L. Mencken to include his definition of public relations in a book on the American language in 1920. The definition: "a vocation applied by a social scientist who advises a client or employer on the social attitudes and actions to take to win the support of the public upon whom the viability of the client or employer depends."
Mr. Bernays pioneered the concept of using exhaustive market research in public relations, said Chester Burger, a retired public relations consultant. "He always had great research to back him," Mr. Burger said.
 
Still Active at 100
 Even at 100, Mr. Bernays said, he still consults with clients (and still maintains a career-long policy of not identifying clients). And he is writing a second volume of his memoirs, entitled "My First Hundred Years." The first volume, "Biography of an Idea," was published in 1965.
"Maybe the greatest contribution he has made is that when most people are sitting back in rocking chairs, he is still actively working and challenging people to think," said Joseph Epley, president of the Public Relations Society of America.
Mr. Bernays, a nephew of Sigmund Freud, was born in Vienna in 1891 and came to the United States a year later. He tried to enlist in the military during World War I, but was rejected because of his vision.
Then, he heard about President Woodrow Wilson's Office of War Information. "It was the first time the U.S. used ideas as weapons of war," he recalled. The office hired him to create propaganda campaigns.
"The Italians had fought a battle at a place called Piabe, and we were going to send a new ship to their aid," he said. "I suggested we call the ship the Piabe. We had Enrico Caruso break a bottle of champagne at the launch and then had photographs of the event made into postcards. We sent the postcards to the front lines and showered them on the Germans to show them that we were helping the Italians win the war."
After the war, Mr. Bernays realized that "if this could be used for war, it can be used for peace."
So his career began. In 1919, Mr. Bernays said, he opened the first public relations consulting firm. In 1923, he said, he taught the first public relations course at New York University, wrote the first book on the subject and coined the term "public relations counsel."
In a cramped office of a rambling Victorian home, just a few blocks from Harvard Square, Mr. Bernays's desk is piled with correspondence, memorabilia and citations. Outside his office, the walls are filled with photographs of Mr. Bernays with Herbert Hoover, Eleanor Roosevelt, Dwight Eisenhower, Enrico Caruso and other notables. And there is a photograph of Freud, his mother's brother, inscribed with best wishes to Mr. Bernays and Doris Fleischman on their wedding day in 1922.
Mr. Bernays said he gave advice to Calvin Coolidge on how to lighten up his dour image, persuaded American women to smoke in public and helped a bacon producer convince Americans that a heavy breakfast was preferable to a light one.
When asked to pick his most successful campaign, Mr. Bernays does not hesitate. "It was the late 1940's and E. L. Bransome, the president of Mack Truck, asked for help in getting more roads paved," Mr. Bernays recounted. He persuaded Mr. Bransome to hold a news conference andcall on Congress to build a road across the country.
Mr. Bernays said the news conference pushed Congress to approve money for Route 66 and ultimately led to the Interstate highways.
"One single idea changed the economics of a country," he said.
 
Foibles of the Industry
 Mr. Bernays enjoys the fuss that has been made about his 100th birthday (it was Nov. 22) and uses the attention to speak out about the foibles of public relations today.
"Some people just use public relations as a euphemism for press agentry," he declared. "A firm sends articles or press releases to newspapers to win favor for a client and it usually ends up in the trash. It's not only not good P.R.; it intensifies the antagonism toward the product. I'm pleased to be known as the father of public relations when the field is taken seriously, like law or architecture."

LENGTH: 595 words

HEADLINE: Patterns

BYLINE: By Woody Hochswender

 BODY:

 
Notes from All Over
 Just because it was a so-so year in fashion doesn't mean it wasn't a great year in history.
An empire crumbled, a bloody tyrant was humbled. The world seemed breathless. There was no time to shop. 
 The fall of Communism dwarfed in significance any drop in hemlines. Fashion took a back seat to the catwalk of world events.
In December, Boris N. Yeltsin's daughter was shown on Cable News Network wearing a black Lycra miniskirt. It was not just a typical Western style worn by a Russian, but a very hot, contemporary look right off the streets of New York. If that doesn't speak eloquently about a new Russian revolution, nothing does.
Export was said to be a new priority on Seventh Avenue, one of the most provincial of American businesses. Oscar de la Renta held two fashion shows in Paris in 1991. The rest of the industry just talked.
The cheap-is-chic phenomenon was not confined to the United States. Conspicuous consumption was no longer de rigueur in Japan, the last best hope of luxury goods conglomerates. The December newletter of Isetan, a Japanese retailer, referred to the "post-bubble economy" and described strategies the company was using to entice reluctant consumers to spend more at Christmas.
Indeed, one could discern a sea change away from throw-away consumerism. Sophisticated people, the ones who usually keep pace with fashion, began to get more sophisticated about the fate of the planet. In this sense, fashionable people have become truly worldy, in contrast to the ersatz worldliness of the traditional fashion poseur.
 
No More Ephemeral Images
 Interestingly, the fashion companies themselves seem attuned to these developments.
Here is a passage from a recent press release sent out by Giorgio Armani : "There are two concepts which the tumultuous beginning of the 90's has eliminated: 'the ephemeral' and 'the image.' In the case of the ephemeral, it is a euphoric, sometimes superficial attitude that tends to be a little excessive toward all consumption. By image, we mean everything that is overly excessive and strident, annihilating content, matter and language."
The text went on to extol the virtues of Mr. Armani's men's wear collection for spring 1992, including the "normality" of the colors and the "normality" of the shapes. It added, "Today, the new success is catching people's eye with normality."
A toast, then, to normality.
 
Street Style
 Street style continued to be at least as important as runway fashion style. And young men, not women, emerged as the fashion leaders of the moment.
Many people began wearing baseball hats with the letter "X." Just when it seemed only a matter of time before some enterprising milliner would come out with a cap bearing the letter "Y," one learned that this style was a gesture of respect for the late Malcolm X.
Another style among young men was wearing their trousers extremely baggy and slung halfway down the hips. Department store executives confessed to be somewhat baffled by the trend. Teen-agers would come in and be measured for, say, a 32-inch waist, then insist on buying 34- or 36-inch trousers. The extra width allowed for them to be alarmingly loose and low.
This style is said to derive from prisons, where inmates are not permitted to wear belts.
Rap stars picked it up, and now it has become mainstream. Marky Mark, a rap singer, took the style to an extreme, appearing in a music video showing at least six inches of his white B.V.D.'s. It made those Button Your Fly T-shirts seem almost discreet.

HEADLINE: Ex-Soviet States Allow Separate Armies

BYLINE: By FRANCIS X. CLINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MINSK, Belarus, Dec. 30

 BODY:
After a long day of disputes, the former Soviet republics agreed today that individual members of their new Commonwealth of Independent States could form separate armies, though strategic weapons would remain under a single command.
The decision to let the states decide on whether to build their own forces or to remain part of a common defense was agreed to in a bargaining session of sharp differences and private compromise that let each of the 11 former republics decide its own military fate. 
 Ukraine, Azerbaijan and Moldova plan to build separate armies, while the other former republics will share in the commonwealth joint defense force, today's announcement said.
 
Freeing of Prices
 In the economic sphere, Ukraine and Belarus said they would go ahead with their own "price liberalizations" after Ukraine failed to get Russia to postpone the price changes scheduled to begin on Thursday. Ukraine had argued that it was not prepared for the drastic price increases that are expected to occur on a wide range of goods. But as a result of today's discussions, Ukraine will begin a new price plan on Jan. 10 and Belarus on Jan. 3, a day after Russia's plan starts.
Various details of the military agreement remain unclear, particularly the question of how the former republics might share units and equipment of the huge Soviet military force.
"It's not easy to take apart one structure and build new ones," said the Belarus President, Stanislav Shushkevich, after the leaders of the fledgling commonwealth put in a full day of wrangling and, ultimately, some tenuous compromising. In all, the leaders signed 15 documents on issues ranging from uses of space to distribution of foreign food.
But they reached only temporary agreement on the two major coordinating bodies of the commonwealth, the Council of Heads of State and the Council of Heads of Government.
Thus far, in little more than a month of effort, the former republics have agreed mainly on the broad strokes of their commonwealth, with critical details on the military and other controversies still remaining for later meetings.
The Russian President, Boris N. Yeltsin, stressed that today's gathering reaffirmed the original agreement to have the four members of the commonwealth with long-range nuclear weapons operate in concert on nuclear policy, with the launch decision ultimately in the hands of Russia and the central military command.
The precise technical details for this new procedure should be worked out in "five or six days," Mr. Yeltsin said.
In response to Secretary of Defense Dick Cheney's stated concern that the Soviet missile fleet continues to be updated and aimed at American targets, Mr. Yeltsin declared, "Everything agreed to in the strategic arms treaties will be respected by Russia and the commonwealth states."
 
Strategy on Economy
 In their efforts to succeed the collapsed Soviet Union, the former republics agreed their remaining differences are many, particularly on a common strategy for dealing with the desperate economy that is the main legacy of Communism.
As economic anxiety rose across the former republics, the commonwealth members announced they would go ahead with a previous agreement to conduct their separate free-market plans in a common "ruble zone" based on the battered currency of Russia.
But President Leonid M. Kravchuk of Ukraine, where nationalist mistrust of the commonwealth is greatest, was openly skeptical.
"The ruble zone can turn into a fiction," he said at a commonwealth press conference tonight. "We could find ourselves in the ruble zone without any rubles," he added, complaining that Russia, which controls the ruble printing presses, had not been shipping enough currency to cover rising expenses in other republics.
 
Shortages of Rubles
 As a result of Mr. Kravchuk's complaints, Mr. Yeltsin said that he would see to it that no ruble shortages occur, particularly as consumer prices are freed Thursday as part of Russia's free-market strategy.
In response to fears that the freeing of prices will dislocate the economies of the rest of the commonwealth, Ukraine officials said today that price rises in their state, the commonwealth's second most populous, would follow on Jan. 10. Kiev also announced that it would double wages for state employees on that date, paying part of the increase in newly issued coupons.
The commonwealth leaders apparently put their main effort today into the question of how to use and share in the vast Soviet military establishment.
Ukraine continues to insist on the right to take over sizable union military units and their equipment. The agreements announced tonight did not specifically settle that question, nor the issue of a common border defense.
The republic leaders did announce agreement, however, on setting up a structure that would put defense policy under a single command controlled jointly by a commonwealth council with representatives of the former republics and advisers from their respective Defense Ministries.
As with many other points of announced resolution, however, it was not immediately clear how this might work in practice.
 
Threat to Resign
 At one point in the day, the chief of the central military command, Marshal Yevgeny I. Shaposhnikov reportedly threatened to resign in the face of a confusion of authority that he foresaw. In the end, his appointment was extended for two months.
"It was not a simple agreement on the armed forces," Mr. Yeltsin commented, confirming considerable differences through the day. "Nevertheless we managed to find common language," that, he said, "Marshal Shaposhnikov now joined in supporting."
"The military issue was most complicated for all of us," said Mr. Yeltsin. He said, however, that "we would be blowing the same old flute" if the leaders had dealt with their disputes in the old monolithic ways of Communism.
"Sovereignty is not something up for bargain or for sale," said Mr. Kravchuk, who repeatedly stressed that the commonwealth's interests are secondary to Ukrainian independence.
 
Stress on Sovereignty
 "It's the Commonwealth of Independent States," he said, stressing independence. "Decisions interfering in internal policies are not accepted by us."
Journalists who were allowed briefly into the meeting room said the debates were often stormy, especially when they dealt with banking and prices. The meeting lasted several hours longer than planned, with almost an hour spent on setting the agenda.
An attempt to discuss fighting in Nagorno-Karabakh, a region of Azerbaijan populated largely by Armenians, was resisted by Azerbaijan as "interference in its internal affairs."

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia, right, and President Stanislav Shushkevich of Belarus, left, talking to reporters yesterday in Minsk, where the leaders of eleven former Soviet republics met. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Fatal Alcohol Abuse Persists On Campus, but Fewer Drink

BYLINE: By WILLIAM CELIS 3d, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BLACKSBURG, Va.

 BODY:
Drinking among college students has declined over the past decade, studies show, as society has grown less tolerant of alcohol. But that decline masks a trend that is proving both stubborn and lethal: the persistence of abusive or "binge" drinking.
"More students are drinking abusively," said Thomas G. Goodale, vice president for student affairs at Virginia Polytechnic Institue here. "I've never seen it like I've seen it in the last 10 years, with the last five years worse than the first five." 
 Although the number of college students who said they drank declined to 80 percent this year from 89 percent in 1981, according to a survey by the University of Florida, another survey for the Federal Government by the University of Michigan suggests that abusive drinking among college students has held relatively steady, declining only three percentage points in the same period. The survey also found that such abusive drinking had declined among 19- to 22-year-olds who were not in college.
 
Grappling With Problem
 At Virginia Tech and Boston College, from Rutgers to Stanford, administrators are grappling with the problem. And at some campuses, there has clearly been an increase in abusive drinking, which is defined by the National Institute of Drug Abuse and other specialists as consuming five or more drinks at one sitting.
At Oklahoma State University in Stillwater, the student health center treats an average of 10 cases of severe alcohol poisoning a semester, up from six a decade ago. At Gritman Medical Center in Moscow, Idaho, which serves the University of Idaho, minor to severe cases of alcohol poisoning among students have risen to an average of eight a week, double the number a decade ago. And at Montgomery Regional Hospital, near Virginia Tech, the same range of cases involving students have increased to five to seven a week from two to three a week in the early 1980's, people who work in the emergency room said.
Generations of college students have drunk to excess, reveling in their newfound freedom from parental reins. But college deans, alcohol rehabilitation specialists and students themselves say many of the factors that lead to abusive drinking have intensified in recent years.
"The 21 drinking age law is the most unenforced law in the country," said Arthur Sandeen, vice president of student affairs at the University of Florida in Gainesville. "Frankly, it's a nightmare."
College officials acknowledge that they could use the help of parents to deal with hard-drinking students. But they say Federal privacy laws bar them from divulging anything about a student's record to his parents, including a destructive drinking problem, without the student's permission.
Students themselves suggest that attempts to curtail alcohol consumption are futile because they will find ways to bypass rules and laws. "If they make the rules harder, then kids will try harder to break them," said Lara Deming, a 19-year-old Virginia Tech sophomore, who added that despite her age she could find alcohol whenever she wanted it. "I don't think they can do anything to keep kids from drinking."
 
Increase in Pressure Seen
 Students and administrators also say today's students are under more peer and academic pressure than in the past. "They have a lot more to deal with than I did," said Candyce Reynolds, a 34-year-old counselor at Portland State University in Oregon who attended college in the 1970's. "I think younger people are disenfranchised. They face many more difficulties, including exposure to alcohol, risky sexual behavior and a staggering economy."
Isaac and Alease Parsons had warned their son Wayne, a 19-year-old sophomore at Virginia Tech, about the perils of alcohol. Wayne, the youngest of three brothers and two sisters, had even been lectured by an older brother.
But when Wayne went to a party with about 30 other people at a friend's apartment one night at the end of September, he gulped a large quantity of beer, then in 15 minutes drank an oversized tumbler of about 32 ounces of tequila, friends told the police.
About an hour later, he collapsed. An emergency medical crew was called but could not revive him. He died in the hospital at 6 the next morning, his distraught and unbelieving parents at his side. The cause of death was alcohol poisoning.
"I rubbed his hand and talked to him, but I never could get a response," said his father, Isaac Parsons, his voice breaking. "I'm crying all the time. I can't get it off my mind."
For all the Parsonses knew, their son had heeded their advice on drinking. "He was very good about doing what he was told," said Mr. Parsons, who added that once he even offered Wayne a drink, which his son declined. "He never gave us a minute's trouble, no back talk. We had no indication that he drank."
That is why the Parsonses are still finding it so hard to come to terms with the death of their son, a sophomore electrical engineering major who was a solid B student with a bright mind and a knack for computers.
"Why would he do something like this?" Mr. Parsons asked, sobbing. "He had a good ability and mind about things. I was constantly telling him not to let outsiders influence him. Was he coaxed?"
The Parsons say they believe that other students at the party cajoled their son into the drinking that killed him because to have done it on his own would have been out of character. To support that they cited statements by students at the party to the Blacksburg police and university officials who investigated the incident that their son got the alcohol at the party.
 
Family Starts Campaign
 "To a certain degree, I blame the other students," Mr. Parsons said. "They could have taken the alcohol away from him."
Unlike the Parsonses, Mike and Sandra Brodwater of Coeur d'Alene, Idaho, knew their son Alan drank and sometimes drank excessively. But they thought they had helped him get over it.
Alan, Mr. Brodwater said, was trying to improve his 3.0 grade point average to 3.4 or 3.5 and used alcohol to ease the stress. But after several conversations with his son about the dangers of drinking, Mr. Brodwater said, "I really felt that the worst was over. So when Alan, the second of four boys and a junior majoring in accounting at the University of Idaho, died at 1:10 A.M. on Nov. 1 after a bout of drinking that university officials said began at his apartment and continued at a Halloween party off campus, his parents were shocked.
"I felt absolute disbelief," Mr. Brodwater said.
"When they are in college, parents in a sense have already released them to the world," Mr. Brodwater said. "But we're all still sorting this out. It's a nightmare all parents dream about, getting that call. Except in our case it happened."
Such deaths have led some grieving families, like that of Larry K. Wooten, a 21-year-old University of Florida junior, to mount campaigns against alcohol abuse.
Mr. Wooten, a history major with a 4.0 grade average, was watching a football game with friends at a bar in Gainesville when he and an old friend challenged each other to see who could drink more.
"It was something these boys always did," said Linda Wooten, Larry's mother. "They competed through baseball, football, grades, girlfriends. Larry was going to beat Stevie's record."
And he did, drinking 23 shots of liquor, one more drink than his friend, in an hour. The other student survived, but when friends took Larry to his apartment late that night, they noticed he had passed out and that his hands and feet were turning blue. The friends called an ambulance, but he died of asphyxiation on the way to the hospital.
Their son's death prompted the Wootens to begin a drive to force the Florida Legislature to adopt tougher laws on the liability of bars and restaurants that serve liquor to people who are drunk. All states have some sort of law that places some liability on bars and restaurants, but the laws vary in their severity.
"I'm going to do everything in my power to get that law passed so that the bars are responsible," said the dead student's father, Larry G. Wooten.
 
Changes on Campuses
 The Wootens' effort is part of a scattershot approach undertaken in recent years by parents, universities, fraternities and students to try to corral a problem that Mr. Goodale of Virginia Tech says "is the one problem colleges haven't been able to get their arms around."
No single approach has emerged from those efforts, but a growing number of universities have ended the serving of alcohol on campus.
Other institutions have taken a more aggressive stand. In New Jersey, Rutgers University in 1988 established special housing at two of its five campuses for students recovering from alcohol and drug dependency. Between classes, they can attend counseling sessions without ever having to leave campus.
Driving some of the effort by colleges and universities is the 1989 Drug Free School and Campuses Act, requiring institutions to make alcohol education programs and services like counseling available to students and employees.
The possibility of lawsuits, too, continues to haunt universities, even though litigation against institutions in the 1960's dealing with a variety of wrongful deaths were won by the colleges and universities when courts ruled that the schools should not be held responsible for student behavior.
For all these efforts, college administrators and alcohol counselors say alcohol abuse will persist on campus as long as it persists in society. "No matter how much money is put behind this, the problem doesn't seem to go away," said Kelly Heryla, a chemical dependency specialist at Indiana University of Pennsylvania.
Many students agree. "Kids feel like they have to drink," said Brenda Wirsbinski, a 20-year-old junior finance major at the University of Wisconsin at Eau Claire. "They feel the pressure from other people. It's really common for incoming freshmen to go out every weekend. That's what college is about."

Markets
The stock market's record-breaking rally accelerated, with the Dow Jones industrial average surging 62.39 points to a high of 3,163.91. In December, the Dow has soared 269.23 points, or 9.3 percent, topping the December 1924 record of 8.4 percent. [D1.]

Wheat and soybean futures prices fell sharply amid growing fears that food would not be sent the former Soviet republics. [D13.]

HEADLINE: Bush Avoids Criticism of Japanese

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HONOLULU, Dec. 30

 BODY:
President Bush, on a brief stopover here on the way to Australia, where he is beginning a four-nation Pacific region trip, was asked today if he agreed with a comment by Commerce Secretary Robert A. Mosbacher that the Japanese were partly responsible for the American recession.
He replied obliquely: "We need to get more access to more markets abroad. I think we will." 
 Asked if he thought the drop today in Japan's discount rate -- to 4.5 percent from 5 percent -- was a gesture to him before his visit Jan. 7-10, Mr. Bush was again oblique.
"I think they realize that growth is a good way for the world economy to go," the President said, "and that was a very important step."
Mr. Bush's Treasury Secretary, Nicholas F. Brady, talked about interest rates on Sunday with the Japanese Finance Minister, Tsutomu Hata.
Of that conversation and the rate cut, Mr. Bush said simply: "It's encouraging."
Mr. Mosbacher was pressed in a television interview on Sunday on whether the Japanese were responsible for the American recession, whether it was "made in Japan," as Representative John D. Dingell, Democrat of Michigan and chairman of the House Energy and Commerce Committee, had said.
"Not totally, no," Mr. Mosbacher said on the NBC news program "Meet the Press."
Asked whether this meant that the Japanese were partly responsible, he said: "I would say they are exacerbating the problem" by not allowing in American imports that would produce jobs at home. Not all the comments here, however, were about economics or were so circumspect.
Reporters asked Barbara Bush what she was planning for New Year's Eve in Sydney, Australia.
"I'm going to go hang around bars," she said, but then added, "Seriously, same thing I do every New Year's Eve with George Bush -- go to bed early."

HEADLINE: Lover's Rage Is Tied To 3 Deaths on L.I.

DATELINE: VALLEY STREAM, L.I., Dec. 30

 BODY:
An unemployed printer shot and killed the father and brother of a woman who had left him a few days before, and then fatally shot himself in the chest, the police said today.
About 10:15 P.M. Sunday, the police said, the printer, Dennis Carney, brandishing a shotgun, kicked in the front door of a house at 29 Bayview Avenue here, searching for Doreen Ferrante, who had just ended a nine-year relationship with him. 
 In the kitchen he found her father, Dominick, a 59-year-old retired New York City correction officer visiting for the holidays from Fort Myers, Fla., and shot him in the abdomen, the police said.
When Miss Ferrante's brother, John, a 28-year-old New York City court officer, ran out of a bedroom and down a hallway, he was shot in the chest. The police said Mr. Carney turned his shotgun on himself in the living room.
Detective Sgt. William Cocks of the Nassau County homicide squad said that Miss Ferrante, a court officer in Brooklyn Criminal Court, and Mr. Carney had lived together for nine years, and had a 6-year-old daughter.
"He was told a day or so ago that she was leaving him because of verbal and physical abuse," Sergeant Cocks said. At the time of the shootings, neither Miss Ferrante nor her daughter were in the Bayview Avenue house, where her brother lived.

"At this stage, we are well on the path of actually achieving the type of goals which we've set out to achieve: a solid economic recovery with the unemployment rate moving down to its lowest sustainable long-term rate, with growth at or close to its maximum long-term sustainable pace, with inflation wholly under control."
Alan Greenspan testifying in July before a House Banking subcomittee on the state of the economy.

Mr. Greenspan testifying in December before the House Ways and Means Committee on the state of the economy.

Edward Yingling, chief lobbyist of the American Bankers Association, discussing Congress's decision not to enact a measure overhauling the banking system.

Robin Leigh-Pemberton, governor of the Bank of England, explaining why regulators seized the Bank of Credit and Commerce International in July.

Jon C. Madonna, KPMG Peat Marwick's chairman, on the firm's decision to lay off 300 partners.

HEADLINE: Minor Candidate's Fund-Raising Success Turns Spotlight on Party

BYLINE: By MARTIN GOTTLIEB

 BODY:
When Lenora B. Fulani, the Presidential candidate of the New Alliance Party, qualified last week for more Federal matching money than any other candidate except President Bush and Senator Tom Harkin of Iowa, she may have done more than surprise almost everyone in politics.
She may finally have focused more than passing attention on a small political movement that has managed over 25 years to turn itself into a complex multimillion-dollar enterprise despite accusations that it operates like a cult and is anti-Semitic. 
 Aside from Ms. Fulani's numerous campaigns for political office, New Alliance and groups affiliated with it support local political campaigns, psychotherapy groups, a lobbying operation in Washington and a publishing arm whose books and magazines support Louis Farrakhan of the Nation of Islam, the Rev. Al Sharpton and the Government of Libya.
 
Actions Defended
 Many other leftist groups and even New Alliance's Presidential candidate in 1984, Dennis Serrette, now disassociated from the party, have labeled the party a disruptive organization in which members take orders from its founder, Fred Newman.
These accusations are dismissed by Mr. Newman and Ms. Fulani, who say that their fund-raising success comes from the commitment of volunteers who believe in the goals of what the party describes as a black-led, pro-gay, pro-socialist, multiracial progressive movement.
Last week, Ms. Fulani, a 41-year-old developmental psychologist, qualified to receive $624,497 in matching Federal money on the strength of her fund-raising effort so far. Of the others who have announced their candidacies for President in 1992, only Mr. Bush, who qualified to receive $2.6 million, and Senator Harkin, who qualified for $1.075 million, did better.
In rare instances, other minor party candidates have received matching funds, most notably Lyndon LaRouche, who in the course of three Presidential campaigns in the 80's received a total of more than $1 million. Citing a 1988 conviction on fraud charges and fund-raising abuses, the Federal Election Commission this month voted to deny him money for the 1992 campaign.
Under the Federal program, candidates receive dollar-for-dollar matching money of up to $250 per contribution if they are able to raise at least $5,000 in 20 states. There will be periodic opportunities for candidates to qualify for more matching funds, and Ms. Fulani hopes to go back for more.
Her press secretary, Madelyn Chapman, said the party's success in raising money for Ms. Fulani reflected the work of perhaps 200 volunteers who collected 40,000 contributions, mainly on street corners and by knocking on doors, particularly in California and New York City.
Political fund raising is but one of many income-generating efforts involving New Alliance and related organizations. In an interview last month, Mr. Newman said the combined efforts produced millions of dollars a year, much of which is spent on services like legal help, advertising, printing and public relations that are provided by organizations started by party members. The organizations operate out of an office suite on West 57th Street in Manhattan and a downtown condominium loft.
 
Sponsor of Cultural Events
 Among the organizations is a Congressional lobbying organization, the Rainbow Lobby. It raised $765,329 in 1988, making it one of the 15 largest Congressional lobbyists that year.
Another New Alliance-related group, the Community Literacy Research Project, sponsors cultural activities that include a teen-age talent show that is put together by a private organization owned by Mr. Newman and Mr. Sharpton. Many activities, including plays, art exhibits, visits by Libyan dignitaries and shows by female impersonators, are conducted in the downtown loft space. Mr. Newman said a therapy center in the loft complex conducts group sessions there..
When Ms. Fulani ran for President in 1988, she collected more than $900,000 in Federal matching funds. Ms. Chapman said much of the money in the current campaign would be spent on travel and room and board for campaign workers and, she hoped, for an expanded advertising campaign.
According to filings with the Federal Election Commission, between July 1 and Sept. 30 the campaign spent $68,478 on services provided by New Alliance-related organizations.
Ms. Fulani, who in addition to her third-party campaign for President has entered the Democratic primary in New Hampshire, said yesterday that the donations were "a loud statement by the people of this country for a broader range of candidates and viewpoints." In a telephone interview yesterday she said health care, housing, improving the economy and opening elections to more viewpoints would be issues in her campaign, but she did not offer specific programs.
The Anti-Defamation League of B'nai B'rith issued a report a year ago that concluded that the party had "the trappings of a cult: one-man leadership and authority figure in Fred Newman; a small, devoted following; a wide variety of 'front groups' that spread its message; an ability to raise money successfully, and a private agenda not readily evident from the party's public positions."
Mr. Newman dismissed the accusations as "absurd." Mr. Newman, who is Jewish, said his group is anti-Zionist but not anti-Semitic.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Lenora B. Fulani, a developmental psychologist who is the Presidential candidate of the New Alliance Party, is eligible to receive more than $600,000 in Federal money to finance her campaign. (Michelle V. Agins/The New York Times)
