HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Zale Jewelers Will Close 400 Outlets

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM

 BODY:
The Zale Corporation, a Dallas-based jewelry retailer, has become an early victim of the recession and the dismal holiday selling season. It said yesterday that it would close 400 of its nearly 2,000 stores and halt all payments to banks, bondholders and suppliers while it devised a plan to rebuild its faltering business.
About 2,500 employees, or 20 percent of Zale's work force, are likely to lose their jobs as part of the closings. Zale has stores throughout the country, and in addition to those operating under the Zale's name, it owns the Bailey Banks & Biddle, Gordon's and Corrigan's jewelry chains. It was not clear yesterday, however, how many stores in each chain would be closed. 
 A year that Zale described as "one of the worst retailing years in recent history" forced the company to take the radical steps to halt its downturn. The retailer has been buffeted by the sharp decline in jewelry sales during the recession while it has struggled to manage interest payments on about $850 million in debt.
Preliminary third-quarter results announced yesterday showed that sales at Zale and Peoples Jewellers Ltd. in Toronto, which owns 47.5 percent of Zale as a result of a leveraged buyout in 1986, dropped by about 10 percent, compared with the similar period last year. The results in the 1991 period largely reflect holiday sales. Zale did not report a profit figure.
Swarovski International Holding A.G., a Swiss crystal company, also owns 47.5 percent of the retailer.
Zale, which had sales of $1.3 billion last year and a loss of $64.1 million, said it would not make a $52 million interest payment on outstanding bonds, which is due on Thursday.
David Glatstein of Barre & Company, a Dallas bond brokerage that holds some of the bonds and has also sold more than $50 million of them, recently threatened to file an involuntary bankruptcy petition against Zale if it failed to make the payments.
"We just received the information, and are trying to digest what the information means, whether we should be more lenient in light of the announcement or not," Mr. Glatstein said yesterday.
Analysts said Zale had little choice but to restructure its operations. Several high-yield bond analysts have recommended selling the company's bonds, particularly after Zale reported that sales for its second quarter, which ended on Sept. 30, dropped by 22 percent from the period a year earlier, and that the company had a loss of $105.6 million for the first six months of its fiscal year.
Although the prices of Zale bonds dropped yesterday, the company's announcement did not incite a flurry of trading. Traders said the market was probably waiting to hear the company's further restructuring plans. The company's debt is already deeply discounted, trading at less than 20 cents on the dollar.
 
Companywide Revamping
 Irving R. Gerstein, the chairman of Zale and Peoples, said that under the restructuring, Zale would also reduce the size and costs of its headquarters office and reorganize its divisional operations.
Analysts have faulted Zale for its acquisition of the Gordon Jewelry Corporation in 1989, saying it was not distinct enough from the company's Zale's stores and thus confused the consumer.
"We are confident that the operational plan will demonstrate substantial cost savings and improve cash flow immediately," Mr. Gerstein said.
Peoples said the restructuring would dilute its stake in Zale and have a negative effect on its net worth, which might render it unable to satisfy its bank loan agreements and pay dividends on its stock.

HEADLINE: Japan's Economic Steps: Soothing U.S.

BYLINE: By STEVEN R. WEISMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Dec. 30

 BODY:
In a display of fresh anxiety over its sagging economy and American trade pressures, Japan has unveiled steps in the last few days to spur economic growth at home and drive up the value of the yen in world currency markets.
The steps were seen as an attempt to defuse American anger and impatience over Japan's mounting trade surpluses before President Bush's visit Jan. 7-10. 
 In recent weeks, Japanese officials have made it clear that Tokyo will take no more than cosmetic or symbolic steps to open up specific markets, like automobiles or auto parts, to imports. Instead, what is emerging is a Japanese effort to recalibrate its economic policies to insure greater growth.
The most substantial indication of this shift by Tokyo came today when the Bank of Japan lowered the official discount rate by a half percentage point to 4 1/2 percent, the third such reduction in six months. Some expected such a cut in the coming months, but few expected it now.
 
Aimed at Economy
 The central bank's governor, Yasushi Mieno, told reporters that the rate cut was aimed solely at stimulating the Japanese economy, and not at trying to please President Bush or at matching the Federal Reserve Bank's own cut in the discount rate on Dec. 20.
Mr. Mieno said it was done now so corporations could take it into account in their financial planning. "We particularly kept in mind the fact that the period between January and March is when firms draw up their capital spending plans," he added.
The lowering of the discount rate set off a surge on the Tokyo Stock Exchange, where the Nikkei Stock average of 225 selected issues climbed 546.45 points, to 22,983.77, in a two-hour session that was curtailed because of the coming New Year's holiday.
Meanwhile, in currency markets, the yen has been surging against the dollar in line with an apparent Japanese policy to make imports cheaper and exports more expensive.
The dollar, however, finished the day in Tokyo up slightly at 125.83 yen after the lowering of the discount rate. Cutting the discount rate usually has the effect of lowering the value of the yen. That Japan ultimately wants the yen to increase in value was seen in other quiet signals sent separately to the financial community, according to financial analysts.
Some noted that the lower rates could help restore business confidence, leading to faster growth next year. That and the Government's plans for public works spending in the $570 billion budget for the next fiscal year adopted over the weekend are likely to stimulate business investment, they said, ultimately driving up the yen.
 
Way to Cut Deficit
 Many American economists say that an ever-higher yen -- its value has nearly doubled since 1985 -- is the most powerful way of reducing the American trade deficit with Japan, which was $41 billion last year.
Most analysts agreed that the discount rate cut was put into effect with the encouragement of Prime Minister Kiichi Miyazawa as a signal to the White House that Japan would try to stimulate its economy to help the United States out of its own recession.
Indeed both Mr. Miyazawa and his Finance Minister, Tsutomu Hata, welcomed the central bank's move, as did most corporate leaders.
"The decision will have favorable effects on the domestic economy," said Gaishi Hiraiwa, chairman of Keidanren, the most powerful of Japan's business trade associations. "The rate cut is welcome in terms of international cooperation ahead of Bush's visit."
Mr. Bush has said repeatedly that his main priority in traveling to Japan is to stimulate American exports and create new jobs. A more robust Japanese economy would be more likely to import American products.
By contrast, American economists fear that if there is a downturn in Japan, companies there may try to increase their profits by exporting more to the United States.
The Bank of Japan's move coincided with other steps by Mr. Miyazawa to stimulate the economy, also in response to American pressures. The budget, for example, relies heavily on borrowing. Mr. Miyazawa said he hoped the budget would serve as a "locomotive" to pull the rest of the global economy out of its slump.
Among the biggest spending increases in the budget is the category of capital spending on roads, bridges and other construction projects. The United States has long demanded that Japan make that a priority on the theory that it would stimulate the economy and also imports. Another big spending increase is in the area of foreign aid to poor countries -- also in line with American requests -- to fill a gap left by United States cutbacks.
One fear of all these steps is that Japan may revive the so-called bubble economy of the last few years, in which easy credit, combined with inflated real estate and stock market prices, led to a borrowing binge and inflation.
The bursting of the bubble has thrown the economy into disarray in recent months, weakening some major corporations and financial institutions, which are now expected to benefit as a result of the new stimuli. But officials sought to make clear today that the bubble economy would not return.
"The Government is committed to sustaining economic growth without fears of inflation, and it is determined to pursue an economic policy which will preclude the rekindling of the bubble economy," said Mr. Hata, the Finance Minister.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
An Even-Handed Collector Of Labor Statistics Retires

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
After 13 1/2 years as head of the Bureau of Labor Statistics, Janet L. Norwood retires from the Government Tuesday with a fax machine presented by 325 admiring colleagues, a near-legendary reputation for nonpartisanship, and plaudits that include one senator's designation of her as a "national treasure."
If an informed public is essential for democracy, Dr. Norwood says, solid data are essential for an informed public. "You can't have a democratic society without having a good data base," she said. 
 But while Dr. Norwood, the first person to rise through the bureau's ranks to the top, is proud of her role in keeping the agency up to date, she frets about unfinished business. In particular, she bemoans the lack of the coherence in Federal data gathering that she considers necessary to help the nation meet competitive challenges in the next century.
"We have a good system, but it is becoming increasingly fragmented," Dr. Norwood said during an extended interview. "We don't have a system that really coordinates all the money that is being spent on statistics -- and most importantly, the quality of that work."
 
Speedier Data Collection
 She cited, among other new efforts, stepped-up data collection in such areas as the environment, education and law enforcement. The highway bill signed by President Bush this month authorizes $90 million to create a Bureau of Transportation Statistics, she added. These efforts are in addition to those of the long-established "Big Four" -- the Commerce Department's Census Bureau and Bureau of Economic Analysis, the Agriculture Department and the Labor Department's Bureau of Labor Statistics.
Better coordination does not necessarily require an umbrella agency as in Canada, Mexico and some other countries, Dr. Norwood said. But she argues that the United States must at least strengthen its coordination of data gathering, using, say, Japan or Britain as a guide.
A second American failing is the collection and analysis of the figures that permit comparison with the performance of other countries, Dr. Norwood said. As a junior economist in the bureau in the 1960's she researched such things as Burmese labor law and Japanese wages. "We are not really operating internationally as effectively as we ought," she said, referring to the bureau's data-gathering. "I'm concerned about the increasing effectiveness of other countries. I worry about where we will be 10 or 15 years from now."
After a January vacation, Dr. Norwood, a Newark native who is 68 years old, will be analyzing and speaking out about such issues at the Urban Institute, a Washington research organization. She will be free from the burden of administering a Federal agency with 2,700 employees and an annual budget of $300 million.
Her successor, expected to come from outside the agency, has not yet been selected, though an advisory committee has screened candidates for the $112,100-a-year job. William G. Barron, the deputy commissioner, will take over temporarily.
Commissioner Norwood will also no longer be a fixture on Capitol Hill, where she has appeared before the Joint Econonomic Committee on the first Friday of every month to interpret the latest figures on the job market for Congress. In a city notorious for its backbiting, she is unusual in having virtually no detractors. At her 137th and final session, apparently a record, she was given perhaps the supreme compliment when Representative David R. Obey, a Wisconsin Democrat, called her "the most difficult witness to lead" that he had ever met.
The committee's hearings are politically charged, especially in election years, as members strive to get the commissioner to agree with their partisan notions of how the economy is performing. Dr. Norwood, though a Presidential appointee, has scrupulously defended the agency's mandate to collect and provide data objectively.
Appointed commissioner by Jimmy Carter in 1979, she was reappointed by Ronald Reagan in 1983 and 1987. Although political pressures never disappear, the commissioner contends that a repeat of an attempt in the Nixon Administration to tamper with the process would probably "backfire" because of the watchfulness of the Joint Economic Committee, the news media and the Bureau of Labor Statistics itself. The committee's monthly hearings were a direct reaction to a commissioner's decision in the early 1970's to cancel, at a time of rising joblessness, what had been a regular news conference.
More recently, when Washington was debating increases in the minimum wage, the bureau handily turned aside attempts by both Republicans and Democrats to elicit policy prescriptions, limiting its role to a review of the literature.
"Frequently there are requests for us to interpret things that we feel go too far," the commissioner said. "It is important for us to say no."
Dr. Norwood's nonpartisanship is personal as well as professional. She registered as an independent ever since she began to vote after her graduation from Douglass College in New Brunswick, N.J. "I prefer to look at the issues and the people and pick out the best ones -- and not vote by party," she said.
 
Survey of Payrolls
 When asked about her legacy apart from enhancing the independence of the agency, Commissioner Norwood mentions the stress she put on building up its data-gathering skills to match the talents of its economists and on reducing the traditional tension between the two disciplines.
She also speaks of her role in developing the Employment Cost Index, vastly improving the monthly survey of company payrolls -- its respondents are now beginning to punch in their responses by telephone or speak them to a computer -- and of two major changes to improve the reliability of the Consumer Price Index.
She and her husband, Bernard, an economist and once a senior Foreign Service officer, have some rental real estate and "a very few" stocks among their investments. Their portfolio has probably suffered, she said, from the fact that they must avoid even buying certificates of deposit during the several times a month when she has sensitive, market-moving information prior to its release.
The only other person outside the Labor Department to directly receive it the day before publication is the President's chief economic adviser, Michael J. Boskin, with whom Dr. Norwood discusses the major indicators the day before their release.
Although Commissioner Norwood will be writing, lecturing and serving on corporate boards, the couple will be able for the first time to enjoy extended periods at a lakeside home in Maine.

HEADLINE: Kerrey Retells War Story in Effort to Connect With the Voters

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
His audience could be the jobless, the elderly, high school students or business executives, but Senator Bob Kerrey always stops midway through his basic Presidential campaign speech, lowers his voice, and confides the central selling point of his campaign. 
 This is his journey from being a Navy commando during the Vietnam War, sure of his enormous physical capacity, feeling indestructable and even "a bit obnoxious," to lying helpless at the Philadelphia Naval Hospital. Mr. Kerrey recalls March 14, 1969, as if it were yesterday: he led a pre-dawn assault against an island held by the Vietcong and walked into a firefight, only to have a grenade shatter his right leg.
 "The United States of America saved my life," the Nebraska Democrat repeats to anyone who will listen. "The American people reached out to me and gave me health care, gave me educational opportunity. This campaign is a life-and-death issue to me. Make no mistake about it: we can save lives as a people."
That is the essence of how Mr. Kerrey is marketing himself as the best Democrat to send to the White House. By telling and retelling the compelling story of Vietnam, with no trace of bitterness, Mr. Kerrey hopes he will strike voters as a candidate who understands pain, understands struggle and understands what it takes to improve the plight of the people.
 
A Rough Entry
Candidate Has Yet To Be Specific
 While Mr. Kerrey's supporters remain convinced that he is the most marketable political comer in America, he has had a rough entry onto the national stage. He is in the anomalous position of being viewed as a candidate who could go all the way and yet during the autumn warm-up, he came be to be regarded as the underachiever of the field.
The signs of early trouble are all there: He had to apologize for an unseemly joke, endure bad publicity about his restaurant business and last week he overhauled his senior campaign staff.
These could be the pitfalls of running a campaign for President based on charisma.
Strip away the war record, many Democrats say, and Mr. Kerrey presents a hodgepodge of ideas with no coherent theme and few specifics. He is polished, looks good and has a war-hero record that is the envy of any politician, yet he has faced a struggle this fall to make a convincing case that he should be President. Two months after his formal announcement, the 48-year-old Medal of Honor winner still faces the task of demonstrating that he is not just another pretty face with an uplifting story.
 
Not Big on Details
 His challenge is all the more onerous because, by his own admission, Mr. Kerrey is more comfortable with sweeping generalizations than being pinned on details about making the Government work.
"One of my least favorite things is clarifying something I said," he said in an interview. Yet Mr. Kerrey seems aware that filling in the details is essential to a successful candidacy. "The real burden of running as a candidate is that when you're not well known, people can paint a picture with a fragment of information."
Setting himself apart from candidates like former Senator Paul E. Tsongas of Massachusetts and former Gov. Edmund G. Brown Jr. of California, who tend to dwell on details, Mr. Kerrey stubbornly clings to the notion that he cannot whittle down his ideas into clever phrases suitable for television "sound bites." Yet he concedes that his ideas do not always connect.
"I'm conscious that I've got a fairly complicated message and the more complicated it gets the more vague it sounds," he said. "When you're talking about fundamental change, rather than reel out 10 or 12 snappy programs, then it gets more difficult. I realize I've got to work so the audience understands who I am."
In a remarkable concession for a candidate who is basing his campaign on character, Mr. Kerrey was hard-pressed to say what should lead people to support him. "I don't assert that there is any special quality about me, in and of itself, that qualifies me to be President," he said. "What matters is the ideas, the values, the willingness to persevere toward the objective."
As Mr. Kerrey seeks to clarify his message, he is paying a price on the trail, sometimes disappointing would-be supporters.
"He's a good speaker," Danny Prats, a political science major at the University of New Hampshire, said after hearing Mr. Kerrey at a forum in Durham. "But there's little that distinguishes him from the other candidates per se, except for health care, and they're all talking about health care." Clutching his cap and gown, he went on: "I'm graduating in five days and I want someone who gives me more than ambiguity when they talk about a better job market."
 
High Expectations
 After addressing a group of unemployed people at a church in Concord, N.H., Mr. Kerrey chased down Wayne Cochran, an unemployed purchasing agent in the audience who voiced skepticism that the Senator's proposals would create jobs. But once he was face-to-face with Mr. Cochran, the candidate was reduced to Bush-speak: "Don't underestimate the importance of this restructuring thing I'm talking about."
Explaining how he would go about this, Mr. Kerrey offers the big picture: "First, we must reduce the size and cost of the Federal Government," he said. "And second, we must renew a productive investment strategy. And finally, we must establish new structures for a global marketplace which will allow the power and integrity of free markets to create a new global prosperity."
His advisers suggested that he is the victim of overblown expectations. "He came into the race as the golden boy with a sense that he was something special," said Sue Casey, who resigned last week as campaign manager after a staff shakeup. "As happens in every campaign and candidate, you have to come down with some reasonable expectations. In December, that's what happened."
Mr. Kerrey has also been held back by his own missteps. In November, unaware he was being recorded by a television microphone, he told an explicit joke about lesbians to another candidate at a political event in New Hampshire. He apologized after being attacked by homosexual and women's groups.
Another jolt late this month threatens to undermine Mr. Kerrey's efforts to portray himself as the only candidate with hands-on business experience. The Labor Department fined the restaurant chain in which he owns 35 percent for violations of labor law restrictions. Mr. Kerrey, who is chairman of the business, insisted that he has not been actively involved in its operations since he was elected Governor in 1982 and that the violations have been corrected.
 
A Compelling Record
War Record Gives A Special Status
 It Mr. Kerrey's war record, not his business, that has afforded him a special status in the political world. It is a story he seldom told before the current campaign (to be sure, it was always touted by his advisers). But adjusting to big league politics often leads candidates to newfound candor about personal tragedies, and Mr. Kerrey has been forced to sacrifice his timidity.
Michael McCurry, a senior adviser to Mr. Kerrey, said the Senator's aides prodded him to talk about a war experience that now forms the spine of his campaign. "It's delicate because you cannot run for President solely on a war record," he said. "Kerrey was trying to run on a basket of ideas that are important, but people did not see the connection between the strength of his character and the power of his ideas -- and they are connected."
Therein is the core psychology of Mr. Kerrey's campaign: he wants people to believe he is on a quest for change, a quest that is above bald political calculations. This element of his campaign has echoes of the most famous casualty of recent campaigns, Gary Hart.
The former Colorado Senator ran for President in 1984 as a candidate with "fresh ideas" but his 1988 campaign collapsed amid disclosures about his personal life. Some of Mr. Hart's former aides now work for Mr. Kerrey, and Mr. Hart said they may be because of similiarities in their messages.
"They do not see him as a traditional politician, or someone who plays the political game for his own advantage," Mr. Hart said. "He gives the impression that politics comes and goes and he can stand with or without it."
While Mr. Kerrey has taken on the Hart mantle, the policy centerpiece of his campaign is not particularly original: a universal health-care plan, including long-term care, that would be paid for largely through a payroll tax and higher levies on the wealthy. Ask him about the economy, about education, and he hammers away on his health-care theme, saying cutting the cost of private health care would allow businesses to compete more vigorously in international markets and generate more jobs at home.
 
Trouble With Business
 But Mr. Kerrey may run into trouble with his credentials on this issue. Officials with his restaurant chain, Grandmother's, say 75 to 100 of its 700 to 800 employees do not have health coverage. The company is within the law, since small businesses are not required to provide such insurance. But wage earners might well take Mr. Kerrey's call for payroll taxes as a stingy businessman's effort to avoid costs. And there is the appearance problem: Can an employer who does not insure his own employees be entrusted with creating a national health plan?
Asked his view on the morality of having employees with no health insurance, he curtly replied: "It's equally questionable if somebody sits in the United States Senate with free health care and then does not express outrage when 12 million children are living in poverty with no health care. To me, that's questionable morality."
After resisting questions about why his business did not provide health insurance for all its employees, Mr. Kerrey said: "I'm like every other small business in America. The answer is, it's unaffordable."
The consensus among professionals is that both Mr. Kerrey's message and his delivery need polishing. Some listeners detect Kennedyesque tones in his best speeches, and he can have an engaging sense of humor. But Mr. Kerrey rarely drives his audience to a rousing ovation.
 
An Unpredictable Man
Decision to Run Was a Quick One
 Mr. Kerrey's troubles connecting on a regular basis are particularly exasperating to his aides because sometimes he has produced riveting moments. In probably the most memorable exchange of the first debate of the Democratic contenders, he effectively cut off Mr. Brown, who was railing at length about special-interest donations to Congress.
In that same debate, though, Mr. Kerrey was clumsy in answering the first, most basic, question: Why did he want to be President? "I first of all made the decision with my family," he replied, "made the decision that I would like to be President of the United States. And then the next question was why. What would I do?"
Mr. Kerrey seemed to be saying that he made his decision on a whim, and figured out his issue stands only as an afterthought.
That is not too far from the truth. He delivered his announcement on Sept. 30, a month after shocking friends and advisers, who were sure he would not make the run.
"In the 15 months that I was there I did not have one conversation with him or as a group with senior staff with him, about running for President, not one," said Jeremy D. Rosner, the policy director on Mr. Kerrey's Senate staff who left in October after helping draft the Senator's announcement speech. So when the Senator told his advisers in late August that he would run, he said, "It sort of knocked the wind out of all of us, how decisive he was and how quickly he decided to get into it."
But Mr. Kerrey's snap decision is in some ways not surprising. He is an impatient, often unpredictable man. A political novice in 1982, he ran against Nebraska's Republican Governor and won in a state where most voters are Republicans. Though he was a popular Governor, Mr. Kerrey unexpectedly left office after one four-year term. He returned to politics in 1988 when he wrested a Senate seat from David K. Karnes, a Republican appointee who filled out a term after the death of Senator Edward J. Zorinsky.
Mr. Rosner and other advisers say the Senator's abrupt decision to run was partly because he was angered by what he regarded as signals from the White House this summer that Mr. Bush was willing to go along with the plotters of the coup against Mikhail S. Gorbachev.
Mr. Kerrey denies this, but he often challenges the President in what is widely viewed as Mr. Bush's strongest area, foreign policy. But Mr. Kerrey is vague about exactly what he would have done differently in dealing with, for example, the collapse of the Soviet Union. What he is offering so far is a critique of Mr. Bush without a concrete alternative.
"His foreign policy's grounded in something different from mine," Mr. Kerrey said of Mr. Bush. "I just find myself being moved by the accomplishments of the cold war to conclude that we have to continue to be strong advocates for freedom. I don't get the sense that the President sees that world out there 10 years from now dramatically transformed."
 
Relying on Hart Aides
 His late decision to run has forced him to assemble his organization on the fly, and initially, Mr. Kerrey relied heavily on top advisers to Mr. Hart. But in a move to give the campaign a lift, Mr. Kerrey last week announced a major staff shakeup. Ms. Casey, a veteran aide to Mr. Hart, resigned as campaign manager and Bill Hoppner, a close friend of the Senator's who was chief of staff under Mr. Kerrey as Governor, will remain as chairman but play less of a day-to-day role in the campaign. Another former Hart aide, Bill Shore, continues to be an important adviser.
Though Mr. Kerrey denies it, advisers said he was seeking to remedy perceptions that the campaign was floundering so he looked outside his staff and picked Tad Devine, who held senior positions in the 1988 Presidential drive of former Gov. Michael S. Dukakis of Massachusetts, to oversee his campaign.
The Kerrey campaign hopes to inherit from Mr. Hart the backing of the Hollywood "glitter money." Mr. Kerrey has aggressively sought financial support in California, where he is popular in part because of his on-again, off-again romance with the actress Debra Winger. Mr. Kerrey has also turned to his geographic base, counting on Nebraskans to donate heavily to his campaign.
The campaign will receive $570,000 in Federal matching subsidies in January, about the same amount as Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas but exceeded among Democrats only by Mr. Harkin, who will get $1.1 million. Ron Fried, Mr. Kerrey's finance director, said the campaign has collected close to $2 million.
No matter how much money he raises, no matter how compelling his biography, Mr. Kerrey must convince voters that he should be President. The man who hates to explain facts now faces what could be months of intense questioning about his proposals.
Maybe that is why he sounded almost fatalistic when he conceded something that few politicians like to admit: "In the end," he said, "it's possible that I don't catch on."

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
2 Big Retailers Reach Out to Minorities

BYLINE: By STUART ELLIOTT

 BODY:
HOPING to stimulate sales among minority shoppers, two of the largest retailers, the K Mart Corporation and Toys "R" Us Inc., have hired advertising agencies that specialize in marketing to blacks or Hispanic Americans.
K Mart, the second-largest retailer, after Wal-Mart Stores Inc., has appointed Burrell Advertising Inc. in Chicago for black-oriented advertising and the Castor Advertising Corporation in New York for Hispanic-oriented advertising. Toys "R" Us, the largest toy retailer, has appointed the Mingo Group Inc. in New York for its black-oriented advertising. 
 Both retailers have aimed sales messages at minority consumers via special advertising and promotional programs, but until now such work had been done by their general-market agencies, for K Mart, the Ross Roy Group in Bloomfield Hills, Mich., and for Toys "R" Us, J. Walter Thompson New York.
The hiring of minority agencies to produce and direct such marketing efforts goes beyond including members of minorities in general-market, mainstream advertising and underscores the increasing clout of black consumers, who spend an estimated $250 billion a year, and Spanish-speaking consumers, who spend about $175 billion a year.
These minorities make up a growing proportion of the overall population, and retailers anticipate that by singling out minority markets for special attention -- in the same manner in which they direct special pitches to older consumers, teen-agers or single-person households -- they might generate additional revenues amid a stalled economy.
"Each year, the importance of these markets continues to expand," said Gerald K. Habeck, K Mart's vice president of advertising, "so we felt we had to have African-American advertising directed toward the African-American community, and Hispanic advertising directed toward the Hispanic community."
That is particularly true in the wake of the disappointing holiday sales season, said Samuel J. Chisholm, the Mingo Group's president.
Retailers "look at this tough Christmas and ask, 'Where do I go from here?' " he added.
Mr. Chisholm declined to discuss specific aspects of the coming Toys "R" Us campaign, but he indicated that efforts to build "credibility and believability and trust" among black consumers would be at its heart.
K Mart's black-oriented campaign, which began earlier this month, involves print advertisements in magazines like Ebony and Essence, and radio commercials, running on black-oriented stations in markets like Chicago and Detroit. The focus is on K Mart's clothing, via a slogan, "Looking good," absent from the company's general-market ads.
That reflects a belief that to black shoppers "apparel is a little more important," said Charles H. Curry, Burrell's president. Thus, he added, it is necessary "to get the black consumer to start thinking about K Mart as a place where you can get more than just paper towels and motor oil."
One radio spot, called "All My Secrets," typifies Burrell's tack. "Check this out and don't tell anyone," a woman says in a casual, colloquial soliloquy. "I went to K Mart the other day to get a lamp and accidentally went down the wrong aisle."
"Uh-huh, the one where the clothes are," she adds. "Girl, I couldn't believe my eyes. I went out and looked at the store name again. It was K Mart, all right."
In the Hispanic campaign, television commercials, which run during programs on Spanish-language networks, show a family shopping at K Mart, unlike general-market spots, which show a husband and wife, sans children, or two teen-aged friends, sans parents.
Hector Elizalde, Castor's senior executive vice president and chief operating officer, said that reflected a tendency on the part of Hispanic consumers "to go to the store as a family unit." The spots also emphasize the brand names K Mart sells, because research has suggested Hispanic consumers value name-brand products.
Selecting minorities for pitches for cigarettes, malt liquor and other products perceived as harmful has brought outcries. There is little debate, however, over aiming ad messages at black or Hispanic audiences when the messages are no more than invitations to participate in the nation's consumption culture.
Among the country's largest retailers, several, like Sears, Roebuck & Company and the Dayton Hudson Corporation, already have on their agency rosters shops that specialize in minority marketing, as have consumer-product companies, like the Procter & Gamble Company, Maybelline and RJR Nabisco Inc.
Though "other people have had a head start," said Frederick Marx, president of Marx Layne & Company, a retail consulting firm in Farmington Hills, Mich., these new campaigns make sense, he added, given the shifts in shopper demographics.

HEADLINE: The Rally In Stocks Accelerates

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON Jr.

 BODY:
The stock market's year-end rally accelerated yesterday as investors eagerly joined a buying rush, and several market indicators set records for the third consecutive session.
The benchmark Dow Jones industrial average surged 62.39 points, to a new high of 3,163.91. In December, the Dow has soared 269.23 points -- or 9.3 percent -- making this month the best December ever. The previous December record of 8.4 percent was set in 1924. 
 "It's a mini-stampede," said Charles Clough, chief investment strategist at Merrill Lynch. "Everybody's caught on. This is real, and it's going to go on."
 
'Running at Breakneck Speed'
 "The market is running at breakneck speed," added William Dodge, chief investment strategist for Dean Witter.
Both analysts cited the multiple, ever-extending benefits of low interest rates as the major factor in the market boom. Investors clamored to pour money into mutual funds, and fund managers to invest in the market.
Volume on the Big Board was heavy yesterday, at 245.6 million shares, and gaining issues led losing ones by 1,387 to 460. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index also set a record, up 8.68 points, to 415.14, as did the Nasdaq composite, up 14.04 points, to 579.75, as smaller stocks soared as well.
The American Stock Exchange index rose 7.21 points, to 390.15, and the Russell Index of 2,000 stocks rose 3.62 points, to 187.85.
 
Collar on Program Trading
 Once the Dow rose more than 50 points, at 3:12 P.M., the New York Stock Exchange's collar on index arbitrage took effect, limiting program trading. Yet the market continued to climb apace, closing at the day's high.
Mr. Clough said he felt that a fundamental change in the market lay in the growing sense among investors that short-term interest rates -- which collapsed this year -- would remain low in the coming year as borrowing remained slack.
Besieged in recent days with telephone calls from individual investors, mutual fund executives said that the striking movement of cash into mutual funds began early this month, although some could not yet provide firm figures for the current rally.
At the Dreyfus Corporation, a major mutual fund organization, $150 million flowed into the equity funds in the first two weeks of December; by contrast, the firm registerd a net outflow of $7 million in November.
"In the first two weeks, $56 million flowed into the Dreyfus Growth Opportunity Fund alone," said Diane Coffey, director of corporate communications at Dreyfus. That figure, she said, represented 10 percent of the fund's total Nov. 30 assets of $528 million.
 
Lobby Jammed in Valley Forge
 Brian S. Mattes of the Vanguard Group in Valley Forge, Pa., said his firm had seen a similar surge of consumer interest yesterday. In the Valley Forge office, despite a light snow, the lobby was jammed with investors who came in person for most of the day, he added.
"And the call volume is just extraordinary," Mr. Mattes said. "The level is well in excess of what we saw in the crash of 1987."
Michael Hines, senior vice president of marketing for Fidelity Investments, the mutual fund giant, said that as of Dec. 27, his firm's net sales for the month were three times the November figure and the strongest for any single month since 1987.
Michael Metz, chief investment strategist at Oppenheimer & Company, noted that in addition to the investors who bought for want of attractive alternatives, there was a sizable movement out of short positions by traders who had expected particular stocks to drop.
"You're seeing one of the greatest short squeezes of all time," Mr. Metz said.
Another factor is that lower interest rates mean that less needs to be spent on debt service, both by companies and individuals, freeing up money. People who refinanced their home mortgages, for example, have larger disposable incomes to spend. Mr. Dodge said estimates on the amount of cash from home mortgage refinancing ranged from $20 billion to $50 billion.
"You don't need that money coming directly into the market," said Rao Chalassani, chief investment strategist at Kemper Securities Group. It increases consumer confidence, saving and spending, he noted.
 
Shifts in Portfolio Models
 On Dec. 23, Mr. Chalassani said, Kemper adjusted the models upon which it bases the breakdown of investments in stocks, bonds and cash. In Kemper's "aggressive" porfolio, he continued, a ratio of 70 percent stocks, 20 percent bonds, 10 percent cash had been adopted to replace the previous ratio of 55-20-25.
At Dean Witter, Mr. Dodge said, the asset model was shifted on Dec. 20 -- about an hour after the Fed's announcement -- to 65 percent stocks, 35 percent bonds and zero cash, from a previous 55-35-10.
"Cash is trash," Mr. Dodge said.
At Merrill Lynch, Mr. Clough said, the asset model in aggressive portfolios was shifted slightly in early December, to 65 percent stocks, 35 percent bonds and zero cash, from an earlier ratio of 65-30-5.
Mr. Metz said he was not shifting his firm's investment emphasis toward equity. "We still think that bonds are attractive," he added.
Among the most heavily traded stocks were General Electric, up 2 7/8, to 76 7/8, on 2.7 million shares traded; Philip Morris, up 3 3/8, to 80 5/8, on 2.7 million, and RJR Nabisco, up 3/8, to 11 1/8, on 4.6 million.
Other gainers included Allied-Signal, up 2 7/8, to 44 1/8; Goodyear Tire, up 3 1/2, to 53 3/4, and Procter & Gamble, up 3 3/8, to 93 3/4. Time Warner was up 2 1/4, to 87 1/4, and First Financial Management rose 3, to 42.
In other markets, Crop Genetics International rose 2 1/2, to 10 1/4, after Friday's announcement that the company had signed an agreement with the Du Pont Company for the joint commercialization of insecticidal virus products. The agreement calls for the two companies to develop, produce and market a broad range of viruses for insect control.
Bolar Pharmaceutical climbed 2 3/8, to 12 1/2, on what an analyst called renewed takeover speculation.

SECTION: Section D; Page 2; Column 1; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 848 words

HEADLINE: Business Scene;
Creating a Market In Yeltsin's Image

BYLINE: By Louis Uchitelle

 BODY:
ALTHOUGH the Soviet Union no longer exists, its national economy limps on, with Boris N. Yeltsin, Russia's president, pretty much at the helm. And now a battle royal is developing over these questions: Should he share control with the 11 other independent republics? If the republics cannot agree on common policies, should the national economy, like the union itself, be chopped up into pieces?
The first answers to these bedrock questions are coming this week with Mr. Yeltsin's decision to lift price ceilings in Russia. Unless he changes his mind, or the other republics take vigorous counteraction, rising prices in Mr. Yeltsin's republic will set off a chain of events that will force up prices throughout the old union. 
 "It may even be convenient for other politicians in other republics to say to their people, 'I would not do this to you guys, but Mr. Yeltsin's actions leave no choice,' " said Stanley Fisher, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology and former chief economist at the International Monetary Fund.
Whatever the reaction, for the time being, Mr. Yeltsin's administration, more than any other republic's, has in its hands the power to steer the national economy. The reason is that the Russian economy casts a shadow the other republics cannot easily escape, accounting for half the new commonwealth's population and nearly two-thirds of the old union's still intertwined economic activity.
If the price of a shirt, for example, rises in Russia to, say, 100 rubles, then shirt makers in Ukraine or Belarus are going to ship to the huge Russian market, rather than accept a still controlled 10 rubles at home. Belarus has already announced the lifting of price ceilings on Friday, the day after Russia acts. And President Leonid M. Kravchuk of Ukraine said yesterday that prices would be allowed to rise in his republic on Jan. 10.
The Ukrainians, the most outspoken critics of Mr. Yeltsin's action, complain among other things that the Russians are failing to supply enough rubles to handle not only the higher prices they are forcing on the republics but also the higher wages that will inevitably result. Despite their political independence, the republics all still use the ruble -- and the Yeltsin Government temporarily controls the printing of rubles.
Among the industrial nations, including the United States, the rooting is for Mr. Yeltsin's initiative on prices. The International Monetary Fund, the proxy for these nations, supports Mr. Yeltsin's action as an important step in forcing all the republics toward a free market system; one in which prices for all but some basic items, including oil, would be allowed to respond to demand.
The hope, of course, is that reality will duplicate economic theory: After an initial spurt of inflation, supply would catch up to demand as producers rush to cash in on rising prices, and then prices would level off. In the process, entrepreneurs and good managers would flourish, and the high-cost, least-efficient producers would disappear -- forced out of business because their costs would rise faster than the prices they could get for their shirts, refrigerators or other goods. What is more, the ruble's enhanced purchasing power would, in theory, make it a desirable currency to hold.
 The main obstacle to the Yeltsin approach is Ukraine. Since the two republics together represent roughly 80 percent of the republics' economic activity, "if they could work out a kind of dominion over economic policy, that would set the pace for the commonwealth," said C. Fred Bergsten, president of the Institute for International Economics.
Ukrainian opposition has already made Mr. Yeltsin cautious, contributing to his decision to postpone the lifting of price ceilings from Dec. 16 to Thursday. But if the Ukrainians hold back, and the Kiev shirt maker ships his goods to Moscow, the Ukrainians must either give ground, raising prices and wages, or resist by putting up export barriers and creating their own currency to replace the ruble -- in effect separating their economy.
Already, Ukraine has moved mildly in this direction. But pursuing such a course can be futile and dangerous, given how intertwined the Ukrainian economy is with the others, particularly Russia's. As an example, Gennadi I. Oudovenko, Ukraine's representative to the United Nations, cites the diesel locomotive factory in Lugansk, which assembles 98 percent of the locomotives used in the old Soviet Union. Most of the parts and materials come from Russia and, to a lesser extent, from the other republics.
"If the prices we pay for these items rise, our locomotive prices will also have to rise," Mr. Oudovenko said.
Such leverage gives Mr. Yeltsin a huge say in economic policy for the commonwealth, but not necessarily the last word. Disrupting the flow of Ukraine's rich supply of industrial and agricultural products is not healthy for Russia either. "We don't want the Russians to decide, but to coordinate with us, and for the time being that seems to be happening," Mr. Oudovenko said.

HEADLINE: THE YEAR IN FINANCE: NEWSMAKERS IN 1991;
Money Movers Caught in the Turbulent Times of the 90's;
Nicholas F. Brady: A Legislative Agenda Fails

BYLINE: By STEPHEN LABATON

 BODY:
Last December, when Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady was asked on a Sunday television news show about the prospect of a recession, he shrugged it off, saying that it was "no big deal."
Now curing the economic slump has become a matter of self-preservation for the Bush Administration, and Mr. Brady has become enmeshed in an internal struggle over what to do, if anything at all. 
 It has been a politically difficult year for Mr. Brady, a genial figure who found himself in Congress more than he wanted to ask for large bailouts for the nation's banks and savings and loans and being accused by some Democrats of concealing the true magnitude of the problems of those two industries.
 His biggest loss was a pivotal opportunity to use the plight of the bank insurance fund to overhaul the nation's banking system by erasing decades of restrictions.
Congress rejected his plan across the board to give banks greater authority to expand into new businesses and across state lines, and instead just provided new money for the insurance fund and tougher laws that give regulators greater powers.
In the face of a $70 billion taxpayer loan to keep afloat the bank insurance fund, he has dropped his oft-quoted phrase that the bank and savings and loan bailouts are "as different as chalk and cheese."
While his legislative agenda failed this year, it is impossible for an outsider to accurately measure Mr. Brady's successes because he has staked out a role in the Administration as the President's close friend and repository of confidences.
His power continues to come from his ability to saunter across the street to talk to Mr. Bush whenever he wants.

HEADLINE: Owners of Times Sq. Tower Seek Bankruptcy Protection

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP

 BODY:
After three years in which they signed only one tenant for their 42-story tower in the theater district of Manhattan, the owners of 1585 Broadway have filed for reorganization under Chapter 11 of the Federal Bankruptcy Code.
The silvery skyscraper, between 47th and 48th Streets, is owned by 1585 Broadway Associates, a partnership controlled by David S. Solomon. He also developed a nearby tower at 750 Seventh Avenue, which lost its sole office tenant in mid-November. 
 "The filing of this petition is not a hostile act," Mr. Solomon said yesterday in a telephone interview. "And it is in keeping with our cooperative effort with the lending group toward a successful prepackaged reorganization of the partnership's assets.
He added: "We view it very positively. It will provide the economic strength to finish the leasing of the building."
Under a "prepackaged" bankruptcy, an agreement is reached before the Chapter 11 filing with senior creditors on the terms of a settlement and a restructuring of the debt.
Mr. Solomon identified the lenders, with whom he had been negotiating for a year and a half, as the Toronto Dominion Bank, the Swiss Bank Corporation and the Bank of Montreal.
In its bankruptcy petition, the 1585 Broadway partnership listed assets of $231 million, chiefly the building and land, and debts of $482 million, said Warren A. Schad, chief financial officer and executive vice president of Solomon Equities. The property is losing $1.9 million a month.
The petition was filed last week and was reported yesterday by American Banker, a trade publication. Mr. Schad said a hearing was scheduled for today on a temporary debtor-in-possession plan under which the lenders finance the building's continuing operation.
At almost 1.35 million square feet, 1585 Broadway is one of the largest of a crop of new buildings that rose in and around Times Square in the late 1980's, under zoning incentives meant to push development to the west side of mid-Manhattan from the east.
Because their completion roughly coincided with a collapse in demand for office space, these and other new buildings in the city are standing vacant, or nearly so. A number of owners have already filed for bankruptcy protection.
At 1585 Broadway, the law firm of Proskauer, Rose, Goetz & Mendelsohn signed a lease in 1988 and moved into the building in 1990. The firm now occupies 340,000 square feet and is to expand to 460,000 square feet over four years. But the law firm is the only tenant.
Aggravating the financial problems, Mr. Solomon said, is that "we've seen a major decline in rents and an increase in tenants' expectations for work letters in recent months."
Indeed, Mr. Schad said, the cost of improvements for tenants specified in these work letters has risen to $75 a square foot, from $25 a square foot when the tower was being planned.
Mr. Solomon said the reorganization would "provide any tenant with the knowledge that the lending group will be there to provide appropriate capital to get tenants into the building."
About his building at 750 Seventh Avenue, at 49th Street, Mr. Solomon would say only, "We're working toward a successful resolution with the lending group," which is led by Citicorp.
Olwine, Connelly, Chase, O'Donnell & Wehyer, a law firm that was the single office tenant at 750 Seventh Avenue, disbanded and left the building in mid-November.

HEADLINE: Dinkins Plans Steps to Spur City Economy

BYLINE: By JAMES C. McKINLEY Jr.

 BODY:
Mayor David N. Dinkins, confronting growing budget deficits and a lingering recession as he nears the midpoint of his term, will use his State of the City address Thursday night to announce several measures to invigorate New York City's ailing local economy.
Speaking to a city troubled by economic stagnation, Mr. Dinkins will focus on actions his government can take to stimulate the private sector, like speeding payments to companies that do business with the city, relaxing licensing rules and pushing some capital projects ahead of schedule, mayoral aides said. 
 The speech affords the Mayor the chance to present a long-term vision for fiscal stability. The challenges he faces include obtaining concessions from municipal labor unions to increase productivity and limit salary increases, and convincing state monitors and bond-rating agencies of the soundness of his plans to bridge $7 billion in projected budget shortfalls over the next five years.
Critics of the Mayor's budget policies say he must also allay the fears of a restive electorate concerned about the city's future.
 
The 'Real Challenge'
 "The Mayor's real challenge," said Felix G. Rohatyn, the chairman of the state's Municipal Assistance Corporation, "is to talk to the frustrations of the people who want to leave the city and the despair of those who are condemned to stay. One way or another he has to convince both of these groups to give him time."
Many of those in the political square dance at City Hall say they will be watching to see how the speech addresses the stumbling blocks, great and small, that have plagued the Dinkins administration, from organizational disarray in his inner circle of advisers to strained relations with the City Council leadership.
Perhaps the greatest of those hurdles has been the administration's failure to win support from municipal unions for work-rule changes and drastic cuts in the work force. Those concessions are crucial to closing nearly the projected budget shortfalls over the next five years.
After gaining office with broad union support and granting a round of pay raises, Mr. Dinkins has so far been unable to persuade the union leaders to help him save money now.
For their part, union leaders complain that the Mayor's top aides have not consulted them on ways to make workers more productive and have instead made decisions unilaterally and sprung them on the workers. They also are critical of what they see as the Mayor's inconsistent policy of laying off more than 7,100 workers in hospitals, libraries and agencies like the Parks Department while hiring thousands of new police officers.
"There's a terrible, terrible feeling toward the Mayor right now," said Stanley Hill, the president of the District Council 37 of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees. "It's going to be very difficult for the simple reason there is still some waste and fat in the Police Department. What the members see is Stanley Hill taking the heavy hits and no one else sharing the pain."
 
Labor's Cooperation Needed
 Without labor's cooperation, the Mayor will have a hard time convincing the State Financial Control Board that the administration's five-year spending plan is sound. And without the control board's stamp of approval, the Mayor cannot persuade the Municipal Assistance Corporation to provide $1 billion in aid by stretching out the payments on outstanding bonds maturing this year.
The Mayor's preliminary plan, unveiled on Nov. 6, depended heavily on the M.A.C. aid to finance a two-year tax freeze. It also said any wage increases would be financed by the unions through increased productivity.
But Mr. Rohatyn, the M.A.C. chairman, has said he will not refinance the bonds unless the control board determines the Mayor's plans are sound. "Any plan the city submits with work-force reductions has to include the cooperation and support of the municipal labor unions," he said.
The Mayor is to release a more detailed, revised financial plan on Jan. 16. The Financial Control Board, set up in the 1970's to oversee city budgeting, can vote to take over city finances if the Mayor fails to balance the budget.
Another challenge facing the Mayor will be to mend fences with the newly empowered and expanded City Council. The Council has been at loggerheads with the Mayor over budget priorities and the administration's plan to scatter small homeless shelters throughout the city. In the last year, relations between Mr. Dinkins and Council Speaker Peter F. Vallone have been anything but cooperative.
 
A Strained Relationship
 Complaining that the Mayor does not yet treat the Council as an equal partner in government, Mr. Vallone has asserted the Council's independence by overriding the Mayor's veto of a grocery item-pricing bill and, more recently, threatening to hold up a package of midyear budget changes until certain programs were restored.
"There is someone on that side of City Hall who thinks that we are tresspassing when we try to make a suggestion," Mr. Vallone said. "The solution is just to recognize you have to sit down with an equal partner and work."
To a large degree, Mr. Dinkins has had a rocky tenure because it has been a challenging time to be Mayor. From the moment he took office, the city has been punished by a national recession that has shriveled tax revenues, driven unemployment up and swelled the welfare rolls to levels not seen in a decade.
So the Mayor, who cobbled together a liberal coalition with the promise of a "new path to progress," has presided over layoffs, service cuts and a get-tough policy on crime. Unlike city officials in the 1970's, Mr. Dinkins has been unable to turn to Washington or Albany for help because the Republican Administration of President Bush has retreated from social spending and the state's budget is also in a shambles.
Of Mr. Dinkins, Deputy Mayor Milton Mollen said, "He came in with a progressive program, and at every turn he's been constantly hampered by the financial problems. It's very frustrating."
Still, the Mayor's critics and even some supporters say he has yet to articulate a clear vision of how to lead the city out of the woods, and his cautious, low-key style has not inspired New Yorkers to weather the recession.
"People do need to feel there is hope," said Sandra Feldman, president of the United Federation of Teachers and one of Mr. Dinkins's early supporters. "Part of what's been a problem this whole time is there hasn't been an articulation of a clear plan by the administration for getting through this. There isn't a feeling that this or that program is a priority, except for cops. It's been diffuse."
The Mayor insists he has not let his supporters down. "I don't know that there are so many people who liked me a lot who are so disappointed," he said in a year-end interview, "because I encounter people every day, polls notwithstanding, who will come up to me and tell me what a great job they think I'm doing."
 
Drop in Approval Rating
 The Mayor's approval rating in polls has dropped in recent months to below 30 percent, and some political strategists say his falling popularity stems from trying to please too many irreconciable groups at once, from business leaders demanding tax relief to fiscal monitors asking for huge work-force cuts to progressives urging him not turn his back on his liberal roots.
"The worst thing in the world is to spread the grief out evenly because then everyone hates you," Norman Adler, a political consultant, said. "It's better to rob Peter to pay Paul because at least you have the gratitude of Paul."
Many inside the city government say the Mayor's biggest tasks in the second half of his four-year term will be to find an inner circle of deputy mayors and advisers who click, something that has eluded him. He has the same team of five deputies he took office with, though a sixth, Cesar A. Perales, will join the administration next month to oversee health agencies, and his finance deputy, Sally B. Hernandez-Pinero, intends to step down.
Mr. Mollen and the First Deputy Mayor, Norman Steisel, have been seeking positions outside government, their associates say, but both men brush off suggestions they may leave soon. The Mayor's task is made more difficult by the stormy departures of the head of the city hospital system, the Sanitation Department and the jail system. Whatever decisions he makes will determine the team that will take him through his re-election campaign in 1993.
The political test for the Mayor will be to rebuild the coalition that elected him by a two-percentage point margin in 1989. Many Hispanic voters, a pillar of his support, complain that he has not appointed enough Hispanic officials to senior posts and has been insensitive to their concerns. Black New Yorkers gave him more than 90 percent of their votes, but a third now disapprove of his performance in a recent New York Times/WCBS-TV Poll.
Mr. Dinkins maintains that when voters are forced to compare him to his probable opponents, City Council President Andrew J. Stein, a Democrat who has been courting conservative voters, and Rudolph W. Giuliani, a Republican, his liberal supporters will not desert him.
"I think in time, as we address these issues and people come to understand the rationale therefore, people will look at this administration and what we have accomplished," Mr. Dinkins said. "They'll weigh that against some people who will promise, because that's about all they can do is promise."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Some political strategists say Mayor David N. Dinkins's falling popularity stems from trying to please too many irreconcilable groups at once. "I don't know that there are so many people who like me a lot who are so disappointed," said Mr. Dinkins, during a year-end interview in his office at City Hall. (John Sotomayor/The New York Times) (pg. B2)

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Owens-Corning Names Chairman

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER

 BODY:
The Owens-Corning Fiberglas Corporation said yesterday that a General Electric executive, Glen H. Hiner, would succeed its chairman and chief executive, Max O. Weber, next month. Mr. Weber has cancer.
Mr. Hiner, who is 57 years old and runs G.E.'s plastics operations, will be moving from one of the nation's strongest industrial giants to a company respected for its operations but saddled with a weak balance sheet and huge asbestos liabilities. 
 Like G.E., Owens-Corning is an industry leader in most of its businesses, with a reputation for strong management. The company, based in Toledo, Ohio, is the nation's leading producer of fiberglass insulation and roofing materials and supplier of fiberglass reinforcement materials to the auto, boating and appliance markets. In the first nine months of this year, it earned $19 million on sales of $2.12 billion.
 
Asbestos Cases and Debt
 "It's a super company," said Donald Pattison, who follows Owens-Corning for C.J. Lawrence, Morgan Grenfell Inc. "But asbestos liabilities will be the prime determinant of the company's earnings and stock performance over the next few years, regardless of who is running it."
Owens-Corning is still paying large debts piled up in fighting off a takeover attempt by the Wickes Companies in 1986. The company has strong cash flow from its operations and has been gaining market share in its major businesses. And it has earned admiration on Wall Street for its progress in reducing its debt to about $1.2 billion from almost $2.6 billion.
One of the first problems Mr. Hiner will face is the impact of the weakening economy. Some analysts now expect that fourth-quarter earnings, which will be reported shortly after Mr. Hiner takes over on Jan. 23, to be about half of what they had been predicted three months ago. But falling interest rates should stimulate the major markets of the company and help it manage its debt.
That leaves asbestos as the problem that really spooks Wall Street. Owens-Corning shocked investors in October and lost more than a third of its market value in two days after it announced that it needed to increase sharply the amount it would set aside from earnings in the coming years to cover expenses from the tens of thousands of asbestos cases it faced. Yesterday, in trading on the New York Stock Exchange, the company's shares rose $1.125 to close at $21.
Owens's asbestos liability stems from the company's sales of pipe insulation containing the hazardous mineral to shipbuilders, a business that ended in 1972. Analysts say the company has been paying an average of $9,500 to dispose of each asbestos claim in the last year. In 1990 alone, the company paid $117 million in asbestos claims. Most of the costs have been covered by insurance, but that coverage is likely to be exhausted by 1996 or 1997. More than 85,000 cases are outstanding, and the number continues to grow.
Mr. Weber, who is 62, joined Owens-Corning as an insulation salesman in 1955. He rose steadily through ranks to become president and chief operating officer in 1988. He was named chairman and chief executive just over a year ago when William W. Boeschenstein retired.
Mr. Weber's replacement, Mr. Hiner, said he had been briefed in detail by Owens-Corning's corporate and outside lawyers about the asbestos situation before accepting the job. He added that he was willing to consider suggestions from Wall Street that the company attempt to assess its total future liability and take one huge write-off to cover it instead of making numerous smaller adjustments.
Mr. Hiner described himself as "a touch and feel kind of guy who doesn't like getting information from reports."
"My orientation," he said, "is toward the marketplace, although my educational background is in electrical engineering. Market share is a big concern of mine."
 
G.E. as Recruiting Ground
 G.E.'s widely admired managerial ranks have been a frequent stopping place for recruiters seeking help for troubled or suddenly leaderless companies. Mr. Hiner's departure comes just six months after his former boss, Lawrence Bossidy, was lured from G.E. to the chief executive's position at Allied-Signal.
Mr. Hiner joined G.E. after graduating from West Virginia University in 1957 and, with the exception of three years in the Air Force, has been there ever since. He has headed G.E.'s plastics business since 1978.
Before taking over in Pittsfield, Mr. Hiner had managed the plastic business's European operations. He said yesterday that he thought his international experience appealed to Owens-Corning, which had begun discussions with him last summer.
Mr. Hiner said the progress of his negotiations had been determined largely by Mr. Weber's deteriorating health and had accelerated last month.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Ends Slightly Higher In Trading Before Holiday

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
The dollar closed slightly higher against major European currencies in slow trading yesterday in New York, with many dealers on vacation ahead of the New Year's holiday.
 The dollar rose to 1.5210 German marks from Friday's close of 1.5185. Against the Japanese yen, it fell to 125.55 from Friday's 125.93.
 Robert White of the First Interstate Bank in Los Angeles said it was hard to come up with any reasons to sell the dollar, as bearish sentiment from the drop in United States interest rates was offset by higher bond and stock prices.
 The Dow Jones industrial average soared 62.39 points, or 2 percent, to close at a record 3,163.91. Bonds also moved higher.
 
Japan Move Expected
 Early yesterday Japan cut its discount rate to 4 1/2 percent from 5 percent. Mr. White said the cut was a surprise only in the timing and had little impact on the dollar. Many dealers had expected a cut after the first of the year.
 News that United States consumer confidence remained essentially level at 52.4 in December, compared with 52.7 a month earlier, and that a barometer of the Chicago area's industrial economy fell sharply also had little impact.
 "The confidence index was not as low as people were expecting it to be," said Frank Cahill at Bank of New York.
 Officials at the Conference Board, a business-supported research group that released the survey, said the virtually flat reading, following big drops in October and November, was relatively good news.
 "We're seeing next to nothing going on," said Steve Flynn, a corporate dealer at First National Bank of Boston. "The market pretty much shrugged off everything," he said.
 Market participants believe any trade package negotiated by the United States and Japan during President Bush's coming visit there will include an agreement to strengthen the yen, said Rick Porter, vice president of the Fuji Bank in New York.
 Traders are reluctant, however, to sell significant amounts of dollars against the yen, said Richard Vullo, senior marketing representative at the Bank of Montreal in New York.

SECTION: Section D; Page 2; Column 5; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 682 words

HEADLINE: Survey of Confidence Shows Little Change

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
Consumer spirits, which tumbled in October and November to recession lows, did not worsen in December, according to the latest survey of consumer confidence by the Conference Board.
"We've leveled off," Fabian Linden, executive director of the Conference Board's Consumer Research Center, said yesterday. "The bad news is that we're still a very disenchanted population." 
 The Conference Board's index of consumer confidence, based on a monthly survey of 5,000 households around the nation, stood at 52.4 in December, compared with 52.7 in November. But the dip of three-tenths of a percentage point does not signify a true change, Mr. Linden said, because any movement in the index of less than a full point reflects "normal static in the data."
 
 
Rate Cut Came Later
 The survey results echo an earlier report on consumer confidence from the University of Michigan, but both reports mostly reflect the mood of consumers before they heard two significant -- and offsetting -- pieces of economic news: the sweeping job cuts announced on Dec. 18 by the General Motors Corporation and the Federal Reserve's reduction in the discount rate on Dec. 20.
The results were based on responses from the first 18 or so days of December, and thus do not measure attitudes during the last days of the month. So it is quite possible that the Conference Board's index for December will be revised next month, although it is unclear whether G.M.'s cuts or the Fed's move will hold greater sway with those surveyed.
 
 
More Hope for the Future
 Consumers were more downbeat in December than in November about current economic conditions, especially job prospects, and less inclined to make major purchases like cars or appliances. But at the same time they were more hopeful than a month earlier that the economy would be in better shape six months from now.
One important barometer of the mood of consumers, existing home sales, rose in November, the National Association of Reators said yesterday. Home resales jumped 5.4 percent, the biggest rise in 9 months, after climbing 1 percent in October. Home buyers, especially in the South and Midwest, are able to take advantage of the lowest mortgage rates in 14 years.
Nonetheless, the December confidence reading from the Conference Board shows that consumers are as depressed now as they have been at any time in the last couple of decades, including the depths of the 1981-82 recession, when unemployment was in the double digits. The jobless rate last month was 6.8 percent.
 
 
Less Job Security
 A fading sense of job security, even at the cream of corporations and in top-paying white-collar professions, is to blame, economists say.
"The problem is much more pervasive than people actually losing jobs," said Neal Soss, the chief economist of First Boston, who sees the entire postwar economic structure being dismantled just as surely as the ending of the cold war.
"Corporations can't be viewed as loyal," he said. "Workers can't have as much confidence in the future."
And consumers' spirits are unlikely to revive for some months, he added. "Until companies fix up their balance sheets, employers can't restore a sense of security," Mr. Soss said. But the Federal Reserve's recent action should help eventually, because "declines in interest rates are the needed therapy because they let companies fix up their balance sheets."
 
 
Little Effect on Spending
 What does the gloomy mood imply for spending in the year's final quarter?
"Consumer spending figures don't follow the confidence numbers," said Edward McKelvey, an economist at Goldman, Sachs & Company. Partly, he said, that is because consumers devote half of their budgets to services like utilities and medical care.
Mr. McKelvey said he thought spending in the fourth quarter stayed roughly even with that of the third quarter. "The spending figures are not behaving the way you'd expect them to," he said, noting that the collapse in confidence indicated in the October and November surveys had not translated into lower expenditures.

HEADLINE: In 1991, a Mixture Of Caution and Elan

BYLINE: By BERNADINE MORRIS

 BODY:
The state of the hemline was a subject agitating both designers and retailers during the last year. Consumers seemed relatively unconcerned.
During the five-year run of the miniskirt they had done pretty much what they wanted. If they liked the way they looked in short skirts, they wore them. If they felt they were too old, too serious or too fat, they avoided them. As simple as that. 
 Designers made it easy. Though they bared the knees, sometimes by a wide margin, in their fashion shows, they shipped the styles longer to the stores, long enough to cover the knees in many cases.
After their long exposure to short skirts, women appeared to be getting accustomed to them. This winter more knees were bared than ever before in the current run of miniskirts. Still, a change seemed to be on the way. In their spring collections, shown in October and November, designers began testing deeper waters. About 10 percent of the styles in many collections descended to the calf or beyond.
 
Can Long Be Sexy?

 
 Several factors were holding designers back from an all-out thrust. First, there was the perception that short skirts were uncluttered, easy to move around in and a completely contemporary idiom. Then there was the problem of making longer styles as lively, young and, yes, sexy as the shorter ones. A few designers said they were stumped by the idea of making full long skirts look appealing. Most chose narrow skirts split high at the front or sides to show the legs. Others chose thin, diaphanous fabrics so the legs would not be completely concealed.
But perhaps the biggest hindrance to change was the economic situation. In 1987 when the stock market dropped, the fashion business did not suffer unduly for people continued to buy new clothes: they still wanted to look prosperous. In the last year, a different attitude developed. Besides the reality of the economic bind, many women did not want to appear showy: it did not seem to be good form. Not only did they demand lower prices, but they bragged about their purchases at discount stores, sample sales and from markdown racks.
At the beginning of the year retailers appeared undecided about whether to stress safe, classic clothes or to seek original, even outrageous designs. By the end of the year a compromise evolved. Fresh design ideas were necessary to stimulate business, many retailers now believed, but the clothes couldn't be too wild. A major change, like the lengthening of skirts, was considered too upsetting by many stores.
The fashion action for the year began on a somber note, with the Paris couture shows taking place in the midst of the Persian Gulf war. The Japanese and American buyers were noticeably absent and the atmosphere of the shows was workmanlike rather than celebratory. Significantly, the most talked about individual style was a floor-length evening dress by Valentino with the word Peace spelled out in silver sequins in 14 languages. Signaling the hemline uncertainty that was to come later, the most prominent evening style in most couture collections showed the knees in front and dipped to the floor in back.
By the time of the fall showings, the mood had lifted. Oscar de la Renta made his debut, the first American to show with the rest of the French ready-to-wear collections. He established a toehold for American fashions abroad, and at the same time laid the groundwork for the importance of plaids for winter.
The jacket was firmly established as the key wardrobe component, worn with short skirts, dresses, pants or tights. For evening, the short slip dress was a major fashion, done notably by Calvin Klein.
As the fashion pace accelerated, some designer reputations soared. Gianni Versace's extravagant clothes, from wildly mixed prints to cropped versions of 18th-century ball gowns, pushed him into the forefront of fashion. On the exuberant side of the ledger, Karl Lagerfeld for Chanel and Christian Lacroix continued to dazzle. Geoffrey Beene's amazingly creative designs, Izaac Mizrahi's exuberant ones and Ralph Lauren's serenely elegant classics all won critical acclaim.
One of the oddest trends was the popularity of underwear as outerwear, expressed in dresses with underwire-bra tops and black-lace-over-beige-net fabrics that looked more appropriate for the boudoir than the ballroom. This was balanced by the man-tailored trend involving pants suits, shirts and even men's ties, best expressed in collections by Mr. Lagerfeld in Europe and Mr. Lauren in New York.
While these represent extremes, there was no paucity of invention in most fashion collections. There was a more-than-usual array of choices in each designer's work. The big problem was not design but loosening the customer's purse strings.

SECTION: Section C; Page 1; Column 3; Science Desk
